Life, Power, Will, and Knowledge Necessitated by Dependence
Sanusi derives life, power, will, knowledge from universal dependence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why universal dependence on God necessitates that He possesses life, power, will, and knowledge.

Core Idea
God's attributes are necessary for creation.

Sanusi begins by drawing out the first set of implications from the foundational truth that everything other than God depends on Him. If God lacked any of these four attributes, He could not create anything, which would contradict universal dependence. This passage establishes the core attributes required for creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how Sanusi states the premise and then makes a conditional argument.
2
Identify the four attributes
List the four attributes mentioned: life, power, will, knowledge. Note that 'power' is described as universal (ʿumūm al-qudra).
3
Understand the conditional logic
The argument says: if any of these attributes were absent, God could not create anything; therefore nothing would depend on Him. But everything does depend on Him, so He must have these attributes.
This is a modus tollens argument in disguise.
4
Reflect on the rhetorical question
The passage ends with a rhetorical question emphasizing that God is the One upon whom everything depends. This reinforces the conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا افْتِقَارُ كُلِّ مَا سِوَاهُ إِلَيْهِ : فَهُوَ يُوجِبُ لَهُ تَعَالَى الْحَيَاةَ ، وَعُمُومَ الْقُدْرَةِ ، وَالإِرَادَةِ ، وَالْعِلْم ؛ إذْ لَوِ انْتَفَى شَيْءٌ مِنْ هَذِهِ لَمَا أَمْكَنَ أَنْ يُوجِدَ تَعَالَى شَيْئاً مِنَ الْحَوَادِثِ ، فَلا يَفْتَقِرُ إِلَيْهِ جَلَّ وَعَزَّ شَيْءٌ ، كَيْفَ وَهُوَ تَعَالَى الَّذِي يَفْتَقِرُ إِلَيْهِ كُلُّ مَا سِوَاهُ ؟! وَيُوجِبُ لَهُ تَعَالَى الْوَحْدَانِيَّة (۱) ؛ إذْ لَوْ كَانَ مَعَهُ تَعَالَى ثَانٍ فِي أُلُوهِيَّتِهِ (٢) لَمَا أَفْتَقَرَ إِلَيْهِ شَيْءٌ ؛ لِلْزُومِ عَجْزِهِمَا حينئذ ، كَيْفَ وَهُوَ جَلَّ وَعَلا الَّذِي يَفْتَفِرُ إِلَيْهِ كُلُّ مَا سِوَاهُ ؟! وَيُؤْخَذُ مِنْهُ أَيْضاً : حُدُوتُ الْعَالَم بِأَسْرِهِ (۳) ؛ إذْ لَوْ كَانَ شَيْءٌ مِنْهُ قَدِيماً لَكَانَ ذَلِكَ الشَّيْءُ مُسْتَغْنِياً عَنْهُ تَعَالَى ، كَيْفَ وَهُوَ جَلَّ وَعَلا الَّذِي يَجِبُ أَنْ يَفْتَقِرَ إِلَيْهِ كُلُّ مَا سِوَاهُ ؟! وَيُؤْخَذُ مِنْهُ أَيْضاً : أَنْ لا تَأْثِيرَ لِشَيْءٍ مِنَ الْكَائِنَاتِ فِي أَثَرِ مَا ، وَإِلا لَزِمَ أَنْ يَسْتَغْنِيَ ذَلِكَ الأَثَرُ عَنْ مَوْلانَا جَلَّ وَعَزَّ ، كَيْفَ وَهُوَ تَعَالَى الَّذِي يَفْتَقِرُ إِلَيْهِ كُلُّ مَا سِوَاهُ عُمُوماً وَعَلَى كُلِّ حَالٍ ؟! هَذَا إِنْ قَدَّرْتَ أَنَّ شَيْئاً مِنَ الْكَائِنَاتِ يُؤَثْرُ بِطَبْعِهِ وَأَمَّا إِنْ قَدَّرْتَهُ مُؤَثّراً بقُوَّةٍ جَعَلَهَا اللهُ تَعَالَى فِيهِ كَمَا يَزْعُمُهُ كَثِيرٌ مِنَ الْجَهَلَةِ .. فَذَلِكَ مُحَالٌ أَيضاً ؛ لِأَنَّهُ يَصِيرُ حِينَئِذٍ مَوْلانَا جَلَّ وَعَزَّ مُفْتَقِراً فِي إِيجَادِ بَعْضِ الْأَفْعَالِ إِلَى وَاسِطَةٍ ، وَذَلِكَ بَاطِلٌ ؛ لِمَا عَرَفْتَ قَبْلُ مِنْ وُجُوبِ اسْتِغْنَائِهِ جَلَّ وَعَزَّ عَنْ كُلِّ مَا سِوَاهُ
And as for the dependence of everything other than Him upon Him: it necessitates for Him, Exalted, life, universality of power, will, and knowledge; for if any of these were absent, He, Exalted, would not be able to bring into existence any of the contingent things, so nothing would depend on Him, Majestic and Mighty; and how is He, Exalted, the One upon whom everything other than Him depends?! And it necessitates for Him, Exalted, oneness (1); for if there were with Him, Exalted, a second in His divinity (2), nothing would depend on Him, because of the necessary mutual incapacity then; and how is He, Majestic and Exalted, the One upon whom everything other than Him depends?! And from it is also taken: the temporal origination of the entire world (3); for if anything of it were eternal, that thing would be independent of Him, Exalted; and how is He, Majestic and Exalted, the One upon whom everything other than Him must depend?! And from it is also taken: that there is no effect for any of the created beings in anything whatsoever; otherwise that effect would necessarily be independent of our Master, Majestic and Mighty; and how is He, Exalted, the One upon whom everything other than Him depends universally and in every case?! This is if you suppose that something among the created beings has effect by its nature. But if you suppose it to be effective by a power that God, Exalted, placed in it, as many of the ignorant claim, that is also impossible; because then our Master, Majestic and Mighty, would become dependent in producing some actions upon an intermediary, and that is false, due to what you have already known of the necessity of His, Majestic and Mighty, independence from everything other than Him.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the universal dependence of all things on God (a premise established earlier) necessitates several attributes and truths. The argument moves by considering what would be missing if any of these were not the case. First, it necessitates life, power, will, and knowledge for God, because if He lacked any of these, He could not create anything, and then nothing would depend on Him—contradicting the premise. Second, it necessitates oneness (tawḥīd), because if there were a second god, neither would be able to act independently due to mutual weakness, so nothing would depend on either, again contradicting universal dependence. Third, from it is derived the temporal origination (ḥudūth) of the entire world, because if anything were eternal, it would be independent of God. Fourth, it implies that no creature has any effect whatsoever, because if it did, that effect would be independent of God. The passage then refutes an objection: even if one claims that a creature acts by a power God gave it, that would make God need a medium for some acts, which violates His absolute self-sufficiency. Thus all these points are shown to be necessary consequences of the foundational truth that everything other than God depends on Him.

Key Terms
افْتِقَار (dependence)
الْحَيَاة (life)
عُمُوم الْقُدْرَة (universality of power)
الإِرَادَة (will)
الْعِلْم (knowledge)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What would happen if God lacked one of the attributes mentioned in the passage?
A) He would still be able to create through other means.
B) He would not be able to create any contingent thing.
C) The universe would still exist independently.
D) Nothing would change.

Key Takeaway
Universal dependence requires that God be living, powerful, willing, and knowing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If everything you do and every breath you take depends entirely on God's power, will, and knowledge, why do you still act as if your own plans are independent of Him? What would change in your daily routine if you truly internalized that nothing happens without His will?
?
When you face a situation that seems out of your control, do you resent God for not intervening, or do you remember that His power is universal and His will is always perfectly wise? How would your response today be different if you genuinely trusted that His attributes necessitate complete control over all things?
?
You say you believe that God is alive, powerful, willful, and knowing. But do your prayers reflect that belief? Do you ask Him with the certainty that He can and will act, or do you pray out of habit without expecting change? Commit to one concrete action today that shows you truly depend on His attributes.

Oneness of God Derived from Universal Dependence
Sanusi derives God's oneness from universal dependence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the universal dependence of all things on God necessitates His oneness.

Core Idea
Two gods would mean mutual weakness and no dependence.

Sanusi now extends the argument from universal dependence to prove God's oneness. The logic is that if there were a second god, both would be incapable of independent action, so nothing would depend on either. But everything depends on God, so He must be one.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the use of the term 'al-waḥdāniyya' (oneness) and the conditional argument.
2
Understand the mutual incapacity argument
The argument says: if there were a second god, both would be unable to act independently due to mutual weakness, so nothing would depend on either. This contradicts universal dependence.
This is a classic kalam argument for tawḥīd.
3
Reflect on the rhetorical question
The passage again ends with a rhetorical question emphasizing that God is the One upon whom everything depends.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا افْتِقَارُ كُلِّ مَا سِوَاهُ إِلَيْهِ : فَهُوَ يُوجِبُ لَهُ تَعَالَى الْحَيَاةَ ، وَعُمُومَ الْقُدْرَةِ ، وَالإِرَادَةِ ، وَالْعِلْم ؛ إذْ لَوِ انْتَفَى شَيْءٌ مِنْ هَذِهِ لَمَا أَمْكَنَ أَنْ يُوجِدَ تَعَالَى شَيْئاً مِنَ الْحَوَادِثِ ، فَلا يَفْتَقِرُ إِلَيْهِ جَلَّ وَعَزَّ شَيْءٌ ، كَيْفَ وَهُوَ تَعَالَى الَّذِي يَفْتَقِرُ إِلَيْهِ كُلُّ مَا سِوَاهُ ؟! وَيُوجِبُ لَهُ تَعَالَى الْوَحْدَانِيَّة (۱) ؛ إذْ لَوْ كَانَ مَعَهُ تَعَالَى ثَانٍ فِي أُلُوهِيَّتِهِ (٢) لَمَا أَفْتَقَرَ إِلَيْهِ شَيْءٌ ؛ لِلْزُومِ عَجْزِهِمَا حينئذ ، كَيْفَ وَهُوَ جَلَّ وَعَلا الَّذِي يَفْتَفِرُ إِلَيْهِ كُلُّ مَا سِوَاهُ ؟! وَيُؤْخَذُ مِنْهُ أَيْضاً : حُدُوتُ الْعَالَم بِأَسْرِهِ (۳) ؛ إذْ لَوْ كَانَ شَيْءٌ مِنْهُ قَدِيماً لَكَانَ ذَلِكَ الشَّيْءُ مُسْتَغْنِياً عَنْهُ تَعَالَى ، كَيْفَ وَهُوَ جَلَّ وَعَلا الَّذِي يَجِبُ أَنْ يَفْتَقِرَ إِلَيْهِ كُلُّ مَا سِوَاهُ ؟! وَيُؤْخَذُ مِنْهُ أَيْضاً : أَنْ لا تَأْثِيرَ لِشَيْءٍ مِنَ الْكَائِنَاتِ فِي أَثَرِ مَا ، وَإِلا لَزِمَ أَنْ يَسْتَغْنِيَ ذَلِكَ الأَثَرُ عَنْ مَوْلانَا جَلَّ وَعَزَّ ، كَيْفَ وَهُوَ تَعَالَى الَّذِي يَفْتَقِرُ إِلَيْهِ كُلُّ مَا سِوَاهُ عُمُوماً وَعَلَى كُلِّ حَالٍ ؟! هَذَا إِنْ قَدَّرْتَ أَنَّ شَيْئاً مِنَ الْكَائِنَاتِ يُؤَثْرُ بِطَبْعِهِ وَأَمَّا إِنْ قَدَّرْتَهُ مُؤَثّراً بقُوَّةٍ جَعَلَهَا اللهُ تَعَالَى فِيهِ كَمَا يَزْعُمُهُ كَثِيرٌ مِنَ الْجَهَلَةِ .. فَذَلِكَ مُحَالٌ أَيضاً ؛ لِأَنَّهُ يَصِيرُ حِينَئِذٍ مَوْلانَا جَلَّ وَعَزَّ مُفْتَقِراً فِي إِيجَادِ بَعْضِ الْأَفْعَالِ إِلَى وَاسِطَةٍ ، وَذَلِكَ بَاطِلٌ ؛ لِمَا عَرَفْتَ قَبْلُ مِنْ وُجُوبِ اسْتِغْنَائِهِ جَلَّ وَعَزَّ عَنْ كُلِّ مَا سِوَاهُ
And as for the dependence of everything other than Him upon Him: it necessitates for Him, Exalted, life, universality of power, will, and knowledge; for if any of these were absent, He, Exalted, would not be able to bring into existence any of the contingent things, so nothing would depend on Him, Majestic and Mighty; and how is He, Exalted, the One upon whom everything other than Him depends?! And it necessitates for Him, Exalted, oneness (1); for if there were with Him, Exalted, a second in His divinity (2), nothing would depend on Him, because of the necessary mutual incapacity then; and how is He, Majestic and Exalted, the One upon whom everything other than Him depends?! And from it is also taken: the temporal origination of the entire world (3); for if anything of it were eternal, that thing would be independent of Him, Exalted; and how is He, Majestic and Exalted, the One upon whom everything other than Him must depend?! And from it is also taken: that there is no effect for any of the created beings in anything whatsoever; otherwise that effect would necessarily be independent of our Master, Majestic and Mighty; and how is He, Exalted, the One upon whom everything other than Him depends universally and in every case?! This is if you suppose that something among the created beings has effect by its nature. But if you suppose it to be effective by a power that God, Exalted, placed in it, as many of the ignorant claim, that is also impossible; because then our Master, Majestic and Mighty, would become dependent in producing some actions upon an intermediary, and that is false, due to what you have already known of the necessity of His, Majestic and Mighty, independence from everything other than Him.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the universal dependence of all things on God (a premise established earlier) necessitates several attributes and truths. The argument moves by considering what would be missing if any of these were not the case. First, it necessitates life, power, will, and knowledge for God, because if He lacked any of these, He could not create anything, and then nothing would depend on Him—contradicting the premise. Second, it necessitates oneness (tawḥīd), because if there were a second god, neither would be able to act independently due to mutual weakness, so nothing would depend on either, again contradicting universal dependence. Third, from it is derived the temporal origination (ḥudūth) of the entire world, because if anything were eternal, it would be independent of God. Fourth, it implies that no creature has any effect whatsoever, because if it did, that effect would be independent of God. The passage then refutes an objection: even if one claims that a creature acts by a power God gave it, that would make God need a medium for some acts, which violates His absolute self-sufficiency. Thus all these points are shown to be necessary consequences of the foundational truth that everything other than God depends on Him.

Key Terms
الْوَحْدَانِيَّة (oneness)
افْتِقَار (dependence)
أُلُوهِيَّة (divinity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why would nothing depend on God if there were a second god?
A) Because the second god would be weaker.
B) Because both gods would be incapable due to mutual weakness.
C) Because the second god would oppose God.
D) Because there would be two creators.

Key Takeaway
Universal dependence on God implies that He must be one, for two gods would negate dependence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If everything in your life depends on God alone, why do you sometimes act as if there are other sources of security or power that you can rely on instead of Him?
?
The argument shows that two gods would be mutually weak. In what areas of your life are you trying to depend on something alongside God, and how does that actually weaken your reliance on Him alone?
?
How does knowing that God's oneness is proven rationally—not just taught—change the foundation of your faith from blind imitation to personal conviction? What would you risk to defend this truth?

Temporal Origination of the World from Dependence
Sanusi derives temporal origination of the world from universal dependence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the universal dependence of all things on God implies that the world is temporally originated (not eternal).

Core Idea
If anything were eternal, it would be independent of God.

Sanusi now derives the temporal origination (ḥudūth) of the entire world from the premise of universal dependence. If any part of the world were eternal, it would be self-sufficient and not need God, contradicting universal dependence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the term 'ḥudūth al-ʿālam' (temporal origination of the world).
2
Understand the conditional argument
The argument: if anything in the world were eternal (qadīm), it would be independent of God. But everything depends on God, so nothing is eternal.
This is a simple contrapositive.
3
Reflect on the inclusive phrase 'bi-asrihi'
The phrase 'the entire world' emphasizes that no part can be eternal, not just the whole.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا افْتِقَارُ كُلِّ مَا سِوَاهُ إِلَيْهِ : فَهُوَ يُوجِبُ لَهُ تَعَالَى الْحَيَاةَ ، وَعُمُومَ الْقُدْرَةِ ، وَالإِرَادَةِ ، وَالْعِلْم ؛ إذْ لَوِ انْتَفَى شَيْءٌ مِنْ هَذِهِ لَمَا أَمْكَنَ أَنْ يُوجِدَ تَعَالَى شَيْئاً مِنَ الْحَوَادِثِ ، فَلا يَفْتَقِرُ إِلَيْهِ جَلَّ وَعَزَّ شَيْءٌ ، كَيْفَ وَهُوَ تَعَالَى الَّذِي يَفْتَقِرُ إِلَيْهِ كُلُّ مَا سِوَاهُ ؟! وَيُوجِبُ لَهُ تَعَالَى الْوَحْدَانِيَّة (۱) ؛ إذْ لَوْ كَانَ مَعَهُ تَعَالَى ثَانٍ فِي أُلُوهِيَّتِهِ (٢) لَمَا أَفْتَقَرَ إِلَيْهِ شَيْءٌ ؛ لِلْزُومِ عَجْزِهِمَا حينئذ ، كَيْفَ وَهُوَ جَلَّ وَعَلا الَّذِي يَفْتَفِرُ إِلَيْهِ كُلُّ مَا سِوَاهُ ؟! وَيُؤْخَذُ مِنْهُ أَيْضاً : حُدُوتُ الْعَالَم بِأَسْرِهِ (۳) ؛ إذْ لَوْ كَانَ شَيْءٌ مِنْهُ قَدِيماً لَكَانَ ذَلِكَ الشَّيْءُ مُسْتَغْنِياً عَنْهُ تَعَالَى ، كَيْفَ وَهُوَ جَلَّ وَعَلا الَّذِي يَجِبُ أَنْ يَفْتَقِرَ إِلَيْهِ كُلُّ مَا سِوَاهُ ؟! وَيُؤْخَذُ مِنْهُ أَيْضاً : أَنْ لا تَأْثِيرَ لِشَيْءٍ مِنَ الْكَائِنَاتِ فِي أَثَرِ مَا ، وَإِلا لَزِمَ أَنْ يَسْتَغْنِيَ ذَلِكَ الأَثَرُ عَنْ مَوْلانَا جَلَّ وَعَزَّ ، كَيْفَ وَهُوَ تَعَالَى الَّذِي يَفْتَقِرُ إِلَيْهِ كُلُّ مَا سِوَاهُ عُمُوماً وَعَلَى كُلِّ حَالٍ ؟! هَذَا إِنْ قَدَّرْتَ أَنَّ شَيْئاً مِنَ الْكَائِنَاتِ يُؤَثْرُ بِطَبْعِهِ وَأَمَّا إِنْ قَدَّرْتَهُ مُؤَثّراً بقُوَّةٍ جَعَلَهَا اللهُ تَعَالَى فِيهِ كَمَا يَزْعُمُهُ كَثِيرٌ مِنَ الْجَهَلَةِ .. فَذَلِكَ مُحَالٌ أَيضاً ؛ لِأَنَّهُ يَصِيرُ حِينَئِذٍ مَوْلانَا جَلَّ وَعَزَّ مُفْتَقِراً فِي إِيجَادِ بَعْضِ الْأَفْعَالِ إِلَى وَاسِطَةٍ ، وَذَلِكَ بَاطِلٌ ؛ لِمَا عَرَفْتَ قَبْلُ مِنْ وُجُوبِ اسْتِغْنَائِهِ جَلَّ وَعَزَّ عَنْ كُلِّ مَا سِوَاهُ
And as for the dependence of everything other than Him upon Him: it necessitates for Him, Exalted, life, universality of power, will, and knowledge; for if any of these were absent, He, Exalted, would not be able to bring into existence any of the contingent things, so nothing would depend on Him, Majestic and Mighty; and how is He, Exalted, the One upon whom everything other than Him depends?! And it necessitates for Him, Exalted, oneness (1); for if there were with Him, Exalted, a second in His divinity (2), nothing would depend on Him, because of the necessary mutual incapacity then; and how is He, Majestic and Exalted, the One upon whom everything other than Him depends?! And from it is also taken: that there is no effect for any of the created beings in anything whatsoever; otherwise that effect would necessarily be independent of our Master, Majestic and Mighty; and how is He, Exalted, the One upon whom everything other than Him depends universally and in every case?! A) It would depend on God.
B) It would be independent of God.

The world must have a beginning because only God is eternal and self-sufficient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If everything other than God is dependent on Him, is there any area of your life where you subconsciously act as if you are self-sufficient? What would it look like to genuinely acknowledge your absolute need for Him in that area today?
?
The passage argues that an eternal thing would be independent of God. Do you ever treat created things—like your health, wealth, or relationships—as if they are eternal or self-sustaining? How does recognizing their dependency change your attachment to them?
?
Sanusi uses a rhetorical question to emphasize that only God deserves dependence. Are there people or systems you rely on more than God? What is one concrete step you can take this week to redirect that dependency back to Him alone?

Divine Attributes
intermediate

No Creaturely Effect and Refutation of Mediated Power
Sanusi denies any creaturely effect and refutes the idea of God-given causal power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why creatures cannot have any causal effect independent of God, and why even a power placed in them by God is impossible.

Core Idea
True independent effect would make the effect independent of God.

Sanusi derives the final implication: no creature has any effect on anything. He then addresses an objection: what if a creature acts by a power God gave it? He shows that even this is impossible because it would make God dependent on a medium, contradicting His absolute self-sufficiency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the two scenarios: effect by nature and effect by God-given power.
2
Understand the argument against natural effect
If a creature had effect by its nature, the effect would be independent of God. This violates universal dependence.
3
Understand the argument against mediated power
If a creature effects by a power God placed in it, then God would need that creature as a medium for some acts, making Him dependent on it. That contradicts His necessary self-sufficiency.
This refutes claims like those of some ignorant people who attribute causal power to creatures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا افْتِقَارُ كُلِّ مَا سِوَاهُ إِلَيْهِ : فَهُوَ يُوجِبُ لَهُ تَعَالَى الْحَيَاةَ ، وَعُمُومَ الْقُدْرَةِ ، وَالإِرَادَةِ ، وَالْعِلْم ؛ إذْ لَوِ انْتَفَى شَيْءٌ مِنْ هَذِهِ لَمَا أَمْكَنَ أَنْ يُوجِدَ تَعَالَى شَيْئاً مِنَ الْحَوَادِثِ ، فَلا يَفْتَقِرُ إِلَيْهِ جَلَّ وَعَزَّ شَيْءٌ ، كَيْفَ وَهُوَ تَعَالَى الَّذِي يَفْتَقِرُ إِلَيْهِ كُلُّ مَا سِوَاهُ ؟! وَيُوجِبُ لَهُ تَعَالَى الْوَحْدَانِيَّة (۱) ؛ إذْ لَوْ كَانَ مَعَهُ تَعَالَى ثَانٍ فِي أُلُوهِيَّتِهِ (٢) لَمَا أَفْتَقَرَ إِلَيْهِ شَيْءٌ ؛ لِلْزُومِ عَجْزِهِمَا حينئذ ، كَيْفَ وَهُوَ جَلَّ وَعَلا الَّذِي يَفْتَفِرُ إِلَيْهِ كُلُّ مَا سِوَاهُ ؟! وَيُؤْخَذُ مِنْهُ أَيْضاً : حُدُوتُ الْعَالَم بِأَسْرِهِ (۳) ؛ إذْ لَوْ كَانَ شَيْءٌ مِنْهُ قَدِيماً لَكَانَ ذَلِكَ الشَّيْءُ مُسْتَغْنِياً عَنْهُ تَعَالَى ، كَيْفَ وَهُوَ جَلَّ وَعَلا الَّذِي يَجِبُ أَنْ يَفْتَقِرَ إِلَيْهِ كُلُّ مَا سِوَاهُ ؟! وَيُؤْخَذُ مِنْهُ أَيْضاً : أَنْ لا تَأْثِيرَ لِشَيْءٍ مِنَ الْكَائِنَاتِ فِي أَثَرِ مَا ، وَإِلا لَزِمَ أَنْ يَسْتَغْنِيَ ذَلِكَ الأَثَرُ عَنْ مَوْلانَا جَلَّ وَعَزَّ ، كَيْفَ وَهُوَ تَعَالَى الَّذِي يَفْتَقِرُ إِلَيْهِ كُلُّ مَا سِوَاهُ عُمُوماً وَعَلَى كُلِّ حَالٍ ؟! هَذَا إِنْ قَدَّرْتَ أَنَّ شَيْئاً مِنَ الْكَائِنَاتِ يُؤَثْرُ بِطَبْعِهِ وَأَمَّا إِنْ قَدَّرْتَهُ مُؤَثّراً بقُوَّةٍ جَعَلَهَا اللهُ تَعَالَى فِيهِ كَمَا يَزْعُمُهُ كَثِيرٌ مِنَ الْجَهَلَةِ .. فَذَلِكَ مُحَالٌ أَيضاً ؛ لِأَنَّهُ يَصِيرُ حِينَئِذٍ مَوْلانَا جَلَّ وَعَزَّ مُفْتَقِراً فِي إِيجَادِ بَعْضِ الْأَفْعَالِ إِلَى وَاسِطَةٍ ، وَذَلِكَ بَاطِلٌ ؛ لِمَا عَرَفْتَ قَبْلُ مِنْ وُجُوبِ اسْتِغْنَائِهِ جَلَّ وَعَزَّ عَنْ كُلِّ مَا سِوَاهُ
And as for the dependence of everything other than Him upon Him: it necessitates for Him, Exalted, life, universality of power, will, and knowledge; for if any of these were absent, He, Exalted, would not be able to bring into existence any of the contingent things, so nothing would depend on Him, Majestic and Mighty; and how is He, Exalted, the One upon whom everything other than Him depends?! Commentary
This passage is a proof that the universal dependence of all things on God (a premise established earlier) necessitates several attributes and truths. The argument moves by considering what would be missing if any of these were not the case. First, it necessitates life, power, will, and knowledge for God, because if He lacked any of these, He could not create anything, and then nothing would depend on Him—contradicting the premise. Key Terms
لا تَأْثِير (no effect)
الْكَائِنَات (created beings)
قُوَّة (power)
الْغَنِي (the Self-Sufficient)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think creatures can be true causes of events, either by their own nature or by a power God placed in them.
Correction: Both are impossible: natural effect makes the effect independent of God; God-given power makes God dependent on the creature.

Checkpoint
Why is it impossible for a creature to effect by a power God placed in it?
A) Because the power would be insufficient.
B) Because God would then depend on an intermediary.
C) Because the creature would become a partner to God.
D) Because the power would be temporary.

Key Takeaway
No creature has any true causal effect; all effects are directly from God, who is absolutely self-sufficient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Sanusi argues that if any creature had effect by its own nature, that effect would become independent of God. Is there any person, possession, or status you secretly treat as if it has power over your life independent of God? How would your heart change if you truly stripped it of that imagined power?
?
When you pray for something and it happens, do you attribute the outcome to the means (your effort, a doctor, a friend) or to God alone? What would it look like to live each moment with the conviction that no creature has any effect—only God?
?
The passage says that even if God gave a creature power to cause effects, that would make God dependent on that creature for that action. Are there ways you unconsciously assume God needs your help or cooperation to bring about His will? What does this lesson demand you repent of in your view of God?

Divine Attributes
beginner
4 min
The Twenty Attributes Are Not Exhaustive
Sanusi states the twenty necessary attributes but clarifies they are not exhaustive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the twenty necessary attributes of Allah are not an exhaustive list, and why we are not obligated to know attributes beyond what proof has been established.

Core Idea
Allah's perfections are infinite; only twenty are known to us through proof.

Sanusi opens the discussion of divine attributes by stating there are twenty necessary attributes, but immediately clarifies that this number is not a limit. He uses the partitive 'min' to signal that these twenty are only a sample. This lesson explores why the list is not exhaustive and how we are only accountable for what we have evidence for.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the key terms and the flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the word 'min' and how it changes the meaning.
2
Understand the significance of 'min'
The partitive 'min' indicates that the twenty attributes are only a portion of the necessary attributes, not all of them. Reflect on why Sanusi would include this nuance.
3
Grasp the accountability principle
Sanusi explains that Allah's perfections have no end, but humans are not blamed for ignorance of what no rational or transmitted proof establishes. Consider how this balances divine transcendence with human responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَمِمَّا يَجِبُ لِمَوْلانَا جَلَّ وَعَزَّ : عِشْرُونَ صِفَةٌ. أشارَ بـ (مِنْ) التبعيضيةِ إلى أنَّ صفاتِ مولانا جل وعزَّ الواجبة لهُ لا تنحصر في هذه العشرينَ؛ إذْ كمالاتُهُ تعالى لا نهاية لها، لكنَّ العجز عن معرفة ما لم يُنصَبْ عليهِ دليل عقلي ولا نقلى لا نُؤاخَذُ بِهِ بفضل الله تعالى. (١) عبارة بعضهم: وهي ـ أي: العشرون صفة ـ ما انتهى إليه إدراك القوى البشرية، وإلا فصفات كمالاته ونعوت جلاله مما يفوت العدَّ، ولا يحيط به الحد، لكنَّا لسنا مكلفين بما لم ينصب عليه دليلاً يوصلنا إليه. «ياسين» (ق ١٠٧)، وكذا سيذكر المصنف (ص ١٦٨) مثل هذا عند حديثه عن الكلام، وانظر تقسيم هذه الصفات في «شرح المقدمات» (ص ٢٣٥).
Among what is necessary for our Lord, may He be glorified and exalted, are twenty attributes. He indicated by the partitive 'min' that the attributes of our Lord, glorified and exalted, that are necessary for Him are not confined to these twenty, because His perfections, exalted is He, have no end, but inability to know what no rational or transmitted proof has been established for is not held against us, by the grace of Allah, may He be exalted. (1) Statement of some: They — i.e., the twenty attributes — are what the perception of human faculties has reached; otherwise, the attributes of His perfections and the descriptions of His majesty cannot be counted and cannot be encompassed by limits, but we are not obligated to know what no proof has been established to lead us to it. 'Yasin' (q 107), and similarly the author will mention (p. 168) such when discussing al-kalam, and see the division of these attributes in 'Sharh al-Muqaddimat' (p. 235).
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage introduces the topic of Allah's necessary attributes. The author, likely Sanusi, begins by stating that among the attributes necessary for Allah are twenty specific ones. However, he immediately qualifies this by noting that the word 'min' (from) in his statement 'among what is necessary' indicates partiality — meaning these twenty are only a subset. The reason is that Allah's perfections are infinite; they have no end. Therefore, to think that only these twenty exist would be a mistake. The author then addresses a potential concern: if there are infinitely many attributes, how can we be accountable for knowing them? He answers that we are not held responsible for knowing what no rational or transmitted proof has been established for. In other words, we are only obligated to know what God has revealed evidence for. The footnote adds a clarifying statement from another scholar: these twenty are the limit of human perception; beyond that, the attributes of majesty cannot be enumerated. This underscores that the list is not exhaustive but is what we can know through reason and revelation.

Key Terms
صفات واجبة
كمالات لا نهاية لها
دليل عقلي
دليل نقلي
تبعضية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the twenty necessary attributes are all of Allah's attributes.
Correction: The text explicitly says they are not confined to these twenty because His perfections are infinite.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why are the twenty attributes not all of Allah's attributes?
A) Because the twenty are only for this world
B) Because only prophets know all attributes
C) Because Allah's perfections have no end
D) Because the twenty are only those mentioned in the Quran

Key Takeaway
The twenty necessary attributes are not exhaustive; Allah's perfections are infinite, and we are only responsible for what has been established by proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that Allah's perfections are infinite, yet you only know twenty attributes. Does your heart feel contentment in this limitation, or does it yearn to know more? What specific step will you take this week to seek knowledge of His attributes that has been established by proof?
?
Are you satisfied with knowing only what you are obligated to know, or do you actively pursue a deeper understanding of Allah beyond the minimum? How does your current pursuit reflect your love for Him?
?
The text says you are not held accountable for what no proof has been established. Do you use this as an excuse to avoid learning about Allah, or do you seek the proofs for the attributes you have been given? What is one attribute you will contemplate deeply today to increase your awe of Him?

The Meaning of Existence as a Self-Subsistent Attribute
Definition of existence as a self-subsistent attribute and its self-evident nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition and self-evident nature of the attribute of existence as a self-subsistent attribute in theological discourse.

Core Idea
Existence is a self-evident attribute that affirms the reality of the essence.

This lesson introduces the attribute of existence (al-wujud) and explains why it is considered a self-subsistent attribute (sifa nafsaniyya). We will see the definition given by Imam al-Haramayn and the initial statement that it is self-evident. This sets the stage for the nuanced discussion on whether existence is identical to or additional to the essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get a feel for the terms. Then read the translation.
Note the use of parentheses for footnote numbers.
2
Identify the meaning of 'self-subsistent attribute'
Find the definition of sifa nafsaniyya given by Imam al-Haramayn and understand its components.
The definition: an attribute of affirmation for a self, necessary as long as the self remains, not caused by causes.
3
Reflect on why existence is self-evident
Consider why the commentator says existence is self-evident in conception and what that implies for theological reasoning.
Self-evident means it does not need proof; it is directly known.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَهِيَ الْوُجُودُ . الصفة النفسية معناه ظاهر (١) ، وفي عدّ الوجودِ صفةً على مذهب الشيخ الأشعري تسامح (٢) ؛ لأنَّهُ عندَهُ عينُ الذاتِ ، ليسَ بزائدِ عليها ، (١) لأنه بديهي التصور ، ومعناه : التحقق والثبوت في الخارج ، وهو صفة نفسية ، ومعنى الصفة النفسية كما قال إمام الحرمين في « الإرشاد » ( ص ٣٠ ) : ( كل صفةِ إثباتٍ لنفس ، لازمةٍ ما بقيت النفس ، غير معللة بعلل قائمة بالموصوف ) ، ولما عجزت العقول عن تصور ذاته تعالى عجزاً لا مطمع لرفعه .. بقي لها من إدراك صفات النفس له سبحانه صفة الوجود ، مع إقرارها أن وجوده تعالى ليس كوجودنا ، وفي كلام إمام الحرمين في « أجوبته لأسئلة الإمام الصقلي » ( ص ٥١ ) ما يوحي بأن عدم التحيز لذاته تعالى من صفاته النفسية ، ولكن قال بعدها ( ص ٥٥ ) : ( الجهل بالصفة النفسية إن صح رجوع صفة النفس إلى غير الوجود . . . ) ، فالتحقيق أن عدم التحيز راجع للصفات السلبية عند التفصيل ، ولكن نبَّه العلامة السكتاني في ( حاشيته على شرح العقيدة الصغرى » ( ق ٤٣ ( على أنه قيل : إن السلوب تسمى حالاً نفسية أو صفة نفسية (٢) وهو مذهب إمام الحرمين أيضاً ، قال في الإرشاد » ( ص ٣١ ) : ( والوجه « المرضي : ألا يعد الوجود من الصفات ؛ فإن الوجود نفس الذات ) ، ثم قال : ) والأئمة رضي الله عنهم متوسعون في عد الوجود من الصفات ، والعلم به علم بالذات ) . بصفة ، لكنْ لمَّا كانَ الوجودُ تُوصَفُ بِهِ الذاتُ في اللفظ ، فيُقالُ : ذات مولانا جل وعزَّ موجودةٌ . . صح أنْ يُعَدَّ صفةً على الجملة (١) . وأما على مذهبِ مَنْ يجعلُ الوجود زائداً على الذاتِ ؛ كالإمام الرازي .. فعدُّهُ مِنَ الصفات صحيح لا تسامح فيه ومنهم مَنْ يجعله زائداً على الذاتِ في الحادثِ دونَ القديم ؛ وهو مذهب الفلاسفة (٣) (١) بيان لوجه التسامح ، وتوطئة لذكر خلاف الإمام الرازي (٢) حيث قال في معالم أصول الدين ) ( ص ٣٢ ) وهو من أواخر كتبه : ( الوجود زائد على الماهيات ) ، وهي مسألة اضطرب فيها الإمام الرازي ، فقوله في المحصل » ( ص ٤٣ ) مخالف لهذا ، وقال في ( الإشارة » ( ص ٧٥ ) وهو من أوائل كتبه : ( لا يجوز أن يكون وجود البارئ تعالى غير حقيقته ) (٣) انظر الردَّ عليهم في هذه المسألة ( محصل أفكار المتقدمين والمتأخرين » ( ص ٤٣ ) . وقد تحصل في الوجود ثلاثة أقوال : الأول : أنه عين الموجود في القديم والحادث الثاني : أنه أمر اعتباري زائد على الموجود في القديم والحادث الثالث : أنه عين الموجود في القديم ، زائد في الحادث
And it is existence. The self-subsistent attribute. Its meaning is apparent (1), and regarding counting existence as an attribute in the school of Shaykh al-Ash'ari, it is a tolerance (2), because according to him it is identical to the essence, not additional to it. (1) Because it is self-evident in conception, and its meaning is: realization and affirmation in the external, and it is a self-subsistent attribute. And the meaning of the self-subsistent attribute as stated by Imam al-Haramayn in 'al-Irshad' (p. 30): 'Every attribute of affirmation for a self, necessary as long as the self remains, not caused by causes subsisting in the described.' And when minds fell short of conceiving His essence, exalted, in a hopeless incapacity, there remained for them from the perception of the self-subsistent attributes of Him, glorified, the attribute of existence, along with their acknowledgment that His existence, exalted, is not like our existence. And in the words of Imam al-Haramayn in his 'Answers to the Questions of Imam al-Siqilli' (p. 51) there is a suggestion that non-delimitedness of His essence, exalted, is among His self-subsistent attributes, but he said after that (p. 55): 'Ignorance concerning the self-subsistent attribute if it is correct that the self-subsistent attribute refers to other than existence...', so the verified view is that non-delimitedness reverts to the negative attributes in detail. But the scholar al-Saktani alerted in his 'Gloss on the Commentary on al-'Aqida al-Sughra' (fol. 43) that it was said: the negations are called a self-subsistent state or a self-subsistent attribute (2), and it is also the school of Imam al-Haramayn. He said in 'al-Irshad' (p. 31): 'The accepted opinion is that existence should not be counted among the attributes; for existence is the essence itself.' Then he said: 'And the imams, may Allah be pleased with them, are lenient in counting existence among the attributes, and knowledge of it is knowledge of the essence.' As an attribute, but when existence is used to describe the essence in speech, so it is said: 'The essence of our Master, glorified and exalted, is existing' ... it is valid to count it as an attribute in a general sense (1). And as for the school of one who makes existence additional to the essence, like Imam al-Razi ... then counting it among the attributes is correct with no tolerance in it. And among them is one who makes it additional to the essence in the contingent but not in the eternal; and that is the school of the philosophers (3). (1) An explanation of the aspect of tolerance, and a prelude to mentioning the disagreement of Imam al-Razi. (2) Where he said in 'Ma'alim Usul al-Din' (p. 32), which is among his later books: 'Existence is additional to the essences.' And it is a matter in which Imam al-Razi held varying positions, for his statement in 'al-Muhassal' (p. 43) opposes this, and he said in 'al-Ishara' (p. 75), which is among his early books: 'It is not permissible that the existence of the Creator, exalted, be other than His reality.' (3) See the refutation of them on this issue in 'Muhassal Afkar al-Mutaqaddimin wa al-Muta'akhkhirin' (p. 43). And three statements have been derived concerning existence: First: that it is identical to the existent in the eternal and the contingent. Second: that it is a mental consideration additional to the existent in the eternal and the contingent. Third: that it is identical to the existent in the eternal, additional in the contingent.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage is a theological discussion on the attribute of existence (al-wujud) and its classification as an attribute of the divine essence. It begins by stating that existence is a self-subsistent attribute (sifa nafsaniyya), meaning its concept is self-evident. The commentator notes that counting existence as an attribute in the Ash'ari school is a tolerance (tasamuh) because according to Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari existence is identical to (identical to, 'ayn) the essence, not additional. The first footnote elaborates: existence means realization in the external world, and it is self-subsistent. Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni defined a self-subsistent attribute as one that is an affirmation for the self, necessary as long as the self remains, and not caused by other attributes subsisting in the subject. The commentator then discusses a related point: one might think that non-delimitedness (adam al-tahayyuz) is a self-subsistent attribute, but the verified view is that it is a negative attribute. The commentary then returns to the main text: because existence is linguistically used to describe the essence (e.g., 'the essence is existing'), it is acceptable to count it as an attribute in a general sense. Next, the passage contrasts this with the school of Imam al-Razi, who holds that existence is additional (za'id) to the essence; in his view, counting existence as an attribute is strictly correct without tolerance. The philosophers hold a middle position: existence is additional for contingent beings but identical for the Eternal (God). The footnote on al-Razi notes his inconsistency across works. Finally, the passage enumerates three positions on existence: (1) identical to the existent in both eternal and contingent, (2) additional in both, (3) identical in the eternal and additional in the contingent. The passage thus clarifies the technical status of existence as an attribute and presents the main theological stances.

Key Terms
الوجود (al-wujud)
الصفة النفسية (al-sifat al-nafsaniyya)
بديهي التصور (badihi al-tasawwur)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the meaning of existence as stated in the first footnote?
A) It is a self-evident concept.
B) It is a created entity.
C) It is additional to the essence.
D) It is a negative attribute.

Key Takeaway
Existence is a self-subsistent attribute that is self-evident in conception and affirms the reality of the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If existence is self-evident, why do you often live as if you are not truly present in your own life? What would change if you daily affirmed your existence as a direct gift from God?
?
The self-subsistent attribute of existence is not caused by anything else. In what ways do you act as if you are self-sufficient, forgetting that your very being depends on God each moment?
?
Imam al-Haramayn says a self-subsistent attribute lasts as long as the self remains. How does knowing that God sustains your existence every instant affect your trust in Him today?

The Ash'ari Tolerance and Linguistic Counting of Existence
Further explanation of the Ash'ari tolerance and the linguistic counting of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why counting existence as an attribute in the Ash'ari school is considered a tolerance, and how linguistic usage allows it.

Core Idea
The Ash'ari school treats existence as identical to essence but counts it as an attribute as a tolerance based on linguistic convention.

This lesson continues the discussion on the attribute of existence. It explores the idea that while al-Ash'ari held existence is identical to the essence, the imams allowed counting it as an attribute due to linguistic usage. It also touches on the relationship between negative attributes and self-subsistent attributes, clarifying that non-delimitedness is a negative attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on minds' incapacity
Read the Arabic text and translation. Notice the statement that minds cannot conceive Allah's essence fully.
This sets the context for why existence is the only self-subsistent attribute perceived.
2
Understand the discussion on non-delimitedness
Follow the argument about non-delimitedness (adam al-tahayyuz) and whether it is a self-subsistent attribute. Note the conclusion that it is a negative attribute.
Imam al-Haramayn initially suggests it might be self-subsistent, but the verified view is that it is negative.
3
Grasp the tolerance in counting existence as an attribute
Identify the reason why existence can be counted as an attribute despite being identical to the essence: because it is used linguistically to describe the essence.
The key phrase: 'when existence is used to describe the essence in speech, it is valid to count it as an attribute in a general sense.'

The commentator notes that counting existence as an attribute in the Ash'ari school is a tolerance (tasamuh) because according to Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari existence is identical to (identical to, 'ayn) the essence, not additional. The first footnote elaborates: existence means realization in the external world, and it is self-subsistent. Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni defined a self-subsistent attribute as one that is an affirmation for the self, necessary as long as the self remains, and not caused by other attributes subsisting in the subject. The commentator then discusses a related point: one might think that non-delimitedness (adam al-tahayyuz) is a self-subsistent attribute, but the verified view is that it is a negative attribute. The commentary then returns to the main text: because existence is linguistically used to describe the essence (e.g., 'the essence is existing'), it is acceptable to count it as an attribute in a general sense. Next, the passage contrasts this with the school of Imam al-Razi, who holds that existence is additional (za'id) to the essence; in his view, counting existence as an attribute is strictly correct without tolerance. The philosophers hold a middle position: existence is additional for contingent beings but identical for the Eternal (God). The footnote on al-Razi notes his inconsistency across works. Finally, the passage enumerates three positions on existence: (1) identical to the existent in both eternal and contingent, (2) additional in both, (3) identical in the eternal and additional in the contingent. The passage thus clarifies the technical status of existence as an attribute and presents the main theological stances.

Key Terms
تسامح (tasamuh)
عدم التحيز (adam al-tahayyuz)
الصفات السلبية (al-sifat al-salbiyya)
عين الذات (ayn al-dhat)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Counting existence as an attribute is a contradiction in Ash'ari theology.
Correction: It is a tolerance based on linguistic usage; existence is identical to essence ontologically but can be called an attribute in speech.

Checkpoint
What is the reason given for allowing existence to be counted as an attribute?
A) Because it is additional to the essence.
B) Because it is linguistically used to describe the essence.
C) Because it is a negative attribute.
D) Because the philosophers required it.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari school counts existence as an attribute as a tolerance based on linguistic usage, despite holding that existence is identical to the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah exists,' do you truly acknowledge that His existence is utterly unlike anything you know, or do you unconsciously imagine a being like yourself but greater? How will you train your mind to maintain that distinction in your daily worship?
?
The imams tolerated calling existence an attribute for the sake of language. In your own life, are there theological concepts you have reduced to mere words and labels without grasping their deeper reality? What step will you take this week to move beyond superficial understanding?
?
If your mind cannot fully conceive Allah's essence, how does that humble you in your pursuit of knowledge? Are you willing to accept the limits of your intellect, or do you still try to box Allah into categories you control?

The Three Positions on Existence and Essence
Enumeration of three positions on existence being identical or additional to essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three theological positions on whether existence is identical to or additional to the essence, for both the eternal and contingent.

Core Idea
There are three views: existence is identical to essence (Ash'ari), additional to essence (Razi), and additional only in contingent (Philosophers).

This lesson presents the three main positions on the relationship between existence and essence, especially regarding the divine and created beings. It includes the view of Imam al-Razi, who held that existence is additional to the essence, and the philosophers' compromise.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on al-Razi's school
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice that al-Razi considers existence additional to the essence, so counting it as an attribute is strictly correct.
The footnote mentions al-Razi's inconsistency across his works.
2
Identify the philosophers' position
Find the philosophers' view: existence is additional for contingent beings but identical for the eternal.
This is a middle position between al-Ash'ari and al-Razi.
3
Study the three enumerated statements
The passage ends with a clear list of three positions. Read each one carefully.
They are: (1) identical in both, (2) additional in both, (3) identical in eternal, additional in contingent.
4
Understand the significance
Reflect on why this difference matters for theology, especially regarding God's simplicity and created beings.
The identity view emphasizes God's simplicity; the addition view allows for a distinction between essence and existence.

And it is existence. The self-subsistent attribute. Its meaning is apparent (1), and regarding counting existence as an attribute in the school of Shaykh al-Ash'ari, it is a tolerance (2), because according to him it is identical to the essence, not additional to it. (1) Because it is self-evident in conception, and its meaning is: realization and affirmation in the external, and it is a self-subsistent attribute. And the meaning of the self-subsistent attribute as stated by Imam al-Haramayn in 'al-Irshad' (p. 30): 'Every attribute of affirmation for a self, necessary as long as the self remains, not caused by causes subsisting in the described.' And when minds fell short of conceiving His essence, exalted, in a hopeless incapacity, there remained for them from the perception of the self-subsistent attributes of Him, glorified, the attribute of existence, along with their acknowledgment that His existence, exalted, is not like our existence. And in the words of Imam al-Haramayn in his 'Answers to the Questions of Imam al-Siqilli' (p. 51) there is a suggestion that non-delimitedness of His essence, exalted, is among His self-subsistent attributes, but he said after that (p. 55): 'Ignorance concerning the self-subsistent attribute if it is correct that the self-subsistent attribute refers to other than existence...', so the verified view is that non-delimitedness reverts to the negative attributes in detail. But the scholar al-Saktani alerted in his 'Gloss on the Commentary on al-'Aqida al-Sughra' (fol. 43) that it was said: the negations are called a self-subsistent state or a self-subsistent attribute (2), and it is also the school of Imam al-Haramayn. He said in 'al-Irshad' (p. 31): 'The accepted opinion is that existence should not be counted among the attributes; for existence is the essence itself.' Then he said: 'And the imams, may Allah be pleased with them, are lenient in counting existence among the attributes, and knowledge of it is knowledge of the essence.' As an attribute, but when existence is used to describe the essence in speech, so it is said: 'The essence of our Master, glorified and exalted, is existing' ... it is valid to count it as an attribute in a general sense (1). And as for the school of one who makes existence additional to the essence, like Imam al-Razi ... then counting it among the attributes is correct with no tolerance in it. And among them is one who makes it additional to the essence in the contingent but not in the eternal; and that is the school of the philosophers (3). (1) An explanation of the aspect of tolerance, and a prelude to mentioning the disagreement of Imam al-Razi. (2) Where he said in 'Ma'alim Usul al-Din' (p. 32), which is among his later books: 'Existence is additional to the essences.' And it is a matter in which Imam al-Razi held varying positions, for his statement in 'al-Muhassal' (p. 43) opposes this, and he said in 'al-Ishara' (p. 75), which is among his early books: 'It is not permissible that the existence of the Creator, exalted, be other than His reality.' (3) See the refutation of them on this issue in 'Muhassal Afkar al-Mutaqaddimin wa al-Muta'akhkhirin' (p. 43). And three statements have been derived concerning existence: First: that it is identical to the existent in the eternal and the contingent. Second: that it is a mental consideration additional to the existent in the eternal and the contingent. Third: that it is identical to the existent in the eternal, additional in the contingent.

This passage is a theological discussion on the attribute of existence (al-wujud) and its classification as an attribute of the divine essence. The Three Opinions on Existence as an Attribute
Sanusi enumerates the three views on existence as an attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three theological opinions on whether existence is identical to the essence or an additional attribute, and the basis for counting existence as an attribute.

Core Idea
Existence is not a real attribute like knowledge; its status depends on one's metaphysical view.

This passage addresses a subtle but important question in Islamic theology: Is existence itself an attribute of God? Different schools of thought have answered differently. Sanusi presents three distinct opinions, ranging from identifying existence with the essence to treating it as an additional mental consideration. Understanding these views is essential for grasping the doctrine of divine simplicity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three views
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then read the translation. Look for the three enumerated opinions about existence.
The passage also discusses when existence can be called an attribute loosely or strictly.
2
Compare the views with divine simplicity
Notice that the first view (existence identical to essence) aligns with strong divine simplicity. The second and third views introduce some form of composition.
This connects to broader debates in kalam.
3
Reflect on why this matters for theology
Consider how each view affects the understanding of God's attributes. Is existence a real attribute or just a concept?
The commentary will help clarify.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بصفة، لكن لما كان الوجود توصف به الذات في اللفظ، فيقال: ذات مولانا جل وعز موجودة... صح أن يعد صفة على الجملة. وأما على مذهب من يجعل الوجود زائدا على الذات؛ كالإمام الرازي... فعدّه من الصفات صحيح لا تسامح فيه. ومنهم من يجعله زائدا على الذات في الحادث دون القديم؛ وهو مذهب الفلاسفة. وقد تحصل في الوجود ثلاثة أقوال: الأول: أنه عين الموجود في القديم والحادث. الثاني: أنه أمر اعتباري زائد على الموجود في القديم والحادث. الثالث: أنه عين الموجود في القديم، زائد في الحادث.
As an attribute, but since existence is described by the essence in verbal expression, it is said: the essence of our Lord, may He be glorified and exalted, is existing... it is correct to count it as an attribute loosely. And as for the school of those who make existence additional to the essence, like Imam al-Razi... so counting it among the attributes is correct without looseness. And among them is one who makes it additional to the essence in originated things but not in the Eternal; this is the school of the philosophers. And three statements have been obtained concerning existence: First: that it is identical to the existent in the Eternal and originated. Second: that it is a mental consideration additional to the existent in the Eternal and originated. Third: that it is identical to the existent in the Eternal, additional in the originated.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage discusses whether existence (al-wujud) is considered an attribute of God. The author begins by noting that existence is used to describe the essence in speech, so it can be counted as an attribute loosely (tasamuh). However, on the view that existence is additional to the essence (like Imam al-Razi's view), it is truly an attribute. A third view holds that existence is additional only for contingent beings, not for the Eternal. Then three summary opinions are listed: (1) that existence is identical to the existent in both eternal and originated (i.e., it is merely conceptual); (2) that it is an additional mental consideration in both; (3) that it is identical in the Eternal but additional in the originated. These represent different theological positions on divine simplicity.

Correction: The passage clarifies that existence is only counted as an attribute in a qualified sense; for many theologians, it is not a real attribute but a mere concept.

Key Takeaway

The Negative Attribute of Pre-Eternity (Qidam)
Sanusi defines pre-eternity as a negative attribute, presents alternative views, and distinguishes its usage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why pre-eternity (qidam) is classified as a negative attribute and how it differs when applied to Allah versus created things.

Core Idea
Pre-eternity does not add a quality; it negates non-existence.

This lesson focuses on the divine attribute of pre-eternity (al-qidam). Sanusi clarifies that this attribute is not a positive quality existing in the divine essence, but rather a negation of prior non-existence. He also contrasts two other scholarly views and explains how the term is used for created beings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three views
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, noting that the author presents three opinions: the most correct view (negative attribute) and two opposing views.
The two opposing views are that pre-eternity is a self-subsistent attribute or an attribute of meaning.
2
Understand the key: negative attribute
Focus on the definition of pre-eternity as a negative attribute: it does not express an existing quality but rather the negation of non-existence before existence.
3
Learn the three equivalent expressions
The author gives three ways to express this negation: negation of prior non-existence, negation of priority for existence, and negation of beginning of existence. Understand that they mean the same thing.
4
Distinguish the meaning for Allah vs. creatures
When applied to Allah, pre-eternity means negation of prior non-existence. When applied to created things (like an ancient building), it only means long duration, not true pre-eternity.
The Quranic example illustrates the created usage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقدم. صفاته صفته القدم. الأصح: أن القدم صفة سلبية، ومقابل الأصح قولان: الأول: أنه صفة نفسية، والثاني: أنه من صفات المعاني. أي: ليست بمعنى موجود في نفسها كالعلم مثلا، وإنما هو عبارة عن سلب العدم السابق على الوجود، وإن شئت قلت: هو عدم الأولية للوجود، وإن شئت قلت: هو عبارة عن عدم افتتاح الوجود، والعبارات الثلاث بمعنى واحد. هذا معنى القدم في حقه تعالى باعتبار ذاته العلية وصفاته الجليلة السنية. وأما معناه إذا أطلق في حق الحادث؛ كما إذا قلت مثلا: هذا بناء قديم، وعرجون قديم: فهو عبارة عن طول مدة وجوده وإن كان حادثا مسبوقا بعدم؛ كما في قوله تعالى: {إنك لفي ضلالك القديم} [يوسف: 95]، وقوله عز وجل: (آية).
And pre-eternity (al-qidam). His attributes. the attribute of pre-eternity. The most correct view is that pre-eternity is a negative attribute; and opposite to the most correct are two views: first, that it is a self-subsistent attribute (nafsiyya), and second, that it is among the attributes of meanings (ma'ani). That is, it is not something existing in itself like knowledge, for example. Rather, it is an expression for the negation of non-existence before existence. And if you wish, you can say: it is the negation of priority for existence. And if you wish, you can say: it is an expression for the negation of the beginning of existence. And the three expressions have the same meaning. This is the meaning of pre-eternity in respect of the Most High, with regard to His exalted essence and His noble attributes. As for its meaning when used in respect of the originated, as if you say, for example: this building is ancient, and a dry palm branch is ancient: it is an expression for the length of its existence, even though it is originated preceded by non-existence; as in His saying, the Most High: 'Indeed you are in your ancient error' (12:95), and His saying, the Most High: (verse).
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage defines the divine attribute of pre-eternity (al-qidam). The author states that the most correct view (al-ashahh) is that pre-eternity is a negative attribute (sifa salbiyya). He then notes two opposing views: one that it is a self-subsistent attribute (nafsiyya) and another that it is an attribute of meaning (ma'nawiyya). The negative attribute means it does not denote an existing entity like knowledge, but rather the negation of non-existence prior to existence. Three equivalent expressions are given: negation of prior non-existence, negation of priority for existence, and negation of the beginning of existence. The author then distinguishes this meaning for the Creator from its meaning for created things. When applied to originated things like an ancient building, it means long duration, not true pre-eternity, since those things are preceded by non-existence. A Quranic example is cited.

Key Terms
القدم
صفة سلبية
صفة نفسية
صفات المعاني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Pre-eternity is a positive attribute that adds a quality to the divine essence, like knowledge.
Correction: Pre-eternity is a negative attribute; it does not posit any quality but rather negates prior non-existence.

Checkpoint
What is the most correct view regarding the attribute of pre-eternity (al-qidam)?
A) It is a self-subsistent attribute.
B) It is an attribute of meaning.
C) It is a negative attribute.
D) It is a relational attribute.

Key Takeaway
Pre-eternity (qidam) is a negative attribute that negates prior non-existence; when applied to creatures it merely indicates long duration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'God is eternal,' do you truly feel the weight that He has no beginning and that your very existence is a gift from One who always was? How does this awareness shape your sense of responsibility toward Him today?
?
If pre-eternity is a negation of non-existence, then your existence is entirely dependent on God. Does your daily life reflect this dependence, or do you live as though you are self-sufficient? What one habit could change that?
?
The passage distinguishes between God's pre-eternity and created 'ancientness.' When you hear about something 'ancient' in the world, do you ever confuse it with God's eternity? In what area of your life do you need to clear away such confusion to truly exalt God as the only Eternal?

Definition of Qidam as a Negative Attribute
Sanusi defines qidam as a negative attribute with three equivalent expressions, then distinguishes its meaning for Allah vs. creatures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the attribute of qidam (eternity) is a negative attribute, meaning the negation of any prior non-existence, and how this meaning differs when applied to Allah versus created things.

Core Idea
Qidam for Allah means no beginning, not old age.

Sanusi begins by clarifying a key divine attribute: qidam. Many people mistakenly think of eternity as a positive quality like knowledge or power. In reality, qidam is a negative attribute – it tells us what Allah is not: He is not preceded by non-existence. In this passage, Sanusi defines qidam and distinguishes its meaning for Allah versus for contingent things like old buildings or trees.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Pay attention to the three equivalent definitions Sanusi gives.
Let the author's own words sink in before analyzing.
2
Identify the three expressions
Notice that Sanusi offers three ways to say the same thing: negation of preceding non-existence, non-priority of existence, and non-commencement. Understand that they all mean the same.
All three are equivalent, capturing the idea of no beginning.
3
Distinguish divine qidam from created oldness
Recognize that when applied to Allah, qidam means absolute lack of beginning; when applied to creatures, it means long duration. See why the latter meaning is impossible for Allah because He is beyond time.
This distinction is crucial to avoid anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالْقِدَمُ. الْأَصَحُّ: أَنَّ الْقِدَمَ صِفَةٌ سَلْبِيَّةٌ، أَيْ: لَيْسَتْ بِمَعْنَى مَوْجُودٍ فِي نَفْسِهَا كَالْعِلْمِ مَثَلًا، وَإِنَّمَا هُوَ عِبَارَةٌ عَنْ سَلْبِ الْعَدَمِ السَّابِقِ عَلَى الْوُجُودِ، وَإِنْ شِئْتَ قُلْتَ: هُوَ عَدَمُ الْأَوَّلِيَّةِ لِلْوُجُودِ، وَإِنْ شِئْتَ قُلْتَ: هُوَ عِبَارَةٌ عَنْ عَدَمِ افْتِتَاحِ الْوُجُودِ، وَالْعِبَارَاتُ الثَّلَاثُ بِمَعْنًى وَاحِدٍ. هَذَا مَعْنَى الْقِدَمِ فِي حَقِّهِ تَعَالَى بِاعْتِبَارِ ذَاتِهِ الْعَلِيَّةِ، وَصِفَاتِهِ الْجَلِيلَةِ السَّنِيَّةِ، وَأَمَّا مَعْنَاهُ إِذَا أُطْلِقَ فِي حَقِّ الْحَادِثِ، كَمَا إِذَا قُلْتَ مَثَلًا: هَذَا بِنَاءٌ قَدِيمٌ، وَعُرْجُونٌ قَدِيمٌ: فَهُوَ عِبَارَةٌ عَنْ طُولِ مُدَّةِ وُجُودِهِ وَإِنْ كَانَ حَادِثًا مَسْبُوقًا بِعَدَمٍ، كَمَا فِي قَوْلِهِ تَعَالَى: ﴿إِنَّكَ لَفِي ضَلَلِكَ الْقَدِيمِ﴾ [يُوسُف: ٩٥]، وَقَوْلِهِ عَزَّ وَجَلَّ: ﴿الْقَدِيمِ﴾ [يس: ٣٩]، فَالْقِدَمُ عَلَى اللَّهِ تَعَالَى بِهَذَا الْمَعْنَى مُحَالٌ; لِأَنَّ وُجُودَهُ جَلَّ وَعَزَّ لَا يَتَقَيَّدُ بِزَمَانٍ وَلَا مَكَانٍ لِحُدُوثِ كُلِّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا، فَلَا يَتَقَيَّدُ بِوَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا إِلَّا مَا هُوَ حَادِثٌ.
And al-qidam (eternity/preexistence). The soundest opinion is that al-qidam is a negative attribute, i.e., it is not in the sense of something existing in itself like knowledge for example, but rather it is an expression for the negation of the non-existence preceding existence. And if you wish you may say: it is the non-existence of priority for existence; and if you wish you may say: it is an expression for the non-existence of the commencement of existence; and the three expressions have one meaning. This is the meaning of qidam with respect to Him Most High considering His exalted essence and His noble and sublime attributes. As for its meaning when applied to a contingent thing, as when you say for example: this is an ancient building, and an ancient palm branch: it is an expression for the length of its duration even though it is contingent and preceded by non-existence, as in His statement Most High: 'Indeed you are in your old error' (Yusuf 95) and His statement Might and Majesty: 'the old' (Yasin 39). So qidam in this meaning with respect to Allah Most High is impossible, because His existence, Glorified and Exalted, is not bound by time nor place due to the contingency of each of them; therefore nothing is bound by either of them except that which is contingent.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage defines the attribute of qidam (eternity/preexistence) as a negative attribute (sifah salbiyyah). It begins by stating the soundest opinion (al-ashah) that qidam is not a positive quality existing in itself (like knowledge), but rather it is the negation of preceding non-existence. The author gives three equivalent expressions: (1) negation of the non-existence that precedes existence, (2) non-existence of priority for existence, and (3) non-existence of the commencement of existence. All three convey the same meaning. Then he distinguishes the meaning of qidam when applied to Allah versus when applied to contingent things. For Allah, it means the negation of any prior non-existence. For contingent things, like an old building or an ancient palm branch, it indicates length of duration even though they are preceded by non-existence, as supported by Quranic verses. Finally, he asserts that qidam in the sense of long duration (i.e., oldness) is impossible for Allah because His existence is not bounded by time or space, since time and space are themselves contingent. Hence, only contingent things are bounded by them.

Key Terms
القدم (al-qidam)
صفة سلبية (sifah salbiyyah)
سلب العدم السابق (salbu al-'adami as-sabiq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Qidam is a positive attribute existing in itself, similar to knowledge.
Correction: Sanusi explicitly states that qidam is a negative attribute; it is not a positive quality but rather the negation of any prior non-existence.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what does the attribute of qidam (eternity) essentially mean when applied to Allah?
A) It means Allah has existed for a very long time.
B) It is the negation of any prior non-existence.
C) It means Allah is old in the sense of having a long duration.
D) It is a positive attribute like knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Qidam is a negative attribute meaning that Allah was not preceded by non-existence, unlike created things which may be described as old but are still contingent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider Allah's eternal existence, do you ever unconsciously imagine Him as having a beginning in time? How does understanding qidam as a negation of any beginning transform your awe of Him? What must you actively remind yourself to avoid reducing His eternity to a stretched-out timeline?
?
The lesson distinguishes qidam for Allah (no beginning) from qidam for creatures (long duration but contingent). Is there any area of your life where you treat Allah as if He operates within created limits like time? What concrete change can you make today to affirm His transcendence over time?
?
If qidam tells you what Allah is not (not preceded by non-existence), are you more comfortable affirming positive attributes (like power) than negative ones? Why does that comfort reveal about your heart's tendency to anthropomorphize? Will you commit to studying negative attributes with the same reverence as positive ones?

The Disagreement over Naming Allah 'al-Qadim'
Sanusi discusses the disagreement on whether the term 'al-Qadim' is permissible as a divine name.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly disagreement about whether 'al-Qadim' is a legitimate divine name and how the Sunnah provides evidence for its usage.

Core Idea
Divine names are revealed, but the Sunnah can confirm a name not explicit in the Quran.

In this passage, Sanusi presents a methodological issue: can we call Allah 'al-Qadim'? While the meaning of pre-eternity is necessary for Allah, some scholars hesitated because the name is not found in the Quran as such. However, evidence from hadith supports its use. Let's examine the argument and its resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: the issue is posed, two positions are described, and then evidence is provided.
Pay attention to the term 'tawqifiyyah' which is key to understanding the hesitation.
2
Identify the two positions
The passage presents two views: (1) It is permissible to say 'Allah is Qadim' because its meaning is necessary. (2) It is not permissible because divine names are tawqifiyyah and the name is not explicitly in the Quran.
3
Understand the evidence from hadith
Al-'Iraqi cites al-Halimi who notes that while the name is not in the Quran as a text, it appears in the Sunnah (Ibn Majah's hadith listing the ninety-nine names). This resolves the hesitation.
This shows that the Sunnah can supplement the Quran in naming Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْخِلَافُ فِي جَوَازِ إِطْلَاقِ لَفْظِ (الْقَدِيمِ) عَلَيْهِ سُبْحَانَهُ. وَهَلْ يَجُوزُ أَنْ يُتَلَفَّظَ بِلَفْظِ (الْقَدِيمِ) فِي حَقِّهِ تَعَالَى; فَيُقَالَ: هُوَ جَلَّ وَعَزَّ قَدِيمٌ; لِأَنَّ مَعْنَاهُ وَاجِبٌ لَهُ جَلَّ وَعَزَّ عَقْلًا وَنَقْلًا، أَوْ لَا يُتَلَفَّظَ بِذَلِكَ، وَإِنَّمَا يُقَالَ: يَجِبُ لَهُ تَعَالَى الْقِدَمُ، أَوْ نَحْوُ هَذَا مِنَ الْعِبَارَاتِ وَلَا يُطْلَقَ عَلَيْهِ فِي اللَّفْظِ اسْمُ (الْقَدِيمِ) لِأَنَّ أَسْمَاءَهُ جَلَّ وَعَزَّ تَوْقِيفِيَّةٌ؟ هَذَا مِمَّا تَرَدَّدَ فِيهِ بَعْضُ الْمَشَايِخِ، لَكِنَّ الْعِرَاقِيَّ قَالَ فِي شَرْحِ أُصُولِ السُّبْكِيِّ: (عَدَّهُ الْحَلِيمِيُّ فِي الْأَسْمَاءِ، وَقَالَ: لَمْ يَرِدْ فِي الْكِتَابِ نَصًّا، وَلَكِنْ وَرَدَ فِي السُّنَّةِ). قَالَ الْعِرَاقِيُّ: وَأَشَارَ بِذَلِكَ إِلَى مَا رَوَاهُ ابْنُ مَاجَهْ فِي سُنَنِهِ مِنْ حَدِيثِ أَبِي هُرَيْرَةَ رَضِيَ اللَّهُ عَنْهُ، وَفِيهِ عَدُّ (الْقَدِيمِ) مِنَ التِّسْعَةِ وَالتِّسْعِينَ.
The disagreement concerning the permissibility of uttering the word 'al-qadim' with respect to Him Exalted: is it permissible to utter the word 'al-qadim' concerning Him Most High, so that it is said: He, Glorified and Exalted, is Qadim, because its meaning is necessarily attributed to Him both rationally and textually? Or should it not be uttered, and rather it is said: qidam is necessary for Him, or some such expression, and the name 'al-qadim' is not applied to Him in speech because His names, Glorified and Exalted, are tawqifiyyah (based on revelation)? This is something about which some shaykhs have hesitated. However, al-'Iraqi said in Sharh Usul al-Subki: 'Al-Halimi counted it among the names, and said: It has not come as a text in the Book, but it has come in the Sunnah.' Al-'Iraqi said: He indicated by that what Ibn Majah narrated in his Sunan from the hadith of Abu Hurayrah, may Allah be pleased with him, in which 'al-Qadim' is counted among the ninety-nine.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage presents the disagreement over whether it is permissible to refer to Allah with the name 'al-Qadim'. Some scholars hesitate because divine names are tawqifiyyah (based on revelation) and the term 'qadim' may not be explicitly mentioned in the Quran as a name. However, al-'Iraqi, quoting al-Halimi, notes that while it is not in the Quran as a direct text, it is found in the Sunnah, specifically in Ibn Majah's hadith listing the ninety-nine names. Therefore, the use of the term is permissible.

Key Terms
القديم (al-qadim)
توقيفية (tawqifiyyah)
العراقي (al-'Iraqi)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that all divine names must be explicitly mentioned in the Quran.
Correction: The Sunnah also serves as revelation and can establish names of Allah, as seen with 'al-Qadim'.

Checkpoint
What was the hesitation of some scholars regarding the term 'al-qadim'?
A) They thought qidam is not an attribute of Allah.
B) They were unsure whether the term 'al-qadim' is a revealed divine name.
C) They believed qadim implies oldness which is inappropriate.
D) They thought the term only applies to creatures.

Key Takeaway
The name 'al-Qadim' is established for Allah through the Sunnah, even though it is not explicitly in the Quran as a name.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you refer to Allah by a name or attribute, do you ever pause to verify that it is revealed? What does this lesson teach you about the responsibility of speaking about Allah?
?
The hesitation of some scholars shows their deep caution. How careful are you in avoiding speculative speech about Allah? Will you commit to only using names and descriptions that have clear scriptural basis?
?
If the name 'al-Qadim' is confirmed by the Sunnah even though not in the Quran, does that challenge any assumption you had about how we know Allah's names? How will you adjust your approach to learning about His attributes?
