The Difference Between Negative and Negating Attributes
Sanusi distinguishes attributes called 'negative' from those that merely negate their opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise distinction between a 'negative attribute' (salbiyya) and a 'negating attribute' (sāliba), and why attributes of meanings are not classified as negative despite negating their opposites.

Core Idea
Not every attribute that negates is a negative attribute—direct vs. indirect indication matters.

In this passage, Sanusi addresses a subtle objection: if attributes of meanings (like power) negate their opposites, why are they not called 'negative'? The answer reveals a critical technical distinction based on how the attribute's wording indicates negation—directly or by implication. This distinction is essential for correctly classifying divine attributes in Ashʿarī theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Understand why someone might think attributes of meanings are negative because they negate opposites.
2
Identify the answer's two parts
Sanusi agrees they are 'negating' but not 'negative' in the technical sense. Note the difference.
3
Grasp the quote from al-Mallawi
The difference lies in direct (muṭābaqa) vs. indirect (iltizām) indication.
4
Apply to the example of power
How 'power' directly indicates a positive quality and indirectly negates weakness.
5
Reflect on the importance
Why Ashʿarīs maintain this distinction for their attribute classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قد يقال : وصفات المعاني أيضاً سلبية ؛ لأنها تسلب أضدادها ؛ فالقدرة مثلاً تسلب عن الله تعالى العجز ، وهكذا . والجواب : لا خلاف في كون صفات المعاني سالبة أضدادها عنه سبحانه ، ولكن هذا السلب حاصل بقيامها بذاته سبحانه ، ولذا نقل العلامة الدسوقي في حاشيته ( ص ۱۱۱ ) عن المحقق عبد الفتـاح الملوي قـولـه : والتحقيق : أن الصفة السلبية مباينة للسالبة ؛ لأن السلبية : ما دل لفظها على نفي نقص مطابقةً ؛ كالخمسة المذكورة ، والسالبة : ما دلّ لفظها على نفي نقص التزاماً ، وذلك كالقدرة وما معها من صفات المعاني ، فلفظ « القدرة » يدل مطابقة على صفة يتأتى بها إيجاد الممكن ، ويدل التزاماً على سلب العجز ، وهكذا
It may be said: The attributes of meanings are also negative because they negate their opposites; for example, power negates weakness from Allah, and so on. The answer: There is no dispute that attributes of meanings are negating (sāliba) of their opposites from Him, but this negation occurs by their inherence in His essence. Hence, the scholar al-Dasūqī in his gloss (p. 111) reported from the investigator Shaykh ʿAbd al-Fattāḥ al-Mallawī his saying: The verification is that the negative attribute (ṣifa salbiyya) is different from the negating attribute (ṣifa sāliba); because the negative (salbiyya) is what its wording indicates directly (muṭābaqa) the negation of a deficiency, such as the five mentioned [attributes]. The negating (sāliba) is what its wording indicates indirectly (iltizāman) the negation of a deficiency, such as power and the other attributes of meanings. For the word 'power' indicates directly an attribute by which the possible is brought into existence, and indicates indirectly the negation of weakness, and so on.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer concerning the classification of attributes. The objector argues that attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-maʿānī) like power and knowledge could also be called 'negative' (salbiyya) because they negate their opposites (e.g., power negates incapability). The answer begins by agreeing that attributes of meanings are indeed 'negating' (sāliba) of their opposites, but clarifies that this negation is a consequence of their actual positive existence in the divine essence. The key distinction is made by quoting Shaykh ʿAbd al-Fattāḥ al-Mallawī: a true 'negative attribute' (ṣifa salbiyya) is one whose wording directly (muṭābaqa) signifies the negation of a deficiency — like the five attributes of pre-eternity, permanence, etc. In contrast, a 'negating attribute' (ṣifa sāliba) is one whose wording directly signifies a positive quality and only indirectly (iltizāman) negates its opposite. For example, the word 'power' directly denotes a positive attribute enabling creation, and only by implication negates weakness. Thus, attributes of meanings are not classified as 'negative' in the technical sense, even though they negate opposites.

Key Terms
سلبية
سالبة
صفات المعاني
مطابقة
التزام
نفي نقص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Because attributes of meanings (like power) negate their opposites, they are technically 'negative attributes' (salbiyya).
Correction: No; they are 'negating' (sāliba) because the negation is indirect (by implication), not the direct meaning of their wording.

Checkpoint
How does the word 'power' (qudra) indicate the negation of weakness?
A) Directly, by its primary meaning
B) Indirectly, by implication (iltizām)
C) Partially, via composition
D) Neither; it does not indicate negation

Key Takeaway
A negative attribute (salbiyya) directly negates deficiency in its wording, while a negating attribute (sāliba) only indirectly negates its opposite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'Allāh is powerful' (qādir), does your heart primarily dwell on His ability to create and command, or on the fact that He is not weak? Which focus brings you closer to awe of Him?
?
This lesson distinguishes direct from indirect knowledge of God. Are you content to know Him only indirectly—by what He is not—or do you actively seek to know His positive attributes through reflection on His names and actions?
?
Precision in describing Allah is not mere intellectual exercise; it shapes worship. In your prayer and supplication, do you ever catch yourself defining Allah by negations ('You are not unjust') more than by affirmations ('You are the Just')? How might that affect your trust in Him?

The Five Negative Attributes Defined
Sanusi defines the five negative divine attributes and explains each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the five divine attributes after existence are called 'negative' and what each negation signifies.

Core Idea
Negative attributes do not add a positive quality; they simply remove what does not befit Allah.

Before defining the five negative attributes, Sanusi first clarifies the special case of existence. Then he explains that the remaining five attributes are 'negative'—each one denies something unbefitting Allah. This lesson walks through that distinction and the meaning of each negative attribute.

Guided Path
1
Clarify the status of existence
Read the first part about existence being a psychological attribute only when considered additional to the essence. Understand why existence is counted among attributes despite the debate.
Sanusi apologizes for counting existence as an attribute, noting it is really a psychological attribute.
2
Understand what negative attributes mean
The passage then introduces the concept of negative attributes: each signifies the absence of a deficiency, not a positive quality like knowledge or power.
3
Study each of the five attributes
Go through each of the five: pre-eternity (negation of prior non-existence), permanence (negation of subsequent non-existence), being unlike contingents (negation of similarity), self-subsistence (negation of need for a substrate and a maker), and oneness (negation of duality, quantity, and partner).
Sanusi gives alternative phrasings for some attributes.
4
Reflect on the overall significance
Consider how these attributes safeguard divine perfection by negating any imperfection. They are foundational to Islamic theology.

فإذا عرفت هذا فاعلم : أنَّ الوجودَ إِنَّما يصحُ أنْ يكونَ صفةً نفسيَّةً عندَ مَنْ يجعلُهُ زائداً على الذاتِ ، وأمَّا مَنْ يجعلُه نفسَ الذاتِ فليس بصفةٍ أصلاً ، وقد سبق الاعتذار عن عدهِ مِنَ الصفاتِ ، وبمثل ذلك يُعتذَرُ هنا عن عدهِ مِنَ الصفات النفسية ؛ أي : معنى الوجودِ راجع للذاتِ ، سواءٌ قلنا : إِنَّهُ عينُ الذاتِ ، أو زائد على حقيقتها ؛ لأنَّ الذات لا تثبتُ في الخارج عن الذهنِ إلا أن تكونَ موجودةً . قوله : والخمسة بعدها سلبيةٌ يعني : أن مدلول كل واحد منها عدم أمر لا يليق بمولانا جل وعزَّ ، وليس مدلولها صفة موجودة في نفسها كما في العلم والقدرة ونحوهما مِنْ سائر صفاتِ المعاني الآتية . فالقدم معناه سلبٌ ؛ وهو نفي سبقِ العدم على الوجودِ ، وإن شئتَ قلت : هو نفي الأوَّليَّة للوجودِ ، والمعنى واحد . والبقاء : هو نفي لحوقِ العدم للوجودِ . والمخالفة للحوادثِ : هي نفي المماثلة لها في الذات والصفاتِ والأفعال . والقيام بالنفس : هو نفي افتقار الذات العليَّة إلى محل ؛ أي : ذات أخرى تقوم بها قيامَ الصفةِ بالموصوف ، ونفي افتقارِهِ تعالى إلى مخصص ؛ أي : فاعل . والوحدانية : عدم الاثنينيَّة في الذاتِ العليَّة والصفاتِ والأفعال ، وإن شئتَ قلتَ : هي نفي الكمِّيَّةِ المتصلة والمنفصلة ، ونفي الشريكِ في الأفعالِ عموماً ، والمعنى واحد . وبالله تعالى التوفيق .
When you know this, then know that existence is only valid as a psychological attribute for one who makes it additional to the essence. As for one who makes it the very essence, it is not an attribute at all. The apology for counting it among the attributes has preceded, and by the like of that one apologizes here for counting it among the psychological attributes; that is: the meaning of existence refers back to the essence, whether we say it is identical to the essence or additional to its reality, because the essence is not established in external reality apart from the mind unless it is existent. His statement: 'And the five after it are negative' means that the signification of each of them is the non-existence of a thing unbefitting our Lord, Mighty and Majestic, and their signification is not an attribute existent in itself as in knowledge, power, and the like from the rest of the attributes of meanings to come. Thus pre-eternity (qidam) means negation; it is the negation of the precedence of non-existence over existence. If you wish, you may say: it is the negation of the firstness for existence, and the meaning is one. And permanence (baqā’) is the negation of the attachment of non-existence to existence. And being unlike contingents (al-mukhālafa li-l-ḥawādith) is the negation of similarity to them in essence, attributes, and actions. And self-subsistence (al-qiyām bi-n-nafs) is the negation of the essence’s need for a substrate; that is, another essence in which it inheres as an attribute inheres in its subject, and the negation of His need for a specifying agent; that is, a maker. And oneness (al-waḥdāniyya) is the absence of duality in the essence, attributes, and actions. If you wish, you may say: it is the negation of continuous and discrete quantity, and the negation of a partner in actions generally, and the meaning is one. And with Allah the Exalted is success.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage defines the five negative divine attributes. First, Sanusi clarifies that existence as an attribute is only considered a 'psychological attribute' (ṣifa nafsiyya) if one holds it to be additional to the essence; otherwise it is the essence itself and not a separate attribute. The apology he mentions refers to a previous justification for counting existence as an attribute despite this ambiguity. Then he notes that the five attributes after existence are 'negative' (salbiyya), meaning each signifies the absence of something unbefitting Allah, not a positive quality like knowledge or power. He then defines each: 1) Pre-eternity (qidam): negation of the precedence of non-existence over existence; equivalent to negation of firstness. 2) Permanence (baqā’): negation of subsequent non-existence. 3) Being unlike contingents (mukhālafa li-l-ḥawādith): negation of similarity to created beings in essence, attributes, and actions. 4) Self-subsistence (qiyām bi-n-nafs): negation of need for a substrate (i.e., another essence in which to inhere) and negation of need for a specifying agent (maker). 5) Oneness (waḥdāniyya): negation of duality in essence, attributes, and actions; alternatively, negation of continuous and discrete quantity (quwwa muttaṣila wa munfaṣila) and negation of a partner in all actions. He concludes with a prayer for divine assistance.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that negative attributes are actual positive qualities inherent in Allah.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that they are mere negations of deficiencies, not positive attributes like knowledge or power.

Key Takeaway
The five negative attributes are essential negations that protect the doctrine of God's transcendence and uniqueness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If the negative attributes only remove imperfections from Allah, do you ever subconsciously imagine Him with human-like traits? What specific image do you need to strip away today?
?
The attribute of oneness negates all duality and partnership—not just in words but in your heart's devotion. Is there any person, desire, or fear that competes with Allah for your ultimate trust? How will you uproot it this week?
?
Sanusi says these negations protect Allah's transcendence. When you face trials, do your complaints imply that Allah is unjust or uncaring—attributes that befit only creation? Confess one moment this week where your heart slandered His perfection, and resolve to replace it with praise.

Five Essential Attributes Defined as Negations
Sanusi defines five essential divine attributes as negations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari tradition defines five essential divine attributes through negations, explaining what each attribute excludes about God.

Core Idea
God's essential attributes are defined by what they negate, not by positive additions.

In classical Ash'ari theology, divine attributes are often described in negative terms to safeguard God's transcendence. This passage from Sanusi's work presents five essential attributes (al-qidam, al-baqa', al-mukhalafa, al-qiyam bi-nafs, al-wahdaniyya) by stating what each one denies about God. Understanding this approach helps avoid anthropomorphism and affirms God's absolute uniqueness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's rhythm come through before interpreting.
Notice that each attribute is introduced with a definition.
2
Identify the negation pattern
For each attribute, find the negation word (salb, nafi, 'adam) and what is being negated. See how the author provides alternative phrasings.
The pattern shows consistency: attribute = negation of imperfection.
3
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider why the author uses negations instead of positive descriptions. What does this guard against?
This method protects God's transcendence and avoids likening Him to creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالقدم معناه سلبٌ ؛ وهو نفي سبقِ العدم على الوجودِ ، وإن شئتَ قلت : هو نفي الأوَّليَّة للوجودِ ، والمعنى واحد. والباقاء : هو نفي لحوقِ العدم للوجودِ. والمخالفة للحوادثِ : هي نفي المماثلة لها في الذات والصفاتِ والأفعال. والقيام بالنفس : هو نفي افتقار الذات العليَّة إلى محل ؛ أي : ذات أخرى تقوم بها قيامَ الصفةِ بالموصوف ، ونفي افتقارِهِ تعالى إلى مخصص ؛ أي : فاعل . والوحدانية : عدم الاثنينيَّة في الذاتِ العليَّة والصفاتِ والأفعال ، وإن شئتَ قلتَ : هي نفي الكمِّيَّةِ المتصلة والمنفصلة ، ونفي الشريكِ في الأفعالِ عموماً ، والمعنى واحد ، وبالله تعالى التوفيق.
Pre-eternity (al-qidam) means a negation, which is the negation of precedence of non-existence over existence. And if you wish, you say: it is the negation of being first for existence, and the meaning is one. And everlastingness (al-baqa') is the negation of the attachment of non-existence to existence. And being different from originated things (al-mukhalafatu lil-hawadith) is the negation of similarity to them in essence, attributes, and actions. And self-subsistence (al-qiyamu bin-nafs) is the negation of the need of the supreme essence for a substrate (mahall), meaning another essence which subsists by it as the attribute subsists by the described, and the negation of His needing a particularizer (mukhasis), meaning an agent. And Oneness (al-wahdaniyya) is the absence of duality in the supreme essence, attributes, and actions; and if you wish, you say: it is the negation of continuous and discrete quantity (al-kammiyya al-muttasila wal-munfasila), and the negation of a partner in actions generally, and the meaning is one. And with God Almighty is success.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage defines five essential divine attributes by stating what each one negates. The author follows the Ash'ari method of defining attributes primarily as negations of imperfections. He begins with al-qidam (pre-eternity): it negates the priority of non-existence over existence, meaning God has always existed and nothing preceded Him. Then al-baqa' (everlastingness) negates the attachment of non-existence, so God never ceases to exist. Al-mukhalafatu lil-hawadith (being different from originated things) negates any similarity to creation in essence, attributes, or actions, emphasizing God's utter transcendence. Al-qiyamu bin-nafs (self-subsistence) negates any need for a substrate or a particularizer; God does not depend on anything else for His existence or distinction. Finally, al-wahdaniyya (oneness) negates any kind of multiplicity or partner, whether in essence, attributes, or actions. The author notes that these definitions are essentially expressing the same idea from different angles.

Correction: The author emphasizes they are negations, not positive attributes added to God's essence.

Key Takeaway
The five essential attributes (pre-eternity, everlastingness, difference from creation, self-subsistence, oneness) are defined by negating what is incompatible with divine perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The passage defines al-qiyam bin-nafs as negating any need for a substrate or particularizer. Do you sometimes act as if God needs your worship to sustain Him, rather than recognizing that He is utterly self-sufficient? What would change in your prayer today if you truly internalize this?
?
Al-mukhalafa teaches that God is unlike anything you know or imagine. What mental image of God have you unconsciously formed that resembles something in creation? Will you actively negate that image, no matter how comfortable it feels?
?
Oneness (al-wahdaniyya) negates any partner in essence, attributes, and actions. Is there a person, possession, or ambition that you treat as indispensable alongside God—something you could not imagine living without? What step will you take today to dethrone it from that place in your heart?

Literal and Implied Meaning of Divine Attributes
Sanusi explains the literal and implied meaning of the term 'power'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how terms for divine attributes have both a literal (mutabaqa) and an implied (iltizam) meaning, using 'power' (qudra) as an example.

Core Idea
Every term for a divine attribute carries two layers of meaning: direct and implied.

Before diving into the classification of attributes, Sanusi illustrates the semantic richness of theological language. He shows that a word like 'power' (al-qudra) names a specific attribute but also implies its opposite's absence. This distinction is foundational for later discussions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the structure: one sentence gives the example.
2
Identify the two types of indication
Find where Sanusi mentions 'direct correspondence' (mutabaqa) and 'implication' (iltizam). Understand what each refers to.
3
Reflect on why this distinction matters
Consider how this double meaning applies to other attribute terms. Why is it important to recognize that terms imply more than their direct meaning?
This connects to avoiding misunderstandings about God's attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نقص التزاماً ، وذلك كالقدرة وما معها من صفات المعاني ، فلفظ « القدرة » يدل مطابقة على صفة يتأتى بها إيجاد الممكن ، ويدل التزاماً على سلب العجز ، وهكذا . صفات المعاني: ثمَّ يَجِبُ لَهُ تَعَالَى سَبْعُ صِفَاتٍ تُسَمَّى : صِفَاتِ الْمَعَانِي . 6 مرادهم بصفاتِ المعاني : الصفاتُ التي هي موجودة في نفسها سواء كانَتْ حادثة ؛ كبياض الجرمِ مثلاً وسوادِه ، أو قديمة ؛ كعلمه تعالى وقدرته . فكلُّ صفةٍ موجودة في نفسها فإنَّها تُسمَّى في الاصطلاح : صفة معنى. وإنْ كانَتِ الصفةُ غير موجودة في نفسِها ؛ فإنْ كانَتْ واجبةً للذاتِ ما دامَتِ الذاتُ غير معلَّلةٍ بعلَّةٍ . . سُمِّيَتْ صفةً نفسيَّةً أو حالاً نفسيةً ومثالها : التحيُّز للجرم ، وكونه قابلاً للأعراض مثلا(۱) وإنْ كانَتِ الصفةُ غير موجودة في نفسها إلا أنَّها معلَّلَةٌ إِنَّما تجب للذاتِ ما دامَتْ علتها قائمة بالذاتِ .. سُمِّيَتْ صفةً معنويةً أو حالاً معنويّةً ، مثالها : كونُ الذاتِ عالمة أو قادرةً مثلاً. (۱) ولذا يعبرون عن هذا المعنى فيقولون : ما بالذات - أو : ما بالنفس لا يتخلف ولا يختلف ، فقبول الجرم للأعراض لا يمكن تخلفه ، لا يقال : يمكننا تصور الجرم ابتداء مع الغفلة عن الأعراض ؛ لأن هذا التصور لا يمكن ثبوته إلا بتصور بعض الأعراض.
Deficiency in implicatory meaning, such as power and what is with it from the attributes of meanings. For the term 'power' (al-qudra) indicates by direct correspondence (mutabaqa) an attribute by which the possible is brought into existence, and indicates by implication (iltizam) the negation of incapacity, and so on. Attributes of Meanings: Then seven attributes become necessary for Him, Most High, called 'attributes of meanings' (sifaat al-ma'ani). 6 By 'attributes of meanings' they mean: attributes that are existent in themselves, whether they are contingent, like the whiteness of a body or its blackness, or eternal, like His knowledge and power. So every attribute existent in itself is called in terminology: an attribute of meaning (sifat ma'na). And if the attribute is not existent in itself, and if it is necessary for the essence as long as the essence is not caused by a cause, it is called an attribute of the self (sifat nafsiyya) or a state of the self (hal nafsiyya). And its example: occupying space (al-tahayyuz) for a body, and its being receptive to accidents, for example. (1) And if the attribute is not existent in itself but it is caused, only becoming necessary for the essence as long as its cause subsists in the essence, it is called an attribute of meaning (sifat ma'nawiyya) or a state of meaning (hal ma'nawiyya). Its example: the essence being knowing or able, for example. (1) Therefore they express this meaning by saying: what is in the self—or what is in the psyche—does not cease and does not differ; so the body's receptivity to accidents cannot cease. It is not said: we can conceptualize the body initially while disregarding accidents, because this conceptualization cannot be established except by conceptualizing some accidents.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage begins by illustrating how terms for divine attributes have both a literal (mutabaqa) and an implied (iltizam) meaning. For instance, 'power' literally denotes the attribute by which God creates, but it also implies the negation of incapacity. This sets the stage for the technical classification of attributes. The author then explains that God has seven attributes called 'attributes of meanings' (sifaat al-ma'ani). These are attributes that exist in themselves, i.e., real, extra-mental entities, whether created (like a body's whiteness) or eternal (like God's knowledge). Next, he distinguishes two other kinds of attributes that do not exist in themselves: first, attributes necessary for an essence as long as the essence is not caused—these are called 'attributes of the self' (sifaat nafsiyya) or 'states of the self' (hal nafsiyya), exemplified by a body's property of occupying space or being receptive to accidents. Second, attributes that do not exist in themselves but are caused, existing only as long as their cause subsists—these are called 'attributes of meaning' (sifaat ma'nawiyya) or 'states of meaning' (hal ma'nawiyya), exemplified by the state of being knowing or powerful, which are caused by the attribute of knowledge or power. The footnote clarifies that such attributes (like a body's receptivity) are inseparable from the essence; one cannot conceive of a body without some accident.

الدلالة الالتزام
القدرة

The term 'power' directly names an attribute and indirectly negates its opposite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If you had held that truth deeply, how would your response have been different? Will you commit to reciting 'Ya Qadir' with the full weight of its implied meaning the next time you feel powerless?

Classification of Attributes: Sifat al-Ma'ani, Nafsiyya, and Ma'nawiyya
Sanusi defines and classifies three types of attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the threefold classification of attributes into those that exist in themselves (sifat al-ma'ani), those that are necessary without cause (sifat nafsiyya), and those that are caused (sifat ma'nawiyya).

Core Idea
Attributes are classified by whether they exist independently and whether they are caused.

Having introduced the concept of attributes, Sanusi now provides a precise technical classification. He distinguishes between attributes that are real entities 'out there' and those that are mere states or properties that do not have independent existence. This taxonomy is essential for understanding later discussions on God's seven attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of Sifat al-Ma'ani
Read the passage about attributes that exist in themselves. Note that they can be contingent or eternal.
2
Distinguish the two non-existent types
Find the descriptions for 'sifat nafsiyya' (uncaused) and 'sifat ma'nawiyya' (caused). Understand the condition that determines each.
3
Study the examples
Look at the examples: occupying space vs. being knowing. See how they illustrate the differences.
4
Consider the footnote's clarification
Read the footnote about inseparable attributes. Why is it impossible to conceive a body without some accident?
This reinforces the idea that some attributes are necessary for the essence.

Deficiency in implicatory meaning, such as power and what is with it from the attributes of meanings. For the term 'power' (al-qudra) indicates by direct correspondence (mutabaqa) an attribute by which the possible is brought into existence, and indicates by implication (iltizam) the negation of incapacity, and so on. Attributes of Meanings: Then seven attributes become necessary for Him, Most High, called 'attributes of meanings' (sifaat al-ma'ani). 6 By 'attributes of meanings' they mean: attributes that are existent in themselves, whether they are contingent, like the whiteness of a body or its blackness, or eternal, like His knowledge and power. So every attribute existent in itself is called in terminology: an attribute of meaning (sifat ma'na). And if the attribute is not existent in itself, and if it is necessary for the essence as long as the essence is not caused by a cause, it is called an attribute of the self (sifat nafsiyya) or a state of the self (hal nafsiyya). And its example: occupying space (al-tahayyuz) for a body, and its being receptive to accidents, for example. (1) And if the attribute is not existent in itself but it is caused, only becoming necessary for the essence as long as its cause subsists in the essence, it is called an attribute of meaning (sifat ma'nawiyya) or a state of meaning (hal ma'nawiyya). Its example: the essence being knowing or able, for example. (1) Therefore they express this meaning by saying: what is in the self—or what is in the psyche—does not cease and does not differ; so the body's receptivity to accidents cannot cease. It is not said: we can conceptualize the body initially while disregarding accidents, because this conceptualization cannot be established except by conceptualizing some accidents.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage begins by illustrating how terms for divine attributes have both a literal (mutabaqa) and an implied (iltizam) meaning. For instance, 'power' literally denotes the attribute by which God creates, but it also implies the negation of incapacity. This sets the stage for the technical classification of attributes. The author then explains that God has seven attributes called 'attributes of meanings' (sifaat al-ma'ani). These are attributes that exist in themselves, i.e., real, extra-mental entities, whether created (like a body's whiteness) or eternal (like God's knowledge). Next, he distinguishes two other kinds of attributes that do not exist in themselves: first, attributes necessary for an essence as long as the essence is not caused—these are called 'attributes of the self' (sifaat nafsiyya) or 'states of the self' (hal nafsiyya), exemplified by a body's property of occupying space or being receptive to accidents. Second, attributes that do not exist in themselves but are caused, existing only as long as their cause subsists—these are called 'attributes of meaning' (sifaat ma'nawiyya) or 'states of meaning' (hal ma'nawiyya), exemplified by the state of being knowing or powerful, which are caused by the attribute of knowledge or power. The footnote clarifies that such attributes (like a body's receptivity) are inseparable from the essence; one cannot conceive of a body without some accident.

Key Terms
صفات المعاني
صفة نفسية
حال نفسية
صفة معنوية
حال معنوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All attributes are independent entities.
Correction: Some attributes (sifat nafsiyya and ma'nawiyya) are not independently existent but are states or properties.

Checkpoint
What distinguishes a 'sifa nafsiyya' (attribute of the self) from a 'sifa ma'nawiyya' (attribute of meaning)?
A) The sifa nafsiyya is existent in itself, while the sifa ma'nawiyya is not.
B) The sifa nafsiyya is caused, while the sifa ma'nawiyya is not.
C) The sifa nafsiyya is necessary for the essence without a cause, while the sifa ma'nawiyya requires a cause.
D) The sifa nafsiyya is an accident, while the sifa ma'nawiyya is a substance.

Key Takeaway
Attributes are classified into three types based on existence and causality: existent in themselves, non-existent uncaused, and non-existent caused.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
God's knowledge is an eternal attribute existing in itself, yet you often act as if He is unaware of your private thoughts. Will you commit today to living with the certainty that every intention and deed is witnessed—and let that transform your inner life?
?
The attribute of existence for God is not an added entity but a necessary state of His essence. Do you truly believe that God's existence is more certain than your own? If you did, what would you dare to risk for His sake starting tomorrow?
?
The classification shows that some of God's states (like His being knowing) are caused by His real attributes (knowledge). Do you ever reduce His perfections to abstract concepts, forgetting they are real and active? How will you train your heart to feel the weight of His actual knowledge and power in your daily decisions?

Attributes of Meaning and Their Types
Sanusi defines attributes of meaning and classifies their types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of attributes of meaning (ṣifāt al-maʿānī) and the distinction between three types: self-subsisting attributes, essential attributes, and cognitive attributes.

Core Idea
Attributes are either self-subsisting, necessary without cause, or caused by a state.

Sanusi begins by establishing that Allah has seven attributes known as attributes of meaning. He then provides a precise classification that distinguishes between attributes that exist in themselves, those that are necessary for an essence without a cause, and those that are caused. This classification is foundational for understanding the divine attributes in Sunni theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the overall classification.
Notice the three categories.
2
Identify the three types
Find in the text the definitions of attributes of meaning, essential attributes, and cognitive attributes. Note the examples given for each.
The footnote clarifies the distinction between 'what is in the essence' and 'what is in the self'.
3
Apply the classification
Think of other attributes of Allah (like will, hearing) and consider which type they belong to. This prepares you for later lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ يَجِبُ لَهُ تَعَالَى سَبْعُ صِفَاتٍ تُسَمَّى : صِفَاتِ الْمَعَانِي . مرادهم بصفاتِ المعاني : الصفاتُ التي هي موجودة في نفسها سواء كانَتْ حادثة ؛ كبياض الجرمِ مثلاً وسوادِه ، أو قديمة ؛ كعلمه تعالى وقدرته . فكلُّ صفةٍ موجودة في نفسها فإنَّها تُسمَّى في الاصطلاح : صفة معنى. وإنْ كانَتِ الصفةُ غير موجودة في نفسِها ؛ فإنْ كانَتْ واجبةً للذاتِ ما دامَتِ الذاتُ غير معلَّلةٍ بعلَّةٍ . . سُمِّيَتْ صفةً نفسيَّةً أو حالاً نفسيةً ومثالها : التحير للجرم ، وكونه قابلاً للأعراض مثلا(۱) وإنْ كانَتِ الصفةُ غير موجودة في نفسها إلا أنَّها معلَّلَةٌ إِنَّما تجب للذاتِ ما دامَتْ علتها قائمة بالذاتِ .. سُمِّيَتْ صفةً معنويةً أو حالاً معنويّةً ، مثالها : كونُ الذاتِ عالمة أو قادرةً مثلاً (۱) ولذا يعبرون عن هذا المعنى فيقولون : ما بالذات - أو : ما بالنفس لا يتخلف ولا يختلف ، فقبول الجرم للأعراض لا يمكن تخلفه ، لا يقال : يمكننا تصور الجرم ابتداء مع الغفلة عن الأعراض ؛ لأن هذا التصور لا يمكن ثبوته إلا بتصور بعض الأعراض
Then seven attributes are necessary for Him, may He be exalted, called: attributes of meaning (ṣifāt al-maʿānī). By 'attributes of meaning' they mean: the attributes that exist in themselves, whether they are contingent (ḥāditha) – like the whiteness of a body, for example, and its blackness – or eternal (qadīma) – like His knowledge and power, may He be exalted. So every attribute that exists in itself is called in the terminology: an attribute of meaning (ṣifat maʿnā). And if the attribute does not exist in itself, then if it is necessary for the essence as long as the essence is not caused by a cause, it is called an essential attribute (ṣifa nafsiyya) or an essential state (ḥāl nafsiyya), and its example is: being qualified with the property (al-taḥayyur) for the body, and its being receptive to accidents, for example. And if the attribute does not exist in itself but is caused, being necessary for the essence only as long as its cause subsists in the essence, it is called a cognitive attribute (ṣifa maʿnawiyya) or a cognitive state (ḥāl maʿnawiyya); its example is: the essence being knowing or powerful, for example. (1) Therefore they express this meaning by saying: what is in the essence (mā bi-l-dhāt) – or: what is in the self (mā bi-l-nafs) does not change and does not differ. So the body's receptivity to accidents cannot be absent. It cannot be said: we can conceive the body initially while neglectful of accidents; because this conception cannot be established except by conceiving some accidents.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage defines the seven attributes of meaning (ṣifāt al-maʿānī) and classifies their types. The author begins by stating that these seven attributes are necessary for Allah. He then explains that by 'attributes of meaning' they mean attributes that exist in themselves, whether contingent or eternal. He then distinguishes two other types: the essential attribute (ṣifa nafsiyya) which is necessary for the essence without a cause, like the body's being qualified with the property (al-taḥayyur) and its receptivity to accidents; and the cognitive attribute (ṣifa maʿnawiyya) which is caused and exists only as long as its cause subsists, like the essence being knowing or powerful. The footnote clarifies this by noting that what is in the essence (mā bi-l-dhāt) is unchangeable; the body's receptivity to accidents is inseparable from it, and one cannot conceive the body without accidents.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that 'attributes of meaning' only refer to divine attributes and not to any self-subsisting attribute.
Correction: The definition applies to any self-subsisting attribute, whether contingent (like whiteness) or eternal (like divine knowledge).

Checkpoint

C) Essential attribute is eternal, cognitive is contingent.

Key Takeaway
Attributes of meaning are self-subsisting attributes; essential attributes are necessary without cause; cognitive attributes are caused.

Power and Will: Their Single Object and Distinct Effects
Sanusi explains the single object of power and will and their distinct effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that divine power and will both relate to all possible things, but while will specifies which possibility occurs, power executes that specification.

Core Idea
Power executes what will specifies — both share one object, possible things.

Sanusi now turns to two key attributes: power (qudra) and will (irāda). To understand divine action, we must see how they relate. This passage explains that they have the same target — all possible things — but their functions are distinct, with power's effect being dependent on will's decision.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once. Notice that Sanusi begins by stating that both attributes relate to all possible things, then distinguishes their effects.
The passage includes two footnotes that clarify the restriction to possible things and the nature of the derivation.
2
Identify the single object and the distinct aspects
Find where Sanusi says power and will have one object (possible things) but different aspects: will specifies the side (existence/non-existence), power brings it about in accordance with will.
3
Understand the derivative relationship
Notice that power's effect is described as a branch (farʿ) of will's effect. Reflect on why this order is intellectual, not temporal.
The second footnote clarifies that this derivation is essential, not temporal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صفتا القدرة والإرادة وتعلقاتها وهي القدرة والإرادة المتعلقتان بجميع الممكنات يعني : أنَّ القدرة والإرادة متعلَّقهما واحدٌ ؛ وهو الممكنات دون الواجبات والمستحيلات ، إلا أنَّ جهة تعلقهما بالممكناتِ مختلفة ؛ فالقدرةُ : صفةٌ تؤثر في وجودِ الممكن وإعدامِهِ على وَفْقِ الإرادة ، والإرادةُ : صفةٌ تؤثر في اختصاص أحد طرفي الممكنِ مِنْ وجود أو عدم أو طول أو قصر ونحوها . . بالوقوع بدلاً عن مقابله • فصار تأثير القدرة فرع تأثير الإرادة (٢) ؛ إذ لا يُوجِدُ مولانا جل وعزَّ مِنَ الممكنات أو يعدم بقدرتِهِ إلا ما أرادَ اللهُ تعالى وجودَهُ أو إعدامَهُ ، (١) القاعدة : أن الخبر المعرف بـ ( أل ) الجنسية يكون محصوراً في المبتدأ ؛ أي: الممكنات محصورة في التعلق ، هذا بحسب القاعدة ، والمراد هنا : أنه من حصر المبتدأ ؛ أي: أن التعلق محصور في الممكنات لا يتجاوزها إلى الواجبات والمستحيلات ، فما هنا مخالف للقاعدة . « عدوي » ( ق ١١ ) ، والتعلُّق : اقتضاء الصفة أمراً زائداً على القيام بمحلها (٢) التفرع أمر عقلي ، وترتب ذاتي لا زماني ، وظاهر أن الكلام في تعلق القدرة والإرادة الحادث ؛ وهو التنجيزي ، ولا يخفى ما في کلامه المسامحة ، والتحقيق أن يقال : إن تأثير الذات بالقدرة فرع تأثير الذات أو تخصيصها بالإرادة . « ياسين » ( ق ١٥٠ ) .
The two attributes of power and will and their relations. And they are power (al-qudra) and will (al-irāda), both relating to all possible things (al-mumkināt). He means: that power and will have one single object of relation – namely possible things, to the exclusion of necessary things and impossible things – except that the aspect of their relation to possible things is different. For power is an attribute that effects the existence of the possible and its non-existence in accordance with will; and will is an attribute that effects the specification of one of the two sides of the possible – existence or non-existence, length or shortness, and the like – by occurring instead of its opposite. So the effect of power is derivative from the effect of will (2); for our Master, glorious and exalted, does not bring into existence or annihilate by His power any of the possible things except what Allah, exalted, has willed to exist or to annihilate. (1) The rule: that the predicate defined by the article (al) indicating genus is restricted to the subject; i.e., possible things are restricted to the relation. This is according to the rule, but what is meant here is that it is from the restriction of the subject: i.e., the relation is restricted to possible things, not extending beyond them to the necessary and impossible. So this is contrary to the rule. (2) The derivation is intellectual (ʿaqlī), and the ordering is essential (dhātī), not temporal. Apparently the discussion concerns the relation of power and will that is contingent (al-ḥādith), i.e., the executive (al-tanjīzī), and there is no hidden vagueness in his statement. The verification is that one should say: the effect of the essence by power is derivative from the effect of the essence or its specification by will.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage explains the attributes of power (qudra) and will (irāda) and their relation to possible things. The author begins by stating that both power and will relate to all possible things, not to the necessary or impossible. However, the aspect of relation is different: power effects the existence or non-existence of the possible in accordance with will, while will effects the specification of which side (existence/non-existence, etc.) occurs. Thus the effect of power is dependent on the effect of will. The first footnote discusses a grammatical rule about the definite article, clarifying that the relation is restricted to possible things. The second footnote notes that this derivation is intellectual and essential, not temporal, and clarifies that the discussion concerns the contingent relational tie (al-tanjīzī).

Misconception: Some might think that power and will have different objects (e.g., power over possible things, will over something else).
Correction: Sanusi explicitly states that both attributes have one single object: all possible things. They differ only in the aspect and effect.

A) Power is independent of will.
B) Power's effect is derived from will.

Key Takeaway
Divine power executes what divine will specifies; they share one object (possible things) but have distinct roles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Ash'ari View: Divine Will Follows Knowledge
Sanusi states the Ash'ari position on divine will following knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari principle that divine will is in accordance with divine knowledge, meaning everything God knows will occur is willed.

Core Idea
God's will aligns perfectly with His knowledge.

Sanusi begins by laying out the foundational Ash'ari position on the relationship between God's will and His knowledge. This principle distinguishes Ash'aris from the Mu'tazila and is crucial for understanding divine decree. Let's examine the statement carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the first part of the Arabic passage up to 'فذلك مراده جلّ وعزّ' and its translation.
2
Identify the key claim
Notice that Sanusi says 'the effect of will is in accordance with knowledge'. This means God wills exactly what He knows will happen among possibilities.
This includes both existence and non-existence of possible things.
3
Reflect on the meaning
Think about why this matters: if God's will were independent of His knowledge, He might will something He knows will not happen, which is incoherent.
The Ash'ari view preserves divine consistency.
4
Transition to contrast
Note that Sanusi immediately contrasts this with the Mu'tazila view in the next lesson.
This sets up the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتأثير الإرادة عند أهل الحقِّ على وَفْقِ العلم (۱) ، فكلُّ ما علم الله تبارك وتعالى أنَّهُ يكونُ مِنَ الممكناتِ أو لا يكونُ . . فذلك مراده جلَّ وعزّ . والمُعْتَزِلَةُ قَبَّحَهُمُ اللهُ تعالى جَعَلُوا تَعَلُّقَ الإِرَادَةِ تَابِعاً لِلأَمْرِ ، فَلَا يُرِيدُ عِنْدَهُمْ مَوْلَانَا جَلَّ وَعَزَّ إِلَّا مَا أَمَرَ بِهِ مِنَ الإِيمَانِ وَالطَّاعَةِ ، سَوَاءٌ وَقَعَ ذَلِكَ أَمْ لَا . فَعِنْدَنَا : إِيمَانُ أَبِي جَهْلٍ مَأْمُورٌ بِهِ غَيْرُ مُرَادٍ لَهُ تَعَالَى ; لِأَنَّهُ جَلَّ وَعَزَّ عَلِمَ عَدَمَ وُقُوعِهِ ، وَكُفْرُ أَبِي جَهْلٍ مَنْهِيٌّ عَنْهُ وَهُوَ وَاقِعٌ بِإِرَادَةِ اللهِ تَعَالَى وَقُدْرَتِهِ . وَعِنْدَ المُعْتَزِلَةِ قَبَّحَ اللهُ تَعَالَى رَأْيَهُمْ (۲) : إِيمَانُهُ هُوَ الْمُرَادُ اللهُ تَعَالَى لَا كُفْرُهُ ، فَلَزِمَهُمْ أَنَّهُ وَقَعَ نَقْصٌ فِي مُلْكِ مَوْلَانَا جَلَّ وَعَزَّ ; إِذْ وَقَعَ فِيهِ عَلَى قَوْلِهِمْ مَا لَا يُرِيدُهُ ، تَعَالَى مَنْ لَهُ مُلْكُ السَّمَاوَاتِ وَالأَرْضِ وَمَا بَيْنَهُمَا عَنْ ذَلِكَ عُلُوّاً كَبِيراً
The effect of will according to the People of Truth is in accordance with knowledge (1). So everything that God, Blessed and Exalted, knows will occur among possible things or will not occur – that is His will, Mighty and Majestic. The Mu'tazila – may God Exalted disfigure them – made the attachment of will dependent on command, so according to them our Master, Mighty and Majestic, wills only what He commands of faith and obedience, whether that occurs or not. So according to us: the faith of Abu Jahl is commanded but not willed by Him Exalted, because He, Mighty and Majestic, knew its non-occurrence; and the disbelief of Abu Jahl is prohibited and it occurs by the will of God Exalted and His power. According to the Mu'tazila – may God disfigure their opinion (2) – his faith is willed by God Exalted, not his disbelief. So it necessarily follows for them that a deficiency occurred in the dominion of our Master, Mighty and Majestic, since according to their statement, what He does not will occurs in it. Exalted is He to Whom belongs the dominion of the heavens and the earth and what is between them, far above that, a great loftiness.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage contrasts the Ash'ari (Ahl al-Haqq) and Mu'tazila positions on divine will. The author first states the Ash'ari view: the effect of will (ta'thir al-irada) is in accordance with divine knowledge – whatever God knows will occur or not among possible things, that is what He wills. Then the Mu'tazila view: they make will dependent on command, so God wills only what He commands (faith and obedience), regardless of actual occurrence. The example of Abu Jahl illustrates the difference: according to Ash'aris, his faith is commanded but not willed (because God knew it would not occur), while his disbelief is prohibited but occurs by God's will and power. For Mu'tazila, his faith is willed, not his disbelief. The author argues that the Mu'tazila position implies a deficiency in God's dominion because something He does not will occurs in it, which is false. This refutes the Mu'tazila view.

العلم
أهل الحق
الممكنات

A) Divine command

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari principle: God wills exactly what He knows will occur among possible things – will follows knowledge.

The Mu'tazila View and Its Refutation
Sanusi contrasts Mu'tazila view and refutes it using Abu Jahl example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila position that divine will follows command, and why the Ash'aris refute it using the example of Abu Jahl.

Core Idea
Mu'tazila wrongly limit God's will to what He commands.

After stating the correct view, Sanusi presents the opposing Mu'tazila position and then refutes it with a concrete example. The example of Abu Jahl shows the absurd consequence of the Mu'tazila view: that something occurs in God's dominion which He does not will. Let's follow the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila claim
Read the sentence stating the Mu'tazila view: they make will dependent on command, so God wills only what He commands (faith and obedience).
2
Examine the Ash'ari example
Read the example of Abu Jahl: according to Ash'aris, his faith is commanded but not willed, while his disbelief is prohibited but willed and occurs.
This illustrates the distinction between command and will.
3
Understand the Mu'tazila position on the example
Read what the Mu'tazila say: Abu Jahl's faith is willed, not his disbelief.
4
Follow the refutation
Read the consequence: if Mu'tazila are correct, then since Abu Jahl's disbelief actually occurs, something happens that God does not will, implying a deficiency in His dominion.
Sanusi concludes this is impossible, affirming God's absolute sovereignty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتأثير الإرادة عند أهل الحقِّ على وَفْقِ العلم (۱) ، فكلُّ ما علم الله تبارك وتعالى أنَّهُ يكونُ مِنَ الممكناتِ أو لا يكونُ . . فذلك مراده جلَّ وعزّ . والمُعْتَزِلَةُ قَبَّحَهُمُ اللهُ تعالى جَعَلُوا تَعَلُّقَ الإِرَادَةِ تَابِعاً لِلأَمْرِ ، فَلَا يُرِيدُ عِنْدَهُمْ مَوْلَانَا جَلَّ وَعَزَّ إِلَّا مَا أَمَرَ بِهِ مِنَ الإِيمَانِ وَالطَّاعَةِ ، سَوَاءٌ وَقَعَ ذَلِكَ أَمْ لَا . فَعِنْدَنَا : إِيمَانُ أَبِي جَهْلٍ مَأْمُورٌ بِهِ غَيْرُ مُرَادٍ لَهُ تَعَالَى ; لِأَنَّهُ جَلَّ وَعَزَّ عَلِمَ عَدَمَ وُقُوعِهِ ، وَكُفْرُ أَبِي جَهْلٍ مَنْهِيٌّ عَنْهُ وَهُوَ وَاقِعٌ بِإِرَادَةِ اللهِ تَعَالَى وَقُدْرَتِهِ . وَعِنْدَ المُعْتَزِلَةِ قَبَّحَ اللهُ تَعَالَى رَأْيَهُمْ (۲) : إِيمَانُهُ هُوَ الْمُرَادُ اللهُ تَعَالَى لَا كُفْرُهُ ، فَلَزِمَهُمْ أَنَّهُ وَقَعَ نَقْصٌ فِي مُلْكِ مَوْلَانَا جَلَّ وَعَزَّ ; إِذْ وَقَعَ فِيهِ عَلَى قَوْلِهِمْ مَا لَا يُرِيدُهُ ، تَعَالَى مَنْ لَهُ مُلْكُ السَّمَاوَاتِ وَالأَرْضِ وَمَا بَيْنَهُمَا عَنْ ذَلِكَ عُلُوّاً كَبِيراً
The effect of will according to the People of Truth is in accordance with knowledge (1). So everything that God, Blessed and Exalted, knows will occur among possible things or will not occur – that is His will, Mighty and Majestic. The Mu'tazila – may God Exalted disfigure them – made the attachment of will dependent on command, so according to them our Master, Mighty and Majestic, wills only what He commands of faith and obedience, whether that occurs or not. So according to us: the faith of Abu Jahl is commanded but not willed by Him Exalted, because He, Mighty and Majestic, knew its non-occurrence; and the disbelief of Abu Jahl is prohibited and it occurs by the will of God Exalted and His power. According to the Mu'tazila – may God disfigure their opinion (2) – his faith is willed by God Exalted, not his disbelief. So it necessarily follows for them that a deficiency occurred in the dominion of our Master, Mighty and Majestic, since according to their statement, what He does not will occurs in it. Exalted is He to Whom belongs the dominion of the heavens and the earth and what is between them, far above that, a great loftiness.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage contrasts the Ash'ari (Ahl al-Haqq) and Mu'tazila positions on divine will. The author first states the Ash'ari view: the effect of will (ta'thir al-irada) is in accordance with divine knowledge – whatever God knows will occur or not among possible things, that is what He wills. Then the Mu'tazila view: they make will dependent on command, so God wills only what He commands (faith and obedience), regardless of actual occurrence. The example of Abu Jahl illustrates the difference: according to Ash'aris, his faith is commanded but not willed (because God knew it would not occur), while his disbelief is prohibited but occurs by God's will and power. For Mu'tazila, his faith is willed, not his disbelief. The author argues that the Mu'tazila position implies a deficiency in God's dominion because something He does not will occurs in it, which is false. This refutes the Mu'tazila view.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
الأمر
الإيمان
الكفر
القدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God wills something, He must also command it, or that what He commands automatically occurs.
Correction: Ash'aris distinguish command (amr) from will (irada): God commands faith but wills it only for those He knows will believe; He prohibits disbelief but wills it for those He knows will disbelieve.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari refutation, what problem follows from the Mu'tazila view?
A) God would be unjust
B) Something occurs in God's dominion that He does not will
C) God's knowledge would be incomplete
D) Human free will would be negated

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila view that will follows command leads to the impossible conclusion that something occurs in God's dominion against His will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If God's will is not limited to what He commands, then your own failures and sins are part of His decree. Does that make you more humble, or more fearful? How should it change your repentance?
?
When you pray for something that is good and commanded, but it doesn't happen, do you still believe God's will is best? What does the example of Abu Jahl teach you about aligning your heart with whatever God wills, even if it means He wills your faith not to occur?

Dividing Divine Will: Failed Attempts
Sanusi presents and refutes attempts to divide divine will into types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why attempts to divide divine will into types fail to resolve the theological issue of divine will and its relationship to human actions.

Core Idea
Dividing divine will does not fix the problem; any failed will implies incapability.

Theologians have tried to reconcile God's universal will with human moral responsibility by dividing the divine will into categories. This passage presents two such attempts and their refutation, showing that no division can remove the fundamental implication of incapability if God's will ever fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to understand the argument flow.
Notice the quotation from Yasin al-Ulaymi embedded in the text.
2
Identify the two divisions
Find the two proposed divisions: (1) into universal/legislative or coercive/elective, and (2) into command/legislation and decree/predestination.
Each division attempts to reconcile opposing theological views.
3
Understand the refutation
The refutation argues that even with these divisions, the failure of any willed object of God implies incapability, so the problem remains.
The key point: the implication of incapability does not depend on the type of will.
4
Note the final comment
The author notes that the second division resolves apparent contradictions in scripture but does not remove the core theological disagreement.
This shows that the issue is not merely scriptural but theological.
5
Reflect on the implication
Consider why any division of divine will fails: because the core issue is that any unfulfilled will implies a deficiency in God.
This reinforces the classical Ash‘ari position on divine will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحاول بعضهم الجمع بين القولين : فقالوا بانقسام الإرادة القديمة إلى كونية وشرعية ، أو قل : قسرية واختيارية ، قال المحقق العلامة ياسين العليمي في حاشيته ( ق ١٥٣ ) : ( وذكر بعضهم ما يرفع الإشكال والخلاف ; فقال : الإرادة نوعان : إرادة اختيار ; بمعنى : أنه تعالى أراد من العباد الإيمان والطاعة برغبتهم واختيارهم ، وإرادة قسر وإلجاء ; بمعنى : أنه ألجأهم إلى الفعل وقسرهم عليه ، ويستحيل تخلف المراد عن الثانية ; لما يلزم من تخلفه العجز ، لا عن الأولى ; لعدم استلزامه لذلك ; لأنه لو شاء لألجأهم وقسرهم على مراده ، ورُدَّ : بأنه يكفي في لزوم العجز تخلف مراد الله تعالى في ذلك وأما قول بعضهم : الإرادة قسمان : إرادة أمر وتشريع ، وإرادة قضاء وتقدير ; فالأولى تُسمَّى الإرادة الشرعية ; تتعلق بالطاعة لا بالمعصية ، والثانية الإرادة التقديرية ; شاملة لجميع الكائنات . . فلا يرفع الخلاف نعم ; يرفع التعارض بين الآيات والأحاديث بحسب الظاهر )
And some attempted to reconcile between the two statements, so they said: by dividing the eternal will into universal (kawniyya) and legislative (sharʿiyya), or rather: coercive (qasriyya) and elective (ikhtiyāriyya). The verifier, the great scholar Yāsīn al-ʿUlaymī, said in his gloss (q 153): (And some mentioned what removes the problem and the disagreement. They said: will is of two types: will of choice, meaning that He Exalted willed from servants faith and obedience willingly and by their choice; and will of coercion and compulsion, meaning that He compelled them to the act and coerced them upon it. It is impossible for the willed object to fail to occur regarding the second, because its failure to occur would imply incapability; not so regarding the first, because it does not necessitate that; for if He wished, He would have compelled them and coerced them upon His will. And it was refuted: that it is sufficient for the implication of incapability the failure of the willed object of God Exalted to occur in that. As for the statement of some: will is two kinds: will of command and legislation, and will of decree and predestination; the first is called the legislative will, it attaches to obedience not to disobedience; the second is the predestining will, encompassing all creatures. So it does not remove the disagreement. Yes, it removes the apparent contradiction between verses and hadiths.)
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage is a presentation and refutation of attempts to resolve the problem of divine will by dividing it into types. Some proposed dividing eternal will into universal (kawniyya) and legislative (shar'iyya), or coercive (qasriyya) and elective (ikhtiyariyya). The scholar Yasin al-Ulaymi reports that some said will is of two types: will of choice (irada ikhtiyar) – God wills faith and obedience willingly from servants, and will of coercion (irada qasr) – God compels. For the second, the willed object must occur; for the first, it may not, because God could have compelled if He wished. The refutation (rudda) states that even on this view, the failure of any willed object of God to occur still implies incapability, so the problem remains. Another division is mentioned: will of command and legislation (irada amr wa tashri') which attaches only to obedience, and will of decree and predestination (irada qada' wa taqdir) which encompasses everything. The author comments that this does not remove the theological disagreement, though it resolves apparent contradictions in scripture. Thus, no division truly solves the core issue.

الإرادة التقديرية
العجز
الخلاف

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that dividing divine will into types solves the problem of evil or human responsibility.
Correction: The refutation shows that any division still implies incapability if any willed object fails, so the core difficulty remains.

Checkpoint

A) Because coercive will cannot fail.
B) Because the failure of any willed object still implies incapability.
C) Because the division is not scriptural.
D) Because the division only works for possible things.

Key Takeaway
No division of divine will removes the implication of incapability if any willed object fails; the fundamental theological problem persists.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a disappointment—a prayer unanswered, a hope unfulfilled—do you subtly accuse God of failing to will what you wanted? This lesson shows that if God truly willed it, it must happen; otherwise, incapability is implied. Are you willing to accept that perhaps you misunderstood what He willed, and surrender your expectations to His perfect will? How will you practice that surrender today?
?
The scholars who divided divine will tried to protect God from the implication of incapability, but the refutation shows that even their best attempts fall short. In your own life, do you try to rationalize God's actions to make them 'fit' your understanding, rather than submitting to the mystery of His absolute sovereignty? What would it mean for you to trust Him without needing to categorize His will?
?
Sanusi notes that dividing will into legislative and predestining does not remove the underlying disagreement—it only reconciles texts. Are you content to live with unresolved tensions in your faith, or do you insist on neat answers? When you cannot reconcile God's decree with human moral responsibility, does it drive you to deeper fear and awe, or to despair? What step can you take today to embrace the paradox as part of tawḥīd?

The Ordered Attachments of Power, Will, and Knowledge
Sanusi enumerates the three attachments and states their hierarchical order.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari principle of the hierarchical ordering of divine attachments: knowledge precedes will, and will precedes power.

Core Idea
In divine action, knowledge guides will, and will directs power.

This passage from Sanusi's work presents a concise yet crucial principle in Ash'ari theology: the three divine attributes of power, will, and knowledge do not operate simultaneously but in a specific causal order. Understanding this hierarchy is key to grasping how God's attributes relate to His actions and the creation of possible things.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice that Sanusi begins with 'In short' and lists three attachments in a specific order.
The Arabic may contain a repetition; focus on the intended meaning.
2
Identify the three attachments
The passage names three: power (qudra), will (irada), and knowledge ('ilm). Identify what each attachment relates to according to the text.
Knowledge is specified as attaching to possible things (mumkinat).
3
Understand the hierarchical ordering
The passage states that the first (power) is ordered upon the second (will), and the second (will) is ordered upon the third (knowledge). This means knowledge is causally prior to will, and will prior to power.
Consider why this order matters for Ash'ari theology of divine action.
4
Reflect on implications
Think about how this hierarchy affects our understanding of God's attributes. Does it mean power is less important? Not at all; it shows the logical sequence in divine causation.
This principle is foundational for later discussions about free will and predestination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة : فالمتعلقات عندَ أهل الحقِّ ثلاثةٌ مُرتَّبَةٌ : تعلُّق القدرة ، وتعلُّق الإرادة ، وتعلق العلم بالممكناتِ العلم بالممكناتِ ، فالأوَّلُ مرتب على الثاني ، والثاني مرتب على الثالث
In short: the attachments according to the People of Truth are three, ordered: the attachment of power, the attachment of will, and the attachment of knowledge to possible things (repetition in Arabic). So the first is ordered upon the second, and the second is ordered upon the third.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage provides a concise enumeration of the three divine attachments (muta'allaqat) according to Ahl al-Haqq (Ash'aris): the attachment of power (qudra), the attachment of will (irada), and the attachment of knowledge ('ilm) to possible things. These are ordered: the first (power) is dependent upon the second (will), and the second (will) is dependent upon the third (knowledge). This means that in the causal sequence, knowledge precedes will, and will precedes power. Power brings about the act only after it is willed, and will decides what is known. Note that while knowledge attaches to all things (necessary, possible, impossible), here it is specified as attaching to possible things because power and will only attach to possible things. This hierarchical ordering is a fundamental Ash'ari principle.

Key Terms
المتعلقات
القدرة
الإرادة
العلم
الممكنات
المرتبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think all three attachments are co-equal or apply to the same objects in the same way.
Correction: The passage explicitly states they are ordered: knowledge guides will, and will directs power. Moreover, knowledge attaches to all things (including necessary and impossible), while power and will only attach to possible things.

Checkpoint

A) Power, then will, then knowledge.
B) Will, then knowledge, then power.
C) Knowledge, then will, then power.
D) All three are simultaneous.

Key Takeaway
The divine attachments are hierarchically ordered: knowledge precedes will, and will precedes power, reflecting the logical sequence in God's action.

Why Divine Power and Will Do Not Attach to the Necessary and Impossible
Sanusi proves the non-attachment of power and will to necessary and impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine power and will logically cannot apply to the necessary and impossible, and why this is not a deficiency.

Core Idea
Divine power and will only attach to possible things; logical impossibility is not impotence.

Sanusi presents a logical proof that the attributes of power and will, being effective, can only produce effects that are possible—i.e., those that admit non-existence and existence. The necessary being (God) cannot be an effect because it never had non-existence, and the impossible cannot be an effect because it can never exist. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this core theological principle.

Guided Path
1
Understand the key terms
Familiarize yourself with the Arabic terms: power (قدرة), will (إرادة), necessary (واجب), impossible (مستحيل), effect (أثر).
2
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall argument.
3
Follow the two-part proof
Notice how Sanusi treats the necessary and impossible separately, each leading to an absurdity (tahsil al-hasil and qalb al-haqiqa).
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand that the non-attachment is not a deficiency; rather, attachment would lead to greater absurdities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنَّما لم تتعلَّقِ القدرة والإرادة بالواجب والمستحيل ; لأنَّ القدرة والإرادة لمَّا كانتا صفتين مؤثرتين ، ومِنْ لازم الأثر أن يكون موجوداً بعد عدم . . لزمَ أنَّ ما لا يقبلُ العدمَ أصلاً كالواجب لا يقبلُ أنْ يكونَ . أثراً لهما ، وإلا لزم تحصيل الحاصل ، وما لا يقبلُ الوجود أصلاً كالمستحيل لا يقبلُ أيضاً أنْ يكونَ أثراً لهما ، وإلا لزم قلب الحقيقة ; برجوع المستحيل عين الجائز ، فلا قصور أصلاً في عدم تعلَّقِ القدرة والإرادة القديمتين بالواجب والمستحيل ، بل لو تعلَّقتا بهما لزمَ حينَئِذٍ القصور ; لأنَّهُ يلزم على هذا التقدير الفاسدِ أنْ يجوز تعلُّقهما بإعدام أنفسهما ، بل وبإعدام الذات العليَّةِ ، وبإثباتِ الألوهِيَّةِ لمَنْ لا يقبلُها
And why power and will do not attach to the necessary (al-wājib) and the impossible (al-mustaḥīl): because since power and will are attributes that produce an effect, and it is a necessary consequence of the effect that it exists after non-existence, it is necessary that what does not accept non-existence at all, like the necessary, cannot be an effect for them; otherwise, it would entail the acquisition of the already acquired (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil). And what does not accept existence at all, like the impossible, also cannot be an effect for them; otherwise, it would entail the inversion of reality (qalb al-ḥaqīqa), by making the impossible return as exactly the possible. So there is no deficiency at all in the non-attachment of the eternal power and will to the necessary and impossible. Rather, if they were to attach to them, then deficiency would necessarily follow, because upon this corrupt assumption it would be permissible for them to attach to the annihilation of themselves, and even to the annihilation of the supreme Essence, and to the establishing of divinity for one who does not accept it.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is a proof (dalil) explaining why divine power and will do not extend to the necessary (al-wajib) and the impossible (al-mustahil). The argument proceeds as follows: (1) Power and will are effective attributes (sifatan mu'athiratani) – they produce an effect. (2) An effect must exist after non-existence (min lazim al-athar an yujada ba'da 'adam). (3) The necessary (e.g., God) cannot be preceded by non-existence; therefore it cannot be an effect. If we assumed it could, that would be 'tahsīl al-hāsil' – obtaining what is already obtained, a logical absurdity. (4) The impossible cannot receive existence at all; it cannot be an effect. If we assumed it could, that would be 'qalb al-haqiqa' – inversion of reality, making the impossible into the possible. (5) Therefore, the non-attachment of power and will to these categories is not a deficiency; on the contrary, if they were attached, deficiency would arise because it would imply absurd consequences like the ability to annihilate God or to make a false god. So God's power is perfect; its scope is limited to possible things by logical necessity, not by incapability.

Key Terms
القدرة
الإرادة
الواجب
المستحيل
الأثر
تحصيل الحاصل
قلب الحقيقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God's power is limited because it cannot create the impossible.
Correction: The impossibility is logical (e.g., a square circle), not a limitation on divine power. The argument shows that attaching power to the impossible would lead to absurdities like inverting reality.

Checkpoint
Why can the necessary being not be an effect of divine power and will?
A) Because it does not exist.
B) Because it cannot be preceded by non-existence.
C) Because it is not a possible thing.
D) Because God does not will it.

Key Takeaway
Divine power and will logically attach only to possible things; their non-attachment to the necessary and impossible is a sign of perfection, not deficiency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever doubted God's power when a deeply desired outcome seemed impossible? How does this lesson challenge you to re-examine that doubt?
?
If you truly internalize that God's non-attachment to the impossible is a sign of His perfection, how would it change your reaction to unanswered prayers or tragic events that seem to contradict His power?
?
In what areas of your life do you secretly wish God would act against His own nature (e.g., override justice with mercy without repentance)? What does this lesson call you to surrender?

The Three Divine Relations and Their Hierarchical Order
Sanusi enumerates the three divine relations and their hierarchical order.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three relations of divine attributes (power, will, knowledge) to possible beings and how they are ordered hierarchically.

Core Idea
Divine knowledge, will, and power relate to possible beings in a necessary order: power depends on will, will on knowledge.

Sanusi begins by defining the three relations that connect God's attributes to created things. He emphasizes that these relations are not random but follow a specific sequence, with knowledge as the foundation, will in the middle, and power as the final executor. This order is crucial for understanding how God's attributes function.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening sentence
Read the sentence that identifies the three relations: power, will, and knowledge.
Notice the phrase 'according to the people of truth' — this signals orthodox Sunni doctrine.
2
Observe the dependency order
Sanusi states that the first (power) depends on the second (will), and the second (will) depends on the third (knowledge). Map out this dependency in your mind.
This order reflects the logical sequence: knowledge informs will, and will directs power.
3
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself why this order matters. How does it affect our understanding of God's actions? Consider that without knowledge, will would be blind; without will, power would be aimless.
This ordering ensures that God's actions are wise and purposeful.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة : فالمتعلقات عندَ أهل الحقِّ ثلاثةٌ مُرتَّبَةٌ : تعلُّق القدرة ، وتعلُّق الإرادة ، وتعلق العلم بالممكناتِ ، فالأوَّلُ مرتب على الثاني ، والثاني مرتب على الثالث
وإنَّما لم تتعلَّقِ القدرة والإرادة بالواجب والمستحيل ؛ لأنَّ القدرة والإرادة لمَّا كانتا صفتين مؤثرتين ، ومِنْ لازم الأثر أن يكون موجوداً بعد عدم ، لزمَ أنَّ ما لا يقبلُ العدمَ أصلاً كالواجب لا يقبلُ أنْ يكونَ أثراً لهما ، وإلا لزم تحصيل الحاصل ، وما لا يقبلُ الوجود أصلاً كالمستحيل لا يقبلُ أيضاً أنْ يكونَ أثراً لهما ، وإلا لزم قلب الحقيقة ؛ برجوع المستحيل عين الجائز ، فلا قصور أصلاً في عدم تعلَّقِ القدرة والإرادة القديمتين بالواجب والمستحيل ، بل لو تعلَّقتا بهما لزمَ حينَئِذٍ القصور ؛ لأنَّهُ يلزم على هذا التقدير الفاسدِ أنْ يجوز تعلُّقهما بإعدام أنفسهما ، بل وبإعدام الذات العليَّةِ ، وبإثباتِ الألوهِيَّةِ لِمَنْ لا يقبلُها
In summary: The relations according to the people of truth are three, arranged: the relation of power, the relation of will, and the relation of knowledge with possible things. The first is dependent on the second, and the second is dependent on the third. And power and will are not related to the necessary and the impossible, because power and will are two effective attributes, and from the requirement of effect is that it exists after non-existence. So it follows that what does not accept non-existence at all, like the necessary, cannot be an effect for them; otherwise, achieving the already achieved would follow. And what does not accept existence at all, like the impossible, also cannot be an effect for them; otherwise, reversing the reality would follow by turning the impossible into the possible. So there is no deficiency at all in the non-relation of the eternal power and will to the necessary and impossible. Rather, if they were related to them, then deficiency would follow, because upon this corrupt estimation it would be permissible for them to be related to the annihilation of themselves, and even to the annihilation of the Supreme Essence, and to the establishment of divinity for the one who does not accept it.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof concerning the three divine relations (ta'alluqāt). The author states that the relations of God to created things are three: the relation of power (qudra), the relation of will (irāda), and the relation of knowledge (ʿilm), all with respect to possible things (mumkināt). These are ordered hierarchically: power depends on will, and will depends on knowledge. He then explains why power and will do not attach to the necessary (wājib) and impossible (mustaḥīl). The reason is that power and will are effective attributes (ṣifatān muʾaththiratān) that produce an effect which comes into existence after non-existence. Therefore, something that cannot cease to exist (the necessary) cannot be an effect, as that would entail 'achieving the already achieved' (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil). Similarly, something that cannot exist (the impossible) cannot be an effect, as that would reverse reality (qalb al-ḥaqīqa). He concludes that there is no deficiency in power and will not relating to the necessary and impossible; rather, if they did relate, that would imply deficiency, because it would lead to absurd consequences like the ability to annihilate God or grant divinity to the undeserving.

Key Terms
المتعلقات (al-mutaʿalliqāt)
القدرة (al-qudra)
الإرادة (al-irāda)
العلم (al-ʿilm)
الممكن (al-mumkin)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following correctly represents the order of dependency among the three divine relations?
A) Power depends on will, will depends on knowledge
B) Knowledge depends on will, will depends on power
C) Will depends on power, power depends on knowledge
D) They are independent of each other

Key Takeaway
The three divine relations are ordered: power depends on will, and will depends on knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In my own life, do I act primarily out of impulse or do I first seek knowledge, then will, then act? How can I align my decision-making with the divine order of knowledge → will → power?
?
When I trust in God's decree, do I truly believe that every event flows from His knowledge and will, even the hardships? What would change in my heart if I internalized this hierarchy?
?
If God's power is dependent on His will and knowledge, what does that say about my own reliance on power without understanding? What is one area where I need to pause and seek knowledge before acting?

Why Divine Power and Will Do Not Apply to the Necessary and Impossible
Sanusi proves why power and will do not relate to the necessary and impossible using two arguments, and defends against the misconception of deficiency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof for why God's power and will do not extend to the necessary (wājib) and impossible (mustaḥīl), and why this is not a deficiency.

Core Idea
Power and will produce effects from non-existence, so they cannot apply to what cannot cease or cannot exist.

Having established the three relations, Sanusi now explains why two of them—power and will—do not relate to the necessary being (Allah) or to impossibilities. He provides two clear arguments based on the nature of effect and then shows that absence of relation is actually a perfection, not a limitation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the principle
Read the first part where Sanusi states that power and will are effective attributes, and effects come into existence after non-existence.
This principle is the foundation for the following arguments.
2
Analyze the argument for the necessary
Why can't power and will affect the necessary being? Because the necessary does not admit non-existence, so it cannot be an effect.
The fallacy here is 'achieving the already achieved' (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil).
3
Analyze the argument for the impossible
Why can't power and will affect the impossible? Because the impossible does not admit existence, so it cannot be an effect.
This would lead to 'reversing reality' (qalb al-ḥaqīqa).
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Sanusi says that non-relation is not a deficiency; rather, relation would be deficient because it would allow absurdities like annihilating God.
Consider how this defends divine perfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وإنَّما لم تتعلَّقِ القدرة والإرادة بالواجب والمستحيل ؛ لأنَّ القدرة والإرادة لمَّا كانتا صفتين مؤثرتين ، ومِنْ لازم الأثر أن يكون موجوداً بعد عدم ، لزمَ أنَّ ما لا يقبلُ العدمَ أصلاً كالواجب لا يقبلُ أنْ يكونَ أثراً لهما ، وإلا لزم تحصيل الحاصل ، وما لا يقبلُ الوجود أصلاً كالمستحيل لا يقبلُ أيضاً أنْ يكونَ أثراً لهما ، وإلا لزم قلب الحقيقة ؛ برجوع المستحيل عين الجائز ، فلا قصور أصلاً في عدم تعلَّقِ القدرة والإرادة القديمتين بالواجب والمستحيل ، بل لو تعلَّقتا بهما لزمَ حينَئِذٍ القصور ؛ لأنَّهُ يلزم على هذا التقدير الفاسدِ أنْ يجوز تعلُّقهما بإعدام أنفسهما ، بل وبإعدام الذات العليَّةِ ، وبإثباتِ الألوهِيَّةِ لِمَنْ لا يقبلُها
