And power and will are not related to the necessary and the impossible, because power and will are two effective attributes, and from the requirement of effect is that it exists after non-existence. And what does not accept existence at all, like the impossible, also cannot be an effect for them; otherwise, reversing the reality would follow by turning the impossible into the possible. So there is no deficiency at all in the non-relation of the eternal power and will to the necessary and impossible. Rather, if they were related to them, then deficiency would follow, because upon this corrupt estimation it would be permissible for them to be related to the annihilation of themselves, and even to the annihilation of the Supreme Essence, and to the establishment of divinity for the one who does not accept it.

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof concerning the three divine relations (ta'alluqāt). The author states that the relations of God to created things are three: the relation of power (qudra), the relation of will (irāda), and the relation of knowledge (ʿilm), all with respect to possible things (mumkināt). These are ordered hierarchically: power depends on will, and will depends on knowledge. He then explains why power and will do not attach to the necessary (wājib) and impossible (mustaḥīl). Key Takeaway
Divine power and will do not apply to the necessary and impossible because they produce effects from non-existence, and non-relation is a sign of perfection.

?
Imagine you encounter someone who says, 'If God is all-powerful, why can't He make a square circle?' After this lesson, how would you answer not just with logic but with conviction? Does your heart fully believe that such questions are foolish, or do they occasionally trouble you?

Two Types of Divine Will: Will of Choice and Will of Compulsion
Sanusi presents and critiques two proposed divisions of divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why dividing divine will into choice and compulsion (or legislative and predestining) does not fully resolve the theological problem of will and action.

Core Idea
Proposed divisions of will fail to solve the real theological difficulty.

This marginal note tackles a persistent puzzle: how can God will everything yet humans be responsible? Some scholars proposed dividing divine will into two types to ease the tension. The note presents these proposals and their refutations, showing that the problem is not so easily dissolved. Let's follow the argument carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the structure: a problem is stated, a solution proposed, then refuted, then another proposal and its verdict.
The passage uses technical terms like 'irāda ikhtiyāriyya and irāda qasriyya.
2
Identify the two divisions of will
There are two separate divisions mentioned. First, will of choice vs. will of compulsion. Second, legislative will vs. predestining will. Mark how each is defined.
The second division is introduced with 'wa ammā qawl baʿḍihim'.
3
Examine the refutation of the first division
The note says the first division was refuted. Why? Because if God's will fails in either case, it implies inability. Understand the logic: even in the 'will of choice' scenario, the failure is still a defect.
The refutation uses the principle that any failure of divine will entails incapacity.
4
Evaluate the second division's conclusion
The note says the second division does not remove the dispute either, though it reconciles apparent contradictions in scriptures. Reflect on why it fails to solve the core issue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حاشيته » ( ق ١٥٣ ) : ( وذكر بعضهم ما يرفع الإشكال والخلاف ؛ فقال : الإرادة نوعان : إرادة اختيار ؛ بمعنى : أنه تعالى أراد من العباد الإيمان والطاعة برغبتهم واختيارهم ، وإرادة قسر وإلجاء ؛ بمعنى : أنه ألجأهم إلى الفعل وقسرهم عليه ، ويستحيل تخلف المراد عن الثانية ؛ لما يلزم من تخلفه العجز ، لا عن الأولى ؛ لعدم استلزامه لذلك ؛ لأنه لو شاء لألجأهم وقسرهم على مراده ، ورُدَّ : بأنه يكفي في لزوم العجز تخلف مراد الله تعالى في ذلك وأما قول بعضهم : الإرادة قسمان : إرادة أمر وتشريع ، وإرادة قضاء وتقدير ؛ فالأولى تُسمَّى الإرادة الشرعية ؛ تتعلق بالطاعة لا بالمعصية ، والثانية الإرادة التقديرية ؛ شاملة لجميع الكائنات . . فلا يرفع الخلاف
نعم ؛ يرفع التعارض بين الآيات والأحاديث بحسب الظاهر )
His marginal note: Some mentioned what removes the problem and dispute; they said: Will is of two types: will of choice, meaning that He willed from the servants faith and obedience willingly and by their choice; and will of compulsion and coercion, meaning that He compelled them to the action and coerced them upon it. It is impossible that the object of will fails from the second, because the failure would entail inability, not from the first, because it does not entail that; for if He willed, He would compel them and coerce them to His will. And it was refuted: that it is sufficient for the necessity of inability that the object of God's will fails in that. As for the saying of some: Will is of two types: will of command and legislation, and will of decree and predestination; the first is called the legislative will; it pertains to obedience not disobedience; the second is the predestining will; it encompasses all beings... it does not remove the dispute. Yes; it removes the apparent contradiction between verses and hadith.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is a marginal note (ḥāshiya) that presents and discusses a proposed solution to a theological problem regarding divine will and human action. The note begins by mentioning that some scholars sought to remove the difficulty by dividing will (irāda) into two kinds: (1) will of choice (irādat ikhtiyār) — by which God wills that people perform faith and obedience of their own free choice, and (2) will of compulsion (irādat qasr) — by which God compels people to actions. They claim that failure of the object of will (takhalluf al-murād) is impossible in the second kind (for that would imply inability), but possible in the first kind because God could still compel if He wished. This view is then refuted: the opponent argues that even in the case of the first kind, if God's will fails, that is still a defect. Then another division is presented: will of command and legislation (irādat amr wa tashrīʿ) versus will of decree and predestination (irādat qaḍāʾ wa taqdīr). The former is called legislative will and pertains only to obedience; the latter is predestining will and covers all events. The note concludes that this division does not resolve the theological dispute either, though it does reconcile apparent contradictions in scriptural texts.

Key Terms
الإرادة الاختيارية (al-irāda al-ikhtiyāriyya)
الإرادة القسرية (al-irāda al-qasriyya)
تخلف المراد (takhalluf al-murād)
العجز (al-ʿajz)
الإرادة الشرعية (al-irāda al-sharʿiyya)
الإرادة التقديرية (al-irāda al-taqdīriyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That dividing divine will into two types solves the problem of human free will and divine predestination.
Correction: The passage shows that both proposed divisions are insufficient: the first is refuted logically, and the second only reconciles apparent scriptural contradictions without resolving the theological problem.

Checkpoint
According to the marginal note, what is the consequence of the object of will failing from the will of compulsion?
A) It shows God's mercy
B) It entails inability (ʿajz)
C) It is impossible
D) It proves free will

Key Takeaway
Proposed divisions of divine will do not resolve the core theological problem; they only address apparent contradictions in scripture.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you genuinely feel you chose it, or do you find yourself thinking 'it was written'? How does Sanusi's critique of the will of choice challenge you to take full responsibility for your actions without hiding behind qadar?
?
You just learned that proposed divisions of God's will do not resolve the tension between divine decree and human accountability. Does this unsettle you? Are you willing to live with that intellectual discomfort, or do you seek a system that makes you feel in control?
?
The passage says the second division only reconciles apparent scriptural contradictions but doesn't remove the dispute. When you read verses that seem to contradict—like those on human choice and divine predestination—do you rush to harmonize them, or do you allow the mystery to deepen your awe of Allah's wisdom?

Refutation of Ibn Hazm's Claim that God Can Take a Son
Sanusi refutes Ibn Hazm's claim about God's power to take a son.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'ari position holds that God's power does not relate to intrinsically impossible things, and why this is not a limitation of His power.

Core Idea
Divine power does not extend to the impossible; that is not a deficiency but a logical necessity.

In this passage, Sanusi addresses a controversial claim by Ibn Hazm that God must be capable of taking a son, otherwise He would be weak. Sanusi points out a critical distinction: inability ('ajz) only occurs when the power itself is deficient, not when the object of power is impossible. This distinction is vital for understanding divine omnipotence within the Ash'ari framework.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then read the literal translation. Pay attention to Sanusi's tone and the structure of his argument.
Notice how he first condemns the 'corrupt estimation' before quoting Ibn Hazm.
2
Identify Ibn Hazm's claim and Sanusi's refutation
Find the exact words of Ibn Hazm quoted by Sanusi. Then identify the key distinction Sanusi makes between inability and non-relation of power.
Sanusi calls Ibn Hazm an 'innovator' and points out his logical error.
3
Reflect on the concept of 'non-relation of power' versus 'inability'
Think about why Ash'aris consider it crucial that God's power does not relate to impossible things. How does this preserve both divine omnipotence and logical consistency?
The story of Idris illustrates how a misunderstanding can lead to such errors.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مِنَ الحوادثِ ، وبسلبها عمَّنْ تجبُ لهُ ؛ وهو مولانا جل وعزَّ ! وأيُّ نقص وفساد أعظمُ مِنْ هذا ؟!
وبالجملة : فذلك التقديرُ الفاسد يؤدّي إلى تخليط عظيم لا يبقى معَهُ شيءٌ مِنَ الإيمانِ ، ولا شيءٌ مِنَ المعقولات أصلاً
ولخفاء هذا المعنى على بعض الأغبياء مِنَ المبتدعةِ صرَّحَ بنقيض ذلكَ ، فنَقَلَ عنِ ابنِ حزمٍ أَنَّهُ قالَ في « الملل والنحل » : ( إِنَّهُ تعالى قادر أن يتخذ ولداً ؛ إذْ لو لم يَقدِرْ لكانَ عاجزاً ) (١) ، فانظر اختلال عقل هذا المبتدع كيفَ غفلَ عمَّا يلزمُهُ على هذه المقالة الشنيعةِ مِنَ اللوازم التي لا تدخل تحتَ وَهُم ، وكيفَ فاتَهُ أَنَّ العجزَ إِنَّما يكونُ لو كانَ القصورُ جَاءَ مِنْ ناحية القدرةِ ، أما لو كانَ لعدم تعلُّق القدرة فلا يَتوهَّمُ عاقل أنَّ هذا عجز
وذكر الأستاذ أبو إسحاق الإسفراينيُّ : أَنَّ أَوَّلَ مَنْ أخذَ منه هذا المبتدعُ وأشياعُهُ ذلكَ بحَسَبِ فهمهمُ الركيكِ .. قصَّةُ إدريس عليهِ السلام (٢) ؛ حيثُ جَاءَهُ إبليس لعنهُ اللهُ تعالى في صورة إنسان وهو
(١) انظر ( الفصل في الملل والأهواء والنحل ) ( ٢/ ١٣٨ ) وعبارته : ( من سأل : هل الله تعالى قادر على أن يتخذ ولداً ؟ فالجواب : أنه تعالى قادر على ذلك ) ، وقال أيضاً : ( من قال : لا يوصف تعالى بالقدرة على المحال . . فقد جعل قدرته سبحانه وتعالى متناهية، وجعل قوته عز وجل منقطعة محدودة ) ، إلى غير ذلك مما يعجب منه امرؤ أن يفوه به مثل هذا العالم الكبير القدر
(٢) قوله : ( مَنْ ) مستعملة هنا فيما لا يعقل ، والتقدير : أول كلام أخذ منه هذا
From the accidents, and stripping them from whom it is necessary, and He is our Master, Glorified and Exalted! And what greater deficiency and corruption than this?! In summary: that corrupt estimation leads to great confusion with which nothing of faith remains, nor anything of rational principles at all. And because this meaning was hidden from some ignorant innovators, they explicitly stated the opposite of that. So he narrated from Ibn Hazm that he said in al-Milal wa al-Nihal: 'Indeed, God is capable of taking a son; because if He were not capable, He would be unable.' So look at the derangement of the mind of this innovator, how he was heedless of the implications that follow from this ugly statement, implications that are beyond imagination. How did it escape him that inability only occurs if deficiency comes from the side of power, but if it is due to the non-relation of power, no rational person would imagine that this is inability. And the master Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini mentioned that the first thing this innovator and his followers took from him, according to their weak understanding, was the story of Idris, peace be upon him, when Iblis came to him in the form of a human being and... (1) See al-Fasl fi al-Milal wa al-Ahwa' wa al-Nihal (2/138) and his statement: 'Whoever asks: Is God capable of taking a son? The answer: He is capable of that.' He also said: 'Whoever says that God should not be described as capable of the impossible... has made His power finite and limited.'... to such an extent that one wonders how such a great scholar could utter this. (2) His saying: 'man' is used here for non-rational beings, and the estimation: the first speech taken from him...
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a specific claim attributed to Ibn Hazm. It begins by decrying the consequences of the 'corrupt estimation' (al-taqdīr al-fāsid), which leads to total confusion and loss of faith. The author then states that some ignorant innovators, because they did not understand the previous point (that power does not relate to the impossible), openly asserted the opposite. He quotes Ibn Hazm's statement from his book al-Milal wa al-Nihal: 'God is capable of taking a son; because if He were not capable, He would be unable.' The author condemns this as a foolish statement, pointing out that inability (ʿajz) only arises when the deficiency lies in the power itself, not when the lack of relation is due to the object being impossible. No rational person would consider that as inability. Then he relates a remark from Abū Isḥāq al-Isfarāyīnī that the origin of this error among the innovator and his followers lies in their misunderstanding of the story of Prophet Idrīs and Iblīs. The footnotes provide the exact text from Ibn Hazm and explain that his view was that God can do even what is considered impossible, otherwise His power would be finite. The author thus refutes Ibn Hazm's position, defending the Ashʿarī principle that divine power only relates to possible things.

Key Terms
التقدير الفاسد (al-taqdīr al-fāsid)
العجز (al-ʿajz)
القدرة (al-qudra)
عدم التعلق (ʿadam al-taʿalluq)
ابن حزم (Ibn Ḥazm)
الملل والنحل (al-Milal wa al-Niḥal)
قصة إدريس (Qiṣṣat Idrīs)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because God does not want a son
B) Because inability is only when the deficiency is in the power itself, not when the object is impossible
C) Because Ibn Hazm misunderstood scripture
D) Because God can take a son but chooses not to

Key Takeaway
Ibn Hazm wrongly assumed that God's inability to do the impossible would be a deficiency, but Sanusi clarifies that inability pertains to power itself, not to the nature of the object.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Refuting the Claim that God Can Do the Impossible
Sanusi refutes the innovator's claim that God is capable of taking a son.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the critical distinction between divine incapacity (ʿajz) and the non-attachment of divine power to impossible objects, and why denying the latter does not imply a limit on God's power.

Core Idea
Denying God's power to do the impossible is not incapacity—it is rational necessity.

This passage from Sanusi tackles a controversial claim made by some innovators, specifically Ibn Hazm, that God must be able to take a son, otherwise He would be incapable. Sanusi exposes the logical flaw in this argument by clarifying what true incapacity means and why impossible objects fall outside the scope of divine power without implying any deficiency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic text and its literal translation to get the gist of Sanusi's argument.
Notice the strong language: 'corrupt estimation', 'mental disorder'.
2
Identify the core distinction
Focus on the key distinction between 'incapacity due to power deficiency' and 'non-attachment of power to impossible objects'.
This distinction is the heart of the refutation.
3
Trace the logical consequences
Understand why the innovator's position leads to absurd implications that destroy both faith and reason.
Sanusi says it leads to 'great confusion'.
4
Reflect on the broader theological principle
Consider how this argument upholds the orthodox view that God's power is perfect while safeguarding rational coherence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة : فذلك التقديرُ الفاسد يؤدّي إلى تخليط عظيم لا يبقى معَهُ شيءٌ مِنَ الإيمانِ ، ولا شيءٌ مِنَ المعقولات أصلاً ولخفاء هذا المعنى على بعض الأغبياء مِنَ المبتدعةِ صرَّحَ بنقيض ذلكَ ، فنَقَلَ عنِ ابنِ حزمٍ أَنَّهُ قالَ في « الملل والنحل » : ( إِنَّهُ تعالى قادر أن يتخذ ولداً ؛ إذْ لو لم يَقدِرْ لكانَ عاجزاً ) ، فانظر اختلال عقل هذا المبتدع كيفَ غفلَ عمَّا يلزمُهُ على هذه المقالة الشنيعةِ مِنَ اللوازم التي لا تدخل تحتَ وَهُم ، وكيفَ فاتَهُ أَنَّ العجزَ إِنَّما يكونُ لو كانَ القصورُ جَاءَ مِنْ ناحية القدرةِ ، أما لو كانَ لعدم تعلُّق القدرة فلا يَتوهَّمُ عاقل أنَّ هذا عجز
In short: that corrupt estimation leads to great confusion such that nothing of faith remains with it, nor anything of rational matters at all. And due to the concealment of this meaning from some of the ignoramuses among the innovators, he declared the opposite of that, and reported from Ibn Hazm that he said in 'al-Milal wa al-Nihal': 'Indeed, He, exalted, is capable of taking a son; since if He were not capable, He would be incapable.' So look at the mental disorder of this innovator: how he was heedless of the implications that follow from this odious statement, implications that cannot be imagined, and how it escaped him that incapacity only occurs if the deficiency came from the side of power; as for if it were due to the non-attachment of power, no rational person would imagine that this is incapacity.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a deviant claim. The author begins by stating that the corrupt estimation (i.e., the view that God is capable of impossible things like taking a son) leads to a great confusion that destroys both faith and rational thought. Then he points out that due to ignorance of this point, some innovators explicitly stated the opposite, quoting Ibn Hazm's statement that God is capable of taking a son, arguing that otherwise He would be incapable. The author then rebukes this innovator for failing to see the absurd consequences. He explains the key distinction: incapacity (al-ʿajz) only arises when the failure comes from the power itself—i.e., the power is insufficient. But if the failure is due to the impossibility of the object (i.e., the thing to be affected is inherently impossible for the divine power to attach to, like a son), then this is not incapacity. Thus, denying capability for impossible things does not imply incapacity.

Key Terms
تقدير فاسد
مبتدع
العجز
تعلق القدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God cannot do something (like take a son), then He must be incapable (ʿajiz).
Correction: The text clarifies that incapacity only arises when the deficiency is in the power itself. If the object is impossible in itself, the non-attachment of power does not indicate weakness.

Checkpoint
According to the text, when would the claim that God is not capable of something actually be considered incapacity?
A) When the object is impossible in itself.
B) When the failure comes from the side of power itself.
C) When the object is something that does not exist.
D) When the object is a created thing.

Key Takeaway
Denying that God can do the impossible does not limit His power; it only recognizes that power does not attach to inherently impossible objects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Sanusi calls the innovator 'ignorant' and 'mentally disordered' for his claim. Have you ever defended a belief about God that, if pressed, leads to logical absurdity? Are you willing to submit your understanding to rational scrutiny?
?
This passage distinguishes between incapacity and non-attachment. In your own life, do you sometimes confuse what you cannot do (due to genuine limitation) with what is simply not meant to be? How does this distinction affect how you view God's will and your own limitations?

The Story of Idris and Iblis: A Model Answer on Divine Power
Al-Isfarayini recounts the story of Idris and Iblis to illustrate a correct response about divine power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the story of Idris and Iblis illustrates a key principle in Islamic theology about divine power and logical impossibility.

Core Idea
God can do all things, but logical contradictions remain impossible.

This lesson presents a famous story from the Islamic tradition that demonstrates how to properly respond to questions about God's power. The story of Prophet Idris and Iblis is used by al-Ash'ari to clarify that divine omnipotence does not extend to logical impossibilities like interpenetration of bodies.

Guided Path
1
Read the story of Idris and Iblis
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Notice how Iblis poses a tricky question about God's power and how Idris responds.
The story is not from the Prophet Muhammad but is widely accepted.
2
Understand the two interpretations
Al-Ash'ari clarifies that Idris's answer has two possible meanings. Identify each interpretation and why one is impossible and the other is possible.
The key is the distinction between logical impossibility (interpenetration) and divine power to change sizes.
3
Apply the principle to other questions
Consider how this principle can be applied to other questions about God's power, such as creating a stone too heavy to lift.
This demonstrates the importance of precise wording in theological questions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذكر الأستاذ أبو إسحاق الإسفراينيُّ : أَنَّ أَوَّلَ مَنْ أَخذَ منه هذا المبتدعُ وأشياعُهُ ذلكَ بحَسَبِ فهمهمُ الركيكِ .. قصَّةُ إدريس عليهِ السلام ؛ حيثُ جَاءَهُ إبليس لعنهُ اللهُ تعالى في صورة إنسان وهو يخيط ويقولُ في كل دخلة الإبرة وخرجتِها : سبحانَ اللهِ والحمد للهِ ، فجاءَهُ بقشرة بيضةٍ  وَقالَ لَهُ : الله تعالى يقدرُ أنْ يجعل الدنيا في هذهِ القشرة ؟ فقالَ لهُ في جوابِهِ : الله تعالى قادر أن يجعل الدنيا في سَمٌ هذه الإبرة ، ونخسَ إحدى عينيهِ ، فصارَ أعور ، قال : وهذا وإن لم يُرْوَ عن رسولِ اللهِ صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ فقد ظهر وانتشر ظهوراً لا يُرَدُّ قال : وقد أخذ الأشعري مِنْ جوابِ إدريس عليه الصلاة والسلامُ أجوبةً في مسائل كثيرةٍ مِنْ هذا الجنس ، وأوضح هذا الجواب فقالَ : إِنْ أراد السائلُ أنَّ الدنيا على ما هي عليه والقشرة على ما هي عليه.. فلم يقل ما يُعقَلُ ؛ فإنَّ الأجسام الكثيرة يستحيلُ أنْ تتداخل وتكونَ في حيّز واحدٍ ، وإنْ أرادَ أَنَّهُ يصغُرُ الدنيا قدْرَ القشرة ويجعلُها فيها ، أو يكبِّرُ القشرة قدْرَ الدنيا ويجعل الدنيا فيها . . فلعمري ؛ الله تعالى قادر على ذلك وعلى أكثر منه
And the master Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini mentioned: that the first thing this innovator and his followers took from [that story] according to their weak understanding was the story of Idris, peace be upon him; when Iblis, may Allah curse him, came to him in the form of a man while he was sewing and saying with every insertion and exit of the needle: 'Glory be to Allah and praise be to Allah.' Then he came to him with an eggshell and said to him: 'Is Allah, exalted, capable of putting the world in this eggshell?' He replied in his answer: 'Allah is capable of putting the world in the eye of this needle,' and he poked one of his eyes, so he became one-eyed. He said: And even if this is not narrated from the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, it has become apparent and widespread in a way that cannot be rejected. He said: And al-Ash'ari took from the answer of Idris, peace and blessings be upon him, answers in many issues of this kind, and he clarified this answer, saying: 'If the questioner meant that the world is as it is and the eggshell is as it is... then he has not said something intelligible; for many bodies are impossible to interpenetrate and be in one space. But if he meant that He makes the world the size of the eggshell and puts it in it, or makes the eggshell the size of the world and puts the world in it... by my life, Allah is capable of that and more.'
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage is a narrative and analysis of a story used to counter the innovator's misunderstanding. Al-Isfarayini states that the source of the innovator's confusion was the story of the Prophet Idris with Iblis. In the story, Iblis came to Idris in human form while Idris was sewing and glorifying Allah. Iblis brought an eggshell and asked whether Allah can put the world inside it. Idris responded by saying Allah can put the world in the eye of a needle, and then poked Iblis's eye, causing him to become one-eyed. Al-Isfarayini notes that though this story is not from the Prophet Muhammad, it is widely known. Then he adds that al-Ash'ari derived from Idris's answer a method for handling similar questions. Al-Ash'ari clarified that there are two interpretations: if the question means placing the world as it is into the eggshell as it is, that is impossible because objects cannot interpenetrate. But if it means altering the sizes, then Allah is indeed capable of that. Thus, the correct answer distinguishes between impossible due to logical contradiction (interpenetration of bodies) and possible by divine power if dimensions are changed.

Key Terms
إدريس
إبليس
تداخل الأجسام
استحالة عقلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that Idris's answer implies God can do contradictory things like placing an object inside itself without change.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari's clarification shows that Idris's answer only applies when size alteration is intended; logical impossibilities remain impossible.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ash'ari's clarification, what is the correct understanding of Idris's answer to Iblis?
A) Idris conceded that God can place the world inside an eggshell as it is.
B) Idris meant that God can change the sizes of the world and the eggshell to make it possible.
C) Idris's answer was a joke and not to be taken seriously.
D) Idris was admitting that God cannot do such a thing.

Key Takeaway
When discussing divine power, we must distinguish between what is logically impossible and what is merely extraordinary; God's omnipotence does not include contradictions.

God's Power and Rational Impossibility
Sanusi relates a story of Idris and explains al-Ash'ari's response on divine power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between what is rationally impossible and what is merely extraordinary, and why God's power does not extend to logical contradictions like the interpenetration of bodies.

Core Idea
God can do all that is possible, but not the rationally impossible.

This lesson presents a story from the tradition about the prophet Idris and a clever response to a question about God's power. Al-Ash'ari later used this response to articulate a key principle: God's omnipotence is limited by rational possibility. The passage draws a sharp line between what is logically impossible (like two bodies in one space) and what is merely extraordinary (like shrinking the world).

Guided Path
1
Read the story carefully
Read the narrative of Idris and the questioner with the eggshell. Notice the dramatic response: Idris pricks the questioner's eye. This is not a proof but a background story.
The story is widely transmitted even if not from the Prophet.
2
Identify al-Ash'ari's distinction
Al-Ash'ari extracts a theological principle from Idris's answer. He interprets the question in two ways. Find both interpretations and note how each leads to a different conclusion about God's power.
3
Grasp the theological principle
Understand that rational impossibility (like two bodies in one space) is a real boundary. God's power does not extend to what is intrinsically impossible. This is a key concept in kalam.
This principle guards against thinking God can do literally anything, including logical contradictions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يخيط ويقولُ في كل دخلة الإبرة وخرجتِها : سبحانَ اللهِ والحمد للهِ ، فجاءَهُ بقشرة بيضةٍ  َوقالَ لَهُ : الله تعالى يقدرُ أنْ يجعل الدنيا في هذهِ القشرة ؟ فقالَ لهُ في جوابِهِ : الله تعالى قادر أن يجعل الدنيا في سَمٌ هذه الإبرة ، ونخسَ إحدى عينيهِ ، فصارَ أعور ، قال : وهذا وإن لم يُرْوَ عن رسولِ اللهِ صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ فقد ظهر وانتشر ظهوراً لا يُرَدُّ قال (١) : وقد أخذ الأشعري مِنْ جوابِ إدريس عليه الصلاة والسلامُ أجوبةً في مسائل كثيرةٍ مِنْ هذا الجنس ، وأوضح هذا الجواب فقالَ : إِنْ أراد السائلُ أنَّ الدنيا على ما هي عليه والقشرة على ما هي عليه.. فلم يقل ما يُعقَلُ ؛ فإنَّ الأجسام الكثيرة يستحيلُ أنْ تتداخل وتكونَ في حيّز واحدٍ ، وإنْ أرادَ أَنَّهُ يصغُرُ الدنيا قدْرَ القشرة ويجعلُها فيها ، أو يكبِّرُ القشرة قدْرَ الدنيا ويجعل الدنيا فيها . . فلعمري ؛ الله تعالى قادر على ذلك وعلى أكثر منه
He was sewing and saying at every entry of the needle and its exit: 'Glory be to Allah and praise be to Allah.' Then someone came to him with an eggshell and said to him: 'Is Allah Almighty able to put the world into this shell?' He replied to him: 'Allah Almighty is able to put the world into the eye of this needle,' and he pricked one of his eyes, so he became one-eyed. He said: 'And even if this is not narrated from the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him), it has appeared and spread in a way that cannot be rejected.' He said (1): 'And al-Ash'ari derived from the answer of Idris (peace be upon him) answers to many questions of this kind, and he clarified this answer, saying: If the questioner meant that the world is as it is and the shell is as it is... then he did not say something reasonable; for it is impossible for many bodies to interpenetrate and be in a single space. And if he meant that He makes the world as small as the shell and places it in it, or makes the shell as large as the world and places the world in it... then by my life, Allah Almighty is able to do that and more.'
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage presents a narrative and a theological analysis. It begins with a story about a person (likely Idris, peace be upon him) who was sewing and constantly glorifying God. A questioner came with an eggshell and asked whether God could fit the entire world into that shell. Idris responded by saying God could fit it into the eye of a needle, and then pricked the questioner's eye, causing him to become one-eyed. The author notes that this story, though not from the Prophet, is widely transmitted and cannot be rejected. The passage then quotes al-Ash'ari, who used the answer of Idris to address similar questions. Al-Ash'ari clarifies the answer: if the questioner meant keeping both the world and the shell in their actual sizes, then the request is unreasonable because it implies interpenetration of bodies (tadakhul al-ajsam), which is impossible. But if the questioner meant that God could shrink the world or enlarge the shell, then God is indeed able to do that. This establishes a key distinction: rational impossibility (like two bodies in one space) is not within God's power, but altering sizes is.

Key Terms
قدرة
تداخل الأجسام
مستحيل عقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God can do absolutely anything, including logical contradictions like two bodies in one space.
Correction: The passage shows that rational impossibilities (like interpenetration of bodies) are not within God's power. God's power is limited to what is possible, not what is logically absurd.

Key Takeaway
God's power encompasses all possible things, but rational impossibilities like two bodies in one space are not within His power.

Divine Knowledge Encompasses All Things Eternally
Sanusi defines divine knowledge and its scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of divine knowledge as an all-encompassing, infallible attribute that immediately discloses all necessary, possible, and impossible things.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is immediate, eternal, and absolutely certain, admitting no error.

This passage establishes the nature of divine knowledge (العلم) as one of God's essential attributes. Sanusi defines it as an attribute that discloses everything without possibility of error, and he clarifies that it relates to all three categories of things: necessary, possible, and impossible. Understanding this definition is foundational for Islamic theology, as it distinguishes God's knowledge from human knowledge, which is acquired and fallible.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get a sense of its rhythm and key terms. Then read the translation to grasp the literal meaning.
Pay attention to the terms الواجبات, الجائزات, المستحيلات.
2
Identify the definition of knowledge
Find the sentence that defines knowledge (العلم). Notice the phrase 'انكشاف لا يحتمل النقيض' – what does it imply about certainty?
3
Understand the scope of divine knowledge
The passage states that knowledge relates to all necessary, possible, and impossible things. Reflect on why impossible things are included – it shows God's knowledge is complete.
Impossible things are known as impossible.
4
Notice the attributes of eternity and immediacy
The commentary highlights that divine knowledge is eternal (أزلاً وأبداً) and without reflection or inference. How does this differ from human knowledge?
This underscores God's perfection.
5
Test your understanding with the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to confirm you grasp the key point about infallibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالْعِلْمُ الْمُتَعَلِّقُ بِجَمِيعِ الْوَاجِبَاتِ وَالْجَائِزَاتِ وَالْمُسْتَحِيلات . الْعِلْمُ : هُوَ صِفَةٌ يَنْكَشِفُ بِهَا مَا تَتَعَلَّقُ بِهِ انْكِشَافاً لَا يَحْتَمِلُ النَّقِيضَ بِوَجْهٍ مِّنَ الْوُجُوهِ . فَمَعْنَى قَوْلِنَا : ( الْمُتَعَلِّقُ بِجَمِيعِ الْوَاجِبَاتِ . . . ) إِلَى آخِرِهِ : أَنَّ جَمِيعَ هٰذِهِ الْأُمُورِ مُنْكَشِفَةٌ لِعِلْمِهِ تَعَالَىٰ ، مُتَّضِحَةٌ لَهُ أَزَلاً وَأَبَداً بِلَا تَأَمُّلٍ وَلَا اسْتِدْلَالٍ اتِّضَاحاً لَا يُمْكِنُ أَنْ يَكُونَ فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ عَلَىٰ خِلَافِ مَا عَلِمَهُ جَلَّ وَعَزَّ .
And knowledge is related to all necessary things, possible things, and impossible things. Knowledge is an attribute by which what it is related to is disclosed in a disclosure that admits of no opposite in any way. So the meaning of our saying: (related to all necessary things ...) to its end: that all these things are disclosed to His knowledge, may He be exalted, and are evident to Him eternally and everlastingly, without reflection and without inference, in an evidentness that cannot in reality be contrary to what He, may He be glorified and exalted, knows.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage defines divine knowledge (العلم) as a divine attribute. The author first states that this attribute relates to all three categories of things: necessary (الواجبات), possible (الجائزات), and impossible (المستحيلات). Then he gives the definition: knowledge is an attribute by which what it relates to is disclosed (ينكشف) in a manner that does not admit the opposite (لا يحتمل النقيض) — meaning it is certain and infallible. He then explains what 'being related to all things' means: all these things are disclosed and evident to God's knowledge, eternally (أزلاً) and everlastingly (أبداً), without any need for reflection (تأمل) or inference (استدلال), and in such a way that it is impossible for the actual state of affairs to contradict what He knows. This emphasizes that divine knowledge is immediate, all-encompassing, and necessarily corresponds to reality.

Key Terms
العلم
الواجبات
الجائزات
المستحيلات
انكشاف لا يحتمل النقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think divine knowledge is like human knowledge that requires deliberation and can be mistaken.
Correction: The passage clarifies that divine knowledge is immediate, eternal, and infallible – it does not admit the opposite and does not require reflection or inference.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does it mean that divine knowledge 'does not admit the opposite'?
A) It means God's knowledge is incomplete.
B) It means God's knowledge is certain and cannot be wrong.
C) It means God only knows necessary things.
D) It means God's knowledge changes over time.

Key Takeaway
Divine knowledge is an eternal, immediate, and infallible attribute that discloses all necessary, possible, and impossible things as they truly are.

?
The passage says that divine knowledge does not admit its opposite. When you doubt God’s promises or fear that your sins are unforgivable, do you realize you are denying the certainty of God's knowledge, which holds no opposite?

The Uniqueness of Divine Life Among Attributes
Sanusi defines divine life and explains why it alone among attributes does not require a relatum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why life is unique among the divine attributes in not requiring an external relation, and how this distinguishes it from attributes like knowledge, power, and will.

Core Idea
Life is the only divine attribute that does not point beyond itself to an object.

Sanusi now turns to the divine attribute of life. After stating that life is not related to anything, he defines it and then explains what this non-relational nature means. The key contrast is with other 'attributes of meanings' which necessarily seek an object beyond the essence. This passage establishes a foundational distinction in Sunni theology regarding the nature of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening statement and definition
Notice that Sanusi begins with a bold claim: life is not related to anything. Then he defines life as enabling perception.
2
Understand the meaning of 'not related'
He explains that 'not related' means life does not require an extra thing beyond subsistence.
3
Contrast with other attributes
He gives examples: knowledge requires a known, power requires a powerful, will requires a willed. This shows they are 'related'.
4
Grasp the generalization
All attributes of meanings are essentially related except life. This relation is intrinsic to them.
This distinction is crucial for later discussions on divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالْحَيَاةُ ، وَهِيَ لَا تَتَعَلَّقُ بِشَيْءٍ . الْحَيَاةُ : هِيَ صِفَةٌ تَصِحُّ لِمَنْ قَامَتْ بِهِ أَنْ يَتَّصِفَ بِالْإِدْرَاكِ . وَمَعْنَىٰ كَوْنِهَا لَا تَتَعَلَّقُ بِشَيْءٍ : أَنَّهَا لَا تَقْتَضِي أَمْراً زَائِداً عَلَى الْقِيَامِ بِمَحَلِّهَا ، وَالصِّفَةُ الْمُتَعَلِّقَةُ : هِيَ الَّتِي تَقْتَضِي أَمْراً زَائِداً عَلَىٰ ذٰلِكَ ، أَلَا تَرَىٰ أَنَّ الْعِلْمَ بَعْدَ قِيَامِهِ بِمَحَلِّهِ يَطْلُبُ أَمْراً يُعْلَمُ بِهِ ، وَكَذٰلِكَ الْقُدْرَةُ وَالْإِرَادَةُ وَنَحْوُهُمَا ؟! . وَبِالْجُمْلَةِ : فَجَمِيعُ صِفَاتِ الْمَعَانِي مُتَعَلِّقَةٌ — أَيْ : طَالِبَةٌ — لِزَائِدٍ عَلَى الْقِيَامِ بِمَحَلِّهَا سِوَى الْحَيَاةِ ، وَهٰذَا التَّعَلُّقُ نَفْسِيٌّ لِتِلْكَ الصِّفَاتِ ، كَمَا أَنَّ قِيَامَهَا بِالذَّاتِ نَفْسِيٌّ لَهَا أَيْضاً .
And life, and it is not related to anything. Life is an attribute that renders correct for the one in whom it subsists to be described with perception. And the meaning of its not being related to anything: that it does not require an extra thing beyond subsisting in its locus. And the related attribute: it is that which requires an extra thing beyond that. Do you not see that knowledge, after its subsistence in its locus, seeks a thing to be known by it, and likewise power and will and the like? In sum, all the attributes of meanings are related — i.e., seeking — an extra beyond subsistence in their locus except life, and this relation is essential (nafsī) for those attributes, just as their subsistence in the essence is also essential for them.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage defines divine life (الحياة) and contrasts it with other divine attributes. The author begins by stating that life is not related to anything (لا تتعلق بشيء). Then he gives the definition: life is an attribute that renders the one in whom it subsists capable of being described with perception (الإدراك). The meaning of 'not related' is that life does not require an extra entity beyond its subsisting in the essence (محل), unlike other attributes such as knowledge, power, and will. Those attributes, after they subsist in the essence, require an object (معلوم, مقدور, مراد) beyond the essence. The author then generalizes: all the 'attributes of meanings' (صفات المعاني) are related — i.e., they seek an extra beyond subsistence — except life. This relation (تعلق) is 'essential' (نفسي) to those attributes, meaning it is intrinsic to their nature, just as their subsistence in the essence is also essential. Thus life stands out as the only attribute that does not entail a relation to something else.

صفات المعاني

Correction: Life is an exception; it does not require an external object.

C) It is not an essential attribute.
D) It is only found in created beings.

Key Takeaway
Life is the only divine attribute that does not require an external object, making it unique among the attributes of meanings.

The Attribute of Life and Its Lack of Relation
Sanusi defines the attribute of life and explains its unique non-relatedness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the attribute of life is unique among the attributes of meanings in having no relation to anything outside itself.

Core Idea
Life is the only attribute that does not require an object beyond its own existence.

Sanusi now turns to the attribute of life, which may seem simple but holds a profound distinction. While other attributes like knowledge and power inherently reach out to something known or powerful, life stands apart. This passage explains what life is and why it has no tā'alluq (relation), marking it as unique among the attributes of meanings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the concise style and the key term 'tā'alluq'.
2
Understand the definition of life
Life is defined as an attribute that qualifies the one in whom it subsists to possess perception (idrāk). Focus on how this definition distinguishes life from other attributes.
3
Grasp the concept of non-relation
Sanusi explains that life does not necessitate any additional matter beyond its subsistence in the essence. Contrast this with related attributes that require an external object.
4
Examine the examples
Notice the examples: knowledge requires an object to be known, power requires an object of power, will requires an object of will. These illustrate the meaning of 'related'.
5
Reflect on the uniqueness of life
Sanusi summarizes that all attributes of meanings are related except life, and this relation is essential (nafsī). Consider why this distinction matters for understanding Allah's attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صفة الحياة
وَالْحَيَاةُ ، وَهِيَ لا تَتَعَلَّقُ بِشَيْءٍ .
الحياةُ : هي صفةٌ تصح لِمَنْ قامَتْ بِهِ أَنْ يَتَصِفَ بالإدراكِ . ومعنى كونها لا تتعلق بشيء : أنَّها لا تقتضي أمراً زائداً على القيام بمحلها ، والصفةُ المتعلقة : هي التي تقتضي أمراً زائداً على ذلك ، ألا ترى أنَّ العلم بعد قيامه بمحله يطلبُ أمراً يُعلَمُ بهِ ، وكذلك القدرة والإرادة ونحوهما ؟!
-
وبالجملة : فجميعُ صفاتِ المعاني متعلّقة ـ أي : طالبة ـ لزائد على القيام بمحلها سوى الحياة ، وهذا التعلُّقُ نفسي لتلك الصفاتِ ، كما أنَّ قيامها بالذاتِ نفسي لها أيضاً
The attribute of life. And life, and it does not relate to anything. Life is an attribute by virtue of which one in whom it subsists is qualified to possess perception. And the meaning of its not relating to anything is that it does not necessitate an additional matter beyond subsistence in its locus. And the related attribute is that which necessitates an additional matter beyond that. Do you not see that knowledge, after its subsistence in its locus, requires an object to be known, and likewise power, will, and the like? In summary: all the attributes of meanings are related – i.e., demanding – an addition beyond subsistence in their locus except life. And this relation is essential (nafsī) for those attributes, just as their subsistence in the essence is also essential for them.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage defines the divine attribute of life and contrasts it with other attributes of meanings. The author begins by stating that life does not relate to anything (لا تتعلق بشيء). He then defines life: it is an attribute by virtue of which the one in whom it subsists is qualified to possess perception (الإدراك). The explanation follows: life's non-relation means it does not require anything beyond its own subsistence in the essence. In contrast, a related attribute (صفة متعلقة) is one that requires something additional. The author gives examples: knowledge requires an object known, power requires an object of power, will requires an object of will. Then he summarizes: all the attributes of meanings (صفات المعاني) are related – they demand an addition beyond subsistence in the locus – except life. He clarifies that this relation is essential (نفسي) for those attributes, just as their subsistence in the essence is essential. Thus life is unique among the attributes of meanings in having no tā'alluq (relation).

The Divine Attributes of Hearing and Sight
Sanusi defines hearing and sight, compares them to knowledge, and explains their universal relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi defines the divine attributes of hearing and sight, their relation to all existents, and how they differ from human hearing and sight in both scope and manner.

Core Idea
Divine hearing and sight encompass all existents, while human ones are limited to specific conditions.

Sanusi now turns to two more essential attributes: hearing (السمع) and sight (البصر). He begins by defining them, comparing them to knowledge, and then carefully distinguishing divine hearing and sight from their human counterparts. This lesson unpacks his precise argument about their universal relation and the critical difference in scope.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without translation, then with the literal translation. Pay attention to how Sanusi defines hearing and sight and what he says about their relation to knowledge.
The passage ends abruptly at 'بكل' due to a page break.
2
Understand the definition
Identify how Sanusi defines hearing and sight as attributes that disclose things, similar to but exceeding knowledge. Note the phrase 'يزيد على الانكشاف بالعلم'.
The disclosure through hearing and sight is greater, not identical.
3
Grasp the relation of objects
Note that the objects of hearing and sight are more specific than knowledge. Everything they relate to is known, but not vice versa.
This shows knowledge is broader.
4
Contrast divine and human
Sanusi explains that the phrase 'to all existents' indicates divine hearing and sight are unlike human ones: human hearing only relates to sounds under certain conditions, human sight only to bodies etc. in specific directions and conditions.
Divine attributes are unrestricted.
5
Reflect on the misconception
Consider the common misunderstanding that divine hearing and sight are like human senses. Sanusi's argument directly refutes anthropomorphism by emphasizing the difference in relation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صفة السمع والبصر و تعلقا تھا
وَالسَّمْعُ وَالْبَصَرُ الْمُتَعَلِّقَانِ بِجَمِيعِ الْمَوْجُودَاتِ
السمع والبصرُ : صفتان ينكشفُ بهما الشيء ويتضحُ كالعلم ، إلا أنَّ الانكشاف بهما يزيد على الانكشاف بالعلم ؛ بمعنى : أنَّه ليس عينه ، وذلك معلوم في الشاهد ضرورةً ، ومتعلقهما أخص مِنْ متعلَّقِ العلم ، فكلُّ ما تعلَّقَ بهِ السمع والبصر تعلَّق بهِ العلم ، ولا ينعكس إلا جزئياً
ونبه بقوله : ( بجميع الموجوداتِ ) على أنَّ سمعَهُ تعالى وبصرَهُ مخالفان لسمعنا وبصرنا في التعلَّقِ ؛ لأنَّ سمعَنا إِنَّما يتعلَّق عادةً ببعض الموجودات ؛ وهي الأصواتُ ، وعلى وجه مخصوص ؛ مِنْ عدمِ البعدِ والقرب جداً، وبصرَنا إِنَّما يتعلَّقُ عادةً ببعض الموجوداتِ ؛ وهي الأجسام وألوانها وأكوانُها ، في جهة مخصوصة وعلى صفة مخصوصة ، وأما سمعُ مولانا جلَّ وعزّ وبصرُهُ : فيتعلقان بكل
The attribute of hearing and sight and their relation. And hearing and sight, the two attributes that relate to all existents. Hearing and sight are two attributes by which a thing is disclosed and becomes clear, like knowledge – except that the disclosure through them exceeds disclosure through knowledge; meaning it is not identical to it. And that is known in the witnessed world by necessity. And their objects of relation are more specific than the object of relation of knowledge, for everything that hearing and sight relate to, knowledge relates to, but the converse is not true except partially. And he indicated by his saying 'to all existents' that His – exalted – hearing and sight differ from our hearing and sight in relation, because our hearing normally relates only to some existents, namely sounds, and in a specific manner – such as not being too distant or too close – and our sight normally relates only to some existents, namely bodies, their colors, and their modes, in a specific direction and on a specific condition. But the hearing and sight of our Master – majestic and exalted – relate to everything.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage defines the divine attributes of hearing (السمع) and sight (البصر) and explains their relation to all existents. The author begins by stating that they relate to all existents. He defines them as two attributes by which a thing is disclosed and becomes clear, similar to knowledge – but with a difference: the disclosure through hearing and sight is greater than that through knowledge; it is not identical. This is known by necessity in the witnessed world (الشاهد). Their objects (متعلقات) are more specific than the object of knowledge, meaning that everything that hearing and sight relate to is also known, but not vice versa, except partially. This means knowledge has a broader scope. Then the author explains the phrase 'to all existents' (بجميع الموجودات) in the text. He points out that divine hearing and sight differ from human ones in their relation: human hearing normally relates only to sounds under specific conditions (not extremely far or near); human sight normally relates only to bodies, colors, and accidents, limited to a specific direction and condition. In contrast, divine hearing and sight relate to all existents without restriction. Thus the attributes are comprehensive and free from human limitations.

البصر
التعلق
الموجودات
الشاهد

Correction: Sanusi explains that divine hearing and sight relate to all existents without restriction, whereas human hearing and sight are limited to specific types of objects under specific conditions.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Divine hearing and sight encompass all existents without any restriction of condition or type, unlike human senses.

God's Hearing and Sight: Definition and Scope
Sanusi defines hearing and sight as divine attributes and contrasts their scope with human senses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi defines God's hearing and sight as attributes that disclose all existents, and how they differ from human hearing and sight in scope and modality.

Core Idea
God's hearing and sight encompass all existents, while human senses are narrowly limited.

Sanusi begins by defining the divine attributes of hearing and sight, comparing them to knowledge but emphasizing their unique disclosure. He then clarifies that these attributes are not like our senses: they relate to every existent, eternal or created, without restriction. This passage establishes the transcendence of God's perception.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to familiarize yourself with the content.
Notice the use of 'talluq' (relation) as a key concept.
2
Identify the definition and relation to knowledge
Find where Sanusi defines hearing and sight and compares them to knowledge. Understand that disclosure through these attributes is not identical to knowledge.
The phrase 'ka al-ilm' indicates similarity but not identity.
3
Analyze the significance of 'bi-jamiʿ al-mawjudat'
Sanusi uses this phrase to indicate that God's hearing and sight differ in scope from human senses. Contrast the limited human scope with the universal divine scope.
Human hearing relates only to sounds under specific conditions; human sight to bodies, colors, etc. God's hearing and sight relate to all existents.
4
Reflect on the eternal and perpetual application
Note that God hears and sees His own essence and attributes in eternity, and all contingent beings in the endless future. This emphasizes the timeless and comprehensive nature of these attributes.
This point underscores divine transcendence and perfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالسَّمْعُ وَالْبَصَرُ الْمُتَعَلِّقَانِ بِجَمِيعِ الْمَوْجُودَاتِ
السمع والبصرُ : صفتان ينكشفُ بهما الشيء ويتضحُ كالعلم ، إلا أنَّ الانكشاف بهما يزيد على الانكشاف بالعلم ؛ بمعنى : أنَّه ليس عينه ، وذلك معلوم في الشاهد ضرورةً ، ومتعلقهما أخص مِنْ متعلَّقِ العلم ، فكلُّ ما تعلَّقَ بهِ السمع والبصر تعلَّق بهِ العلم ، ولا ينعكس إلا جزئياً
ونبه بقوله : ( بجميع الموجوداتِ ) على أنَّ سمعَهُ تعالى وبصرَهُ مخالفان لسمعنا وبصرنا في التعلَّقِ ؛ لأنَّ سمعَنا إِنَّما يتعلَّق عادةً ببعض الموجودات ؛ وهي الأصواتُ ، وعلى وجه مخصوص ؛ مِنْ عدمِ البعدِ والقرب جداً، وبصرَنا إِنَّما يتعلَّقُ عادةً ببعض الموجوداتِ ؛ وهي الأجسام وألوانها وأكوانُها ، في جهة مخصوصة وعلى صفة مخصوصة ، وأما سمعُ مولانا جلَّ وعزّ وبصرُهُ : فيتعلقان بكل موجود ، قديماً كانَ أو حادثاً ، فيسمعُ جلَّ وعزَّ ويرى في أزلِهِ ذاتَهُ العليَّة وجميع صفاتِهِ الوجوديَّةِ ، ويسمع ويرى تبارك وتعالى معَ ذلك فيما لا يزالُ ذواتِ الكائناتِ كلها وجميع صفاتها الوجوديَّة سواء كانَتْ مِنْ قبيل الأصوات أو مِنْ غيرِها ، أجساماً كانَتْ أو ألواناً أو أكواناً أو غيرها
And hearing and sight, which relate to all existents. Hearing and sight are two attributes by which a thing is disclosed and becomes clear, like knowledge, except that disclosure by them exceeds disclosure by knowledge, meaning it is not identical to it. That is known necessarily in the empirical world. Their objects are more specific than the object of knowledge: everything to which hearing and sight relate, knowledge relates to it, but not vice versa except partially. And He indicated by His saying 'to all existents' that His, Exalted, hearing and sight differ from our hearing and sight in relation, because our hearing normally relates to only some existents, namely sounds, and in a specific manner, namely not excessively distant or near, and our sight normally relates to only some existents, namely bodies, their colors, and their spatial qualities, in a specific direction and in a specific condition. As for the hearing and sight of our Master, Glorious and Exalted, they relate to every existent, whether eternal or originated. So He, Glorious and Exalted, hears and sees in His eternity His own exalted essence and all His existential attributes. And He, Blessed and Exalted, along with that, hears and sees in the endless future the essences of all contingent beings and all their existential attributes, whether they are of the category of sounds or not, whether bodies, colors, spatial qualities, or other things.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage defines the attributes of hearing (al-sam‘) and sight (al-baṣar) as divine attributes. It begins by stating that these are attributes that disclose things and make them clear, similar to knowledge (al-‘ilm), but the disclosure through them exceeds disclosure through knowledge in the sense that it is not identical to knowledge. This distinction is known necessarily (ḍarūratan) from empirical experience. The objects of hearing and sight are more specific than the object of knowledge: whatever hearing and sight relate to, knowledge also relates to, but not conversely except partially. Then Sanusi explains that the phrase 'to all existents' in the definition indicates that God's hearing and sight differ from human hearing and sight in scope. Human hearing normally relates only to certain existents (sounds) and under specific conditions (not too far or too near). Human sight normally relates only to bodies, colors, and spatial qualities (akwān) in a specific direction and condition. In contrast, God's hearing and sight relate to every existent, whether eternal (qadīm) or originated (ḥādith). In His eternity (azal), He hears and sees His own essence and all His existential attributes. In the endless future (mā lā yazāl), He hears and sees all contingent beings and their existential attributes, regardless of type. Thus the passage emphasizes divine transcendence (tanzīh) and the comprehensive nature of God's attributes.

Key Terms
سمع
بصر
تعلق
علم
موجودات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's hearing and sight are similar to human senses, just more powerful.
Correction: The passage clarifies that God's hearing and sight are fundamentally different in scope and modality: they relate to all existents without restriction, whereas human senses are limited to specific types of objects under specific conditions.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, how does the scope of God's hearing and sight differ from human hearing and sight?
A) God hears and sees only sounds and bodies, but with greater detail.
B) God's hearing and sight relate to all existents, both eternal and originated, whereas human senses are limited to certain existents under specific conditions.
C) God's hearing and sight are identical to His knowledge, so they add nothing new.
D) Human hearing and sight encompass all things, but are weaker than God's.

Key Takeaway
God's hearing and sight are all-encompassing, relating to every existent in eternity and perpetuity, unlike the limited human senses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's hearing encompasses your smallest whisper and even your silent thoughts, how does that change what you allow yourself to say or think when you are alone? Will you commit to guarding your inner speech from today?
?
Since God sees all your deeds, not just outward actions but inner states, what secret habit or attitude are you harboring that you need to repent from today because He already sees it?
?
The lesson says God hears and sees His own essence eternally. Are you more concerned with being seen by people than by God who sees your true self? What step will you take this week to live more for His sight than for human approval?

The Definition of Divine Speech
Sanusi defines the divine attribute of speech and lists its negations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Allah's speech is an eternal attribute without letters or sounds, and that it is not subject to non-existence, silence, division, or temporal precedence and delay.

Core Idea
Allah's speech is not like our speech—no letters, no sounds, no beginning.

Sanusi begins by defining the divine attribute of speech (kalam). He states clearly that it is an eternal attribute subsisting in Allah's essence, not composed of letters or sounds, and not subject to temporal limitations. This establishes the uniqueness of divine speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening definition
Read the Arabic passage from the beginning up to 'ولا التقديم ولا التأخير'. Notice how Sanusi first negates letters and sounds, then lists the properties of this eternal attribute.
Pay attention to the five negations: no letter, no sound, no non-existence, no silence, no division, no precedence, no delay.
2
Understand the concept of eternity
The attribute is described as 'أزليَّة' (eternal). Consider what this means: it has no beginning, no end, and does not change.
3
Reflect on the negations
Why does Sanusi deny letters and sounds? Because human speech is composed of them, and divine speech is unlike creation.
This guards against anthropomorphism.
4
Connect to the learning objective
Ask yourself: How does this definition set divine speech apart from all created speech?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صفته الكلام

وَالْكَلَامُ الَّذِي لَيْسَ بِحَرْفٍ وَلَا صَوْتٍ ، وَيَتَعَلَّقُ بِمَا يَتَعَلَّقُ بِهِ الْعِلْمُ مِنَ الْمُتَعَلَّقَاتِ

كلام الله تعالى القائم بذاتِهِ : هو صفةٌ أزليَّةٌ ليسَ بحرفٍ ولا صوت (۱) ، ولا يقبلُ العدمَ ولا ما في معناه مِنَ السكوت ، ولا التبعيض ، ولا التقديم ولا التأخير .

ثم هو معَ وحدتِهِ متعلق ؛ أي : دال ازلاً وأبداً على جميع معلوماته التي لا نهاية لها ، وهو الذي عُبّر عنه بالنظم المعجز المسمى أيضاً بكلام الله تعالى حقيقة لغويَّةً ؛ لوجودِ كلامِهِ عزَّ وجلَّ فيه بحسَبِ الدلالة ، لا بالحلول ، ويسميان بالقرآن أيضاً (۲)

وكُنه هذه الصفة وسائر صفاتِهِ تعالى محجوب عن العقل كذاتِهِ جل وعز (۳) ، فليس لأحد أن يخوض في الكُنْهِ بعد معرفةِ
His attribute of Speech:

And speech which is not with letter nor sound, and it relates to what knowledge relates to of the objects of knowledge.

The speech of Allah the Exalted subsisting in His Essence: it is an eternal attribute, not with letter nor sound (1), and it does not accept non-existence nor what is in its meaning of silence, nor division, nor precedence nor delay.

Then it, despite its oneness, is related; i.e., indicating eternally and forever all His objects of knowledge which have no end, and it is that which is expressed by the miraculous composition also called the speech of Allah in a literal linguistic sense; because His speech, Mighty and Majestic, is present in it by way of indication, not by indwelling, and both are called the Qur'an as well (2).

And the reality of this attribute and the rest of His attributes, Exalted is He, is veiled from the intellect like His Essence, Mighty and Majestic (3), so no one may delve into the reality after knowing
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage defines the divine attribute of speech (kalam). It begins by stating that Allah's speech is not composed of letters or sounds, and it relates to the same objects as divine knowledge. Then it formally defines the speech of Allah subsisting in His essence as an eternal attribute, not made of letters/sounds, and not subject to non-existence, silence, division, or temporal precedence/delay. Despite its unity, this attribute relates (dāll) eternally to all infinite objects of knowledge. It is the same attribute that is expressed by the miraculous composition (al-naẓm al-muʿjiz) called the Qur'an, in a literal sense because the eternal speech is indicated by the Qur'an, not by indwelling. Finally, the passage notes that the true reality (kunh) of this attribute and all divine attributes is veiled from intellect, just as Allah's essence is, so no one should delve into it after knowing its existence.

Key Terms
صفة الكلام
أزلي
حرف
صوت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Allah's speech is similar to human speech (letters and sounds).
Correction: The passage explicitly states that it is not with letter nor sound, and it is eternal, unlike created speech.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, which of the following is NOT a property of Allah's speech?
A) It is an eternal attribute.
B) It is composed of letters and sounds.
C) It does not accept division.
D) It does not accept silence.

Key Takeaway
Allah's speech is an eternal attribute, not composed of letters or sounds, and free from temporal limitations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Quran, do you ever reflect that its reality is an eternal attribute of Allah, not just letters and sounds? How should that change the reverence with which you engage with it?
?
If Allah's speech is not like ours, then understanding Him requires transcending your own experience. Are you willing to accept that Allah is beyond your imagination, even when it feels uncomfortable?
?
The attribute of speech is eternal, without silence or interruption. How does that affect your trust in His promises? Can you recall a moment when you doubted His word? What would change if you truly internalized that His speech never fails?

The Eternal Speech and the Qur'an
Sanusi explains the relation of the eternal attribute to the Qur'an and the veiled reality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the eternal divine speech relates to the revealed Qur'an as an indication, not an indwelling, and why its true reality is veiled from human intellect.

Core Idea
The Qur'an indicates Allah's eternal speech without being identical to it.

Having established that Allah's speech is an eternal attribute, Sanusi now explains its relation to the revealed Qur'an. He emphasizes that the same eternal attribute indicates all divine knowledge, and that the miraculous composition (al-naẓm al-muʿjiz) called the Qur'an is a literal expression of that eternal speech by way of indication, not indwelling. He also reminds us that the true reality of this attribute, like all divine attributes, is veiled from the intellect.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on relation
Read from 'ثم هو معَ وحدتِهِ متعلق' to the end. Notice how Sanusi describes the oneness of the attribute and its multiple relations.
The attribute is one but indicates all things.
2
Understand 'indication' vs 'indwelling'
Sanusi clarifies that the eternal speech is present in the Qur'an by way of indication (دلالة), not by indwelling (حلول). This means the Qur'an points to the eternal speech without containing it physically.
This refutes the idea that Allah's speech literally enters creation.
3
Reflect on the veiled reality
The passage ends by stating that the reality (كُنه) of this attribute is veiled from the intellect, just as Allah's essence is. No one should delve into it after acknowledging its existence.
This teaches humility before divine mysteries.
4
Connect to the learning objective
Ask yourself: How does this passage safeguard the transcendence of Allah's speech while affirming the Qur'an as His literal word?

Defining Allah's Speech as an Eternal Attribute
Defines Allah's speech as an eternal attribute not composed of letters or sounds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Allah's speech is an eternal, non-material attribute that is indicated by the Quran without indwelling.

Core Idea
Allah's speech is eternal, not made of letters or sounds, and transcends all temporal attributes.

Sanusi now turns to the divine attribute of Speech (al-kalam). He defines it as an eternal attribute subsisting in Allah's essence, distinct from human speech made of letters and sounds. This passage also clarifies the relationship between the eternal attribute and the revealed Quran, and warns against delving into the essence of this attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall teaching about Allah's speech as an eternal attribute.
Notice the key negations: not a letter, not a sound.
2
Identify the key properties
List the properties: eternal, not composed of letters/sounds, indivisible, not subject to silence or delay, and it relates to all objects of knowledge.
These properties differentiate divine speech from human speech.
3
Understand the relationship with the Quran
The Quran is called 'speech of Allah' linguistically because it indicates the eternal attribute, not because the attribute indwells in it.
This clarifies that the Quran is a created expression pointing to the uncreated attribute.
4
Reflect on the caution about quiddity
The passage warns that the true nature of this attribute is beyond human intellect, so we must not speculate beyond affirming what is necessary.
This applies to all divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صفته الكلام
وَالْكَلَامُ الَّذِي لَيْسَ بِحَرْفٍ وَلَا صَوْتٍ ، وَيَتَعَلَّقُ بِمَا يَتَعَلَّقُ بِهِ الْعِلْمُ مِنَ الْمُتَعَلَّقَاتِ
كلام الله تعالى القائم بذاتِهِ : هو صفةٌ أزليَّةٌ ليسَ بحرفٍ ولا صوت (۱) ، ولا يقبلُ العدمَ ولا ما في معناه مِنَ السكوت ، ولا التبعيض ، ولا التقديم ولا التأخير .
ثم هو معَ وَحدتِهِ متعلق ؛ أي : دال ازلاً وأبداً على جميع معلوماته التي لا نهاية لها ، وهو الذي عُبّر عنه بالنظم المعجز المسمى أيضاً بكلام الله تعالى حقيقة لغويَّةً ؛ لوجودِ كلامِهِ عزَّ وجلَّ فيه بحسَبِ الدلالة ، لا بالحلول ، ويسميان بالقرآن أيضاً (۲)

His attribute of Speech. And the attribute of Speech that is not letter nor sound, and relates to what knowledge relates to of the objects of relation. The speech of Allah the Exalted subsisting in His essence: it is an eternal attribute, not a letter nor a sound (1), and it does not accept non-existence nor what is in its meaning of silence, nor division, nor precedence nor delay. Then it, despite its unity, is related, i.e., it signifies from eternity and eternally all of His infinite objects of knowledge, and it is that which is expressed by the miraculous composition also called 'the speech of Allah' in a linguistic reality; because His speech, glory be to Him, is present in it in terms of indication, not by indwelling, and both are also called the Quran (2). And the quiddity of this attribute and all His attributes the Exalted is veiled from the intellect like His essence, glory be to Him (3), so no one may delve into the quiddity after knowing what is obligatory for His essence and attributes.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage defines the divine attribute of Speech (صفة الكلام). It begins by stating that Allah's speech is an eternal attribute (أزليَّة) subsisting in His essence, not composed of letters or sounds. This negates both temporal creation and material composition. The attribute is indivisible and does not allow for sequential ordering. Despite its unity, it signifies (يَتَعَلَّق) all of Allah's knowledge from pre-eternity to post-eternity. The passage then clarifies that the miraculous composition of the Quran is called 'the speech of Allah' in a true linguistic sense because it indicates (by implication) the eternal attribute, not by indwelling. Finally, a caution is given: the true essence of this attribute, like Allah's essence, is beyond the reach of any intellect, so one should not speculate beyond affirming what is necessary.

Key Takeaway
Allah's speech is an eternal, indivisible attribute that signifies all knowledge, and the Quran indicates it without indwelling.

Refuting Mu'tazilites with Inner Speech
Refutes Mu'tazilite confinement of speech via inner speech analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the kalam scholars' analogy of inner speech is a refutation of the Mu'tazilites and not a claim of similarity between divine and human speech.

Core Idea
The analogy with inner speech refutes the Mu'tazilites without comparing essences.

The Mu'tazilites claimed that speech consists only of letters and sounds. In response, Sunni scholars appealed to the concept of inner speech (al-kalam al-nafsi) found in human beings. This passage clarifies the correct purpose of that analogy and warns against misunderstanding it as a claim of equality between divine and human speech.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazilite position
Recognize that the Mu'tazilites confined speech to letters and sounds, denying any speech that is not composed of such physical elements.
This is the view being refuted.
2
Identify the scholars' use of analogy
Notice that the scholars mention inner speech not to compare essences, but to show that speech can exist without letters/sounds, thus refuting the Mu'tazilite confinement.
The phrase 'النقض على المعتزلة' indicates refutation.
3
Grasp the warning against misinterpretation
Understand that imagining similarity in essence between divine and human speech leads to anthropomorphism, like the Hashwiyyah.
The author stresses that divine speech is incomparable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما يوجد في كتب علماء الكلامِ مِنَ التمثيل بالكلامِ النفسي في الشاهدِ عندَ ردّهم على المعتزلة القائلين بانحصار الكلام في الحروف والأصوات .. لا يُفهَمُ منهُ تشبيه كلامِهِ جلَّ وعزَّ بكلامنا النفسي في الكُنْه ، تعالى وجلَّ عن أنْ يكونَ لهُ شريك في ذاته أو صفاته أو أفعاله
وكيف يُتوهّمُ أنَّ كلامَهُ تعالى مماثل لكلامنا النفسي وكلامنا النفسي أعراض حادثة يوجد فيها التقديم والتأخير ، وطروه البعض بعد عدمِ البعض الذي يتقدَّمُهُ (۱) ، ويترتب (۲) وينعدم بحسب وجودِ ذلك في كلامنا اللفظي ؟!
فمَنْ توهَّمَ هذا في كلامِهِ جلَّ وعزَّ فليس بينه وبين الحشويَّةِ ونحوهم مِنَ المبتدعة القائلين بأنَّ كلامَهُ تعالى حروفٌ وأصوات .. فرق .
وإنَّما مقصد العلماء بذكرِ الكلامِ النفسي في الشاهد : النقض على المعتزلة في حصرِهمُ الكلامَ في الحروفِ والأصوات ، فقيل لهم : ينتقض حصركم ذلكَ بكلامنا النفسي ؛ فإنَّهُ كلام حقيقةً وليس بحرفٍ ولا صوت ، وإذا صح هذا فكلامُ مولانا جل وعزَّ أيضاً كلام ليسَ بحرفٍ ولا صوت .
And what is found in the books of the kalam scholars of analogizing with inner speech in the witnessed realm when refuting the Mu'tazilites who claim that speech is confined to letters and sounds — it is not understood from this a comparison of His speech, glory be to Him, with our inner speech in quiddity. Exalted and high is He above having a partner in His essence or His attributes or His actions. How can it be imagined that His speech is similar to our inner speech, while our inner speech are accidental attributes that come into being, in which there is precedence and delay, and the occurrence of some after the non-existence of the one that precedes it (1), and they are ordered (2) and cease to exist according to the existence of that in our verbal speech?! So whoever imagines this concerning His speech, glory be to Him, there is no difference between him and the Hashwiyyah and similar innovators who say that His speech consists of letters and sounds. Rather, the purpose of the scholars in mentioning inner speech in the witnessed realm is to refute the Mu'tazilites in their confinement of speech to letters and sounds. So it was said to them: Your confinement is refuted by our inner speech, for it is truly speech without being a letter or sound. And if this is correct, then the speech of our Lord, glory be to Him, is also speech not composed of letter or sound.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite claim that speech consists only of letters and sounds. The author first clarifies that when kalam scholars analogize with human inner speech (الكلام النفسي), they do not intend to compare the essence of Allah's speech to human speech. Rather, they use the analogy only to refute the Mu'tazilite restriction. The proof proceeds by pointing out that human inner speech is real speech despite being neither letter nor sound; therefore, the Mu'tazilite claim that speech is restricted to letters and sounds is invalid. If human inner speech qualifies as speech, then Allah's speech, which is eternal and not composed of letters, is even more worthy of the name. The author concludes by warning that anyone who imagines similarity in essence between divine and human speech is no different from the Hashwiyyah (anthropomorphists) and other innovators who claim Allah's speech is composed of letters and sounds.

Key Terms
الكلام النفسي
المعتزلة
الحشوية
التمثيل
الشاهد
حرف
صوت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The analogy of inner speech implies that divine speech is similar to human inner speech in essence.
Correction: The scholars use the analogy only to show that speech can be without letters/sounds; they do not claim similarity in quiddity. Divine speech is eternal and non-accidental, while human inner speech is created and temporal.

Checkpoint
What is the main purpose of mentioning human inner speech in the refutation of the Mu'tazilites?
A) To prove that human speech is similar to divine speech.
B) To demonstrate that speech can exist without letters and sounds, thus refuting the Mu'tazilite confinement.
C) To show that inner speech is superior to verbal speech.
D) To establish that divine speech is also composed of letters and sounds.

Key Takeaway
The analogy of inner speech serves solely to refute the Mu'tazilite confinement, not to assert any similarity in essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever, in your understanding of Allah's speech, unconsciously limited it to the words you recite, as the Mu'tazilites did? How does this lesson challenge you to expand your conception of divine speech beyond letters and sounds, and what practical change will you make in how you approach the Quran starting today?
?
The scholars used an analogy (inner speech) knowing it could be misunderstood. When you explain Islam to others, do you guard against implying that Allah resembles His creation? Think of a specific attribute you often talk about. How will you now teach it using refutation without essence-comparison?
?
If Allah's speech is not composed of letters or sounds, then every meaning you derive from the Quran is not confined to its Arabic text. Does your heart tremble at the weight of that? How would your life change if you truly believed that Allah's eternal speech is beyond human language, yet He chose to reveal it in words for you? Write one concrete commitment you will make this week to honor that truth.
