Divine Speech: Not Letter nor Sound
Sanusi distinguishes divine speech from human inner speech via a negative attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that divine speech is not composed of letters or sounds and that its only similarity to human inner speech is this negative attribute, while their realities are completely distinct.

Core Idea
Divine speech shares only a negation with human speech, not its reality.

Sanusi presents a crucial distinction about the nature of divine speech (kalam). He clarifies that when we say Allah's speech is not a letter or sound, we are describing a negative attribute — and this negative attribute is the only point of commonality with human inner speech. But he warns us not to confuse this shared negation with any similarity in positive essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the warning tone at the end.
2
Identify the two things compared
Sanusi compares divine speech (kalam Allah) and human inner speech (kalam nafsi). Identify what they share and where they differ.
3
Understand the negative attribute
The shared attribute is negative: 'not a letter nor a sound.' Recognize that this is a negation, not a positive description.
Negative attributes (sifat salbiyya) describe what Allah is not.
4
Grasp the warning
Sanusi emphatically states that the realities (haqiqa) are totally distinct. Reflect on why someone might mistakenly think they are the same.
The warning about slipping feet underscores the seriousness of this error.
5
Reflect on the error
Consider how sharing a negation could lead someone to wrongly equate the two. This lesson guards against that mistake.

بحرف ولا صوتٍ ، فلم يقع الاشتراك بينهما إلا في هذه الصفة السلبية ؛ وهي أنَّ كلام مولانا جل وعز ليس بحرفٍ ولا صوت كما أنَّ كلامنا النفسي ليسَ بحرفٍ ولا صوتٍ ، أما الحقيقة فمباينة للحقيقة كل المباينة فاعرف هذا ؛ فقد زلَّتْ هنا أقدام لم تُؤيَّد بنورٍ مِنَ الملكِ العلام .
Not a letter nor sound; so no commonality occurred between them except in this negative attribute: that the speech of our Lord, exalted and majestic, is not a letter nor a sound, just as our inner speech is not a letter nor a sound. As for the reality, it is totally distinct from the reality. So know this; for here have slipped feet that were not supported by light from the All-Knowing King.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage clarifies the nature of divine speech (kalam) in relation to human speech. It begins by stating that divine speech is not composed of letters or sounds, and that the only commonality between divine speech and human inner speech (kalam nafsi) is this negative attribute: both are not letters or sounds. However, the author emphatically asserts that their realities (haqiqa) are completely distinct and different. He then warns the reader that failure to grasp this distinction has caused many to err, as their feet slipped due to lack of divine illumination. The key point is that sharing a negative attribute does not imply similarity in positive essence.

Misconception: Because both divine speech and human inner speech are not letters or sounds, they are essentially the same.
Correction: Sanusi explains that sharing a negative attribute (not being letter/sound) does not imply any similarity in positive reality; the two realities are completely distinct.

Checkpoint

B) Their being not a letter nor a sound
C) Their being composed of letters and sounds

Key Takeaway
The only thing divine speech shares with human inner speech is that both are not letters or sounds; their true natures are utterly distinct.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
You now know that sharing a negation does not make two things similar. Has your heart ever felt comforted by thinking 'Allah is like this because I understand it'? Repent of that comfort; His reality is utterly beyond. How will you maintain awe in your worship now?

The Four Divisions of Divine Attributes by Relation
Sanusi divides the attributes of meanings into four types based on their relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi classifies the essential divine attributes (sifat al-ma'ani) into four categories based on what they relate to, and the subtle comparisons between them.

Core Idea
Each divine attribute has a specific range of relation—some relate to nothing, some to contingents, some to all existents, and some to rational judgments.

Sanusi has just enumerated the seven attributes of meanings. Now he provides a deeper classification by examining what each attribute 'relates to' (ta'alluq). This analysis reveals important differences: while Life stands alone as relating to nothing beyond itself, other attributes have specific objects—some relate only to created things, others to all existents, and still others to the very structure of rational thought. Understanding these relations helps avoid confusion about how God's attributes operate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the four divisions
Read the Arabic passage once. Then read the translation. Look for the four categories Sanusi lists: life, power and will, hearing and sight, knowledge and speech. Notice what each relates to.
The division is based on 'tacalluq' (relation).
2
Analyze the objects of relation for each division
Life relates to nothing. Power and will relate only to contingent beings. Hearing and sight relate to all existents. Knowledge and speech relate to all divisions of rational judgment.
Contingent beings are possible entities; existents include necessary and contingent.
3
Compare Power/Will with Hearing/Sight
Sanusi notes a partial overlap and difference: Power and Will also relate to nonexistent contingents, while Hearing and Sight also relate to the necessary existent (God's essence and attributes). They share the existent contingent.
This comparison shows the precision of theological language.
4
Understand the footnote about Knowledge and Speech
Read Dasuqi's caution: the phrase 'all divisions of rational judgment' might wrongly suggest that knowledge and speech do not cover the components of judgment (subject, predicate, relation). The footnote clarifies they do.
This nuance prevents a misunderstanding about God's knowledge and speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقسام صفات المعاني من حيث التعلقات وهنا انتهى ما عدَّ في العقيدةِ مِنْ صفاتِ المعاني ، وحاصلها : أنَّها تنقسم أربعة أقسام : قسم لا يتعلق بشيء : وهي الحياة . وقسم يتعلق بالممكنات فقط : وهي اثنتان ؛ القدرة والإرادة وقسم يتعلق بجميع الموجوداتِ : وهي اثنتان ؛ السمع والبصر وقسم يتعلَّق بجميع أقسام الحكم العقلي : وهو العلم والكلام (١) . وأعم الصفات المتعلقة في التعلقِ العلم والكلام . وبين متعلَّق القدرة والإرادة وبين متعلَّق السمع والبصر عموم وخصوص مِـنْ وجه ؛ فتزيد القدرة والإرادة بتعلقهما بالمعدوم الممكن ، ويزيدُ السمع والبصر بتعلُّقهما بالموجودِ الواجب ؛ كذاتِ مولانا جل وعزَّ وصفاتِهِ ، ويشترك القسمان في تعلقهما بالموجودِ الممكن (١) هذه العبارة توهم عدم تعلقهما بتصور أطراف الحكم ؛ كتصور الموضوع والمحمول والنسبة ، وليس كذلك ، بل علمه تعالى كما ينكشف به الأحكام ینكشف به أطرافها ، وكما أن كلامه يدل على الحكم يدل على أطرافه ، ولو قال : أقسام الحكم العقلي وبمتعلقاته .. لكان أحسن . « دسوقي » ( ص ١٣٥ ) .
Divisions of the attributes of meanings in terms of relations. Here ends what was counted in the creed of the attributes of meanings, and their summary: that they are divided into four divisions: a division that does not relate to anything: that is life. And a division that relates only to contingent beings: they are two: power and will. And a division that relates to all existents: they are two: hearing and sight. And a division that relates to all divisions of rational judgment: that is knowledge and speech. And the most general of the attributes in terms of relation are knowledge and speech. And between the related object of power and will and the related object of hearing and sight there is general and specific in some respect: power and will increase by relating to the nonexistent contingent, and hearing and sight increase by relating to the necessary existent, such as the essence of our Lord, exalted and majestic, and His attributes. And the two divisions share in relating to the existent contingent. (1) This statement might suggest that they do not relate to the conceptualization of the parts of judgment, like the conceptualization of the subject, predicate, and relation, but it is not so. Rather, His knowledge, exalted, as it discloses the judgments, also discloses their parts, and just as His speech indicates the judgment, it indicates its parts. And if he had said: 'divisions of rational judgment and its related matters' it would have been better. — Dasuqi (p. 135).
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage presents a classification of the attributes of meanings (sifat al-ma'ani) — the essential divine attributes — based on what they relate to. Sanusi enumerates four divisions: (1) Life, which does not relate to anything outside itself; (2) Power and Will, which relate only to contingent beings (mumkinat); (3) Hearing and Sight, which relate to all existents (including the necessary existent and contingent existents); (4) Knowledge and Speech, which relate to all divisions of rational judgment (ahkam 'aqliyya). He then notes that Knowledge and Speech are the most general in their relation. Next, he compares the relata of Power/Will and Hearing/Sight: they share the contingent existent but differ in that Power/Will also relate to nonexistent contingents (ma'dum mumkin), while Hearing/Sight relate to the necessary existent (the divine essence and attributes). A marginal note by Dasuqi (included in the text) cautions that the phrase 'relates to all divisions of rational judgment' might be misunderstood as excluding the conceptualization of the judgment's components (subject, predicate, relation); he clarifies that knowledge and speech indeed encompass those components as well, and suggests a better phrasing.

Key Terms
صفات المعاني
تعلق
حكم عقلي
ممكن
واجب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Knowledge and Speech relate only to judgments (e.g., propositions) and not to the components of judgments (subject, predicate, relation).
Correction: The footnote clarifies that God's knowledge and speech indeed encompass the parts of judgments, not just the judgments themselves.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, which attribute does not relate to anything?
A) Power
B) Will
C) Life
D) Knowledge

Key Takeaway
The attributes of meanings are distinguished not only by what they are, but by what they relate to—a crucial nuance in Islamic theology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your life is full of possibilities that may never happen—dreams, fears, paths you didn't take. Sanusi says God's power and will relate to each of those nonexistent possibilities. Do you trust Him with the life you didn't live, or do you cling to regrets? Name one unrealized possibility you need to surrender to God's will today.
?
God's hearing and sight relate to all existents, including His own essence. You cannot perceive Him, but He perceives you completely—including every secret thought. Is there a hidden sin you have been avoiding? Write it down, and commit now to confess it to God and seek His forgiveness.
?
Your every belief is known by God in its full logical structure—subject, predicate, relation. Are there areas of your faith where you rely on weak or incomplete reasoning, expecting God to overlook your negligence? Commit today to studying one Islamic doctrine you have not fully understood, asking God to guide you to sound knowledge.

The Four Categories of Attributes of Meanings
Sanusi enumerates the four categories of attributes of meanings based on their objects of relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four categories of attributes of meanings based on their objects of relation, how they relate to each other, and a common clarification about knowledge and speech.

Core Idea
Attributes of meanings are classified by what they relate to: nothing, contingents, all existents, or rational judgments.

Sanusi now completes the discussion of attributes of meanings by classifying them into four categories according to their objects of relation. This classification reveals the scope of each attribute and the relationships between them. The passage also includes an important comparison and a footnote clarifying the range of knowledge and speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the four categories
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the translation. Notice the four sections Sanusi outlines for the attributes of meanings.
The categories are based on what each attribute relates to (تعلق).
2
Understand why life relates to nothing
Life is the condition for other attributes but has no specific object of relation. Ask why it is included here.
Life is essential but does not 'attach' to anything.
3
Compare the second and third categories
Power and will relate only to contingent things, while hearing and sight relate to all existents (including God). Note their overlap and differences.
This is a 'general and specific in some respect' relationship.
4
Examine the most general attributes and the footnote
Knowledge and speech are the most general because they relate to all rational judgments and their terms. The footnote corrects a possible misunderstanding.
The footnote clarifies that knowledge and speech cover both judgments and their parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقسام صفات المعاني من حيث التعلقات وهنا انتهى ما عدَّ في العقيدةِ مِنْ صفاتِ المعاني ، وحاصلها : أنَّها تنقسم أربعة أقسام : قسم لا يتعلق بشيء : وهي الحياة . وقسم يتعلق بالممكنات فقط : وهي اثنتان ؛ القدرة والإرادة وقسم يتعلق بجميع الموجوداتِ : وهي اثنتان ؛ السمع والبصر (١) وقسم يتعلَّق بجميع أقسام الحكم العقلي : وهو العلم والكلام (١) . وأعم الصفات المتعلقة في التعلقِ العلم والكلام . وبين متعلَّق القدرة والإرادة وبين متعلَّق السمع والبصر عموم وخصوص مِـنْ وجه ؛ فتزيد القدرة والإرادة بتعلقهما بالمعدوم الممكن ، ويزيدُ السمع والبصر بتعلُّقهما بالموجودِ الواجب ؛ كذاتِ مولانا جل وعزَّ وصفاتِهِ ، ويشترك القسمان في تعلقهما بالموجودِ الممكن (١) هذه العبارة توهم عدم تعلقهما بتصور أطراف الحكم ؛ كتصور الموضوع والمحمول والنسبة ، وليس كذلك ، بل علمه تعالى كما ينكشف به الأحكام ینكشف به أطرافها ، وكما أن كلامه يدل على الحكم يدل على أطرافه ، ولو قال : أقسام الحكم العقلي وبمتعلقاته .. لكان أحسن . « دسوقي ( ص ١٣٥ ) .
Divisions of the attributes of meanings from the perspective of relations. And here ends what was enumerated in the Creed of the attributes of meanings, and their summary: that they are divided into four sections: a section that relates to nothing: which is life. And a section that relates only to contingent things: which are two: power and will. And a section that relates to all existents: which are two: hearing and sight. And a section that relates to all divisions of rational judgment: which are knowledge and speech. And the most general of the attributes with respect to relation are knowledge and speech. And between the object of relation of power and will and the object of relation of hearing and sight there is general and specific in some respect; for power and will increase by relating to the non-existent contingent, and hearing and sight increase by relating to the existent necessary, like the essence of our Lord, may He be glorified and exalted, and His attributes, and the two sections share in relating to the existent contingent. (1) This expression suggests that they do not relate to the conception of the terms of the judgment, like the conception of the subject, predicate, and relation, but it is not so; rather His knowledge, may He be exalted, as judgments are disclosed by it, so its terms are disclosed by it, and just as His speech indicates the judgment, it indicates its terms. If he had said: 'the divisions of rational judgment and their relations' it would have been better. — al-Dasuqi (p. 135).
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage is a classification with enumeration and comparison. It first states that the seven attributes of meanings (صفات المعاني) that were enumerated in the Creed (العقيدة, meaning Sanusi's Umm al-Barahin) are divided into four categories based on their objects of relation (تعلقات). 

1. The first category is life (الحياة), which relates to nothing—it is a condition for the other attributes but has no specific object. 
2. The second category is power (القدرة) and will (الإرادة), which relate only to contingent things (الممكنات), i.e., possible existents. 
3. The third category is hearing (السمع) and sight (البصر), which relate to all existents (جميع الموجودات), both contingent and necessary. 
4. The fourth category is knowledge (العلم) and speech (الكلام), which relate to all divisions of rational judgment (أقسام الحكم العقلي)—i.e., the judgments of the mind about existence, non-existence, etc. The passage then notes that knowledge and speech are the most general in relation because they encompass both the judgment and its terms (subject, predicate, relation).

Then the author compares the second and third categories: they share a general-specific relationship in some respect. Power and will have a broader scope because they relate to non-existent contingents (المعدوم الممكن) before creation, while hearing and sight have a broader scope because they relate to the necessary existent (God's essence and attributes). They share in relating to existent contingents.

A footnote by al-Dasuqi clarifies that the phrase 'all divisions of rational judgment' might imply that knowledge and speech do not relate to the terms of the judgment (subject, predicate, relation), but they do. Knowledge discloses both the judgment and its terms, and speech indicates both. Al-Dasuqi suggests the author could have said 'the divisions of rational judgment and their relations' to avoid misunderstanding.

Key Terms
صفات المعاني
تعلقات
الحياة
القدرة
الإرادة
السمع
البصر
العلم
الكلام
الحكم العقلي
عموم وخصوص من وجه
المعدوم الممكن
الموجود الواجب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge and speech relate only to the judgment itself, not to its terms (subject, predicate, relation).
Correction: Knowledge discloses both judgments and their terms; speech indicates both the judgment and its terms. Thus, they relate to all parts of rational judgment.

Checkpoint
How many categories are the attributes of meanings divided into, and which attribute belongs to the category that relates to nothing?
A) Three; hearing
B) Four; life
C) Five; power
D) Two; knowledge

Key Takeaway
The attributes of meanings are divided into four categories based on their objects: life relates to nothing, power/will to contingents, hearing/sight to all existents, and knowledge/speech to all rational judgments, with the latter two being the most general.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
God's knowledge encompasses all rational judgments, including your hidden thoughts and intentions. What secret thought have you been harboring that you would be ashamed to bring into the light of His knowledge? Will you repent of it now, knowing He sees it?
?
The attribute of life relates to nothing—it is self-sufficient. How does your constant need for others' validation and approval contradict the reality that only God is truly self-sufficient? What is one attachment you must sever to live in light of His sufficiency?

Why the Attribute of Perception Was Omitted
Sanusi explains why the eighth attribute (perception) was omitted from the seven attributes of meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dispute over the attribute of perception and why Sanusi confined his creed to the seven agreed-upon attributes.

Core Idea
The eighth attribute was omitted due to scholarly dispute and lack of scriptural evidence.

Sanusi's creed lists seven attributes of meanings, but an eighth—perception—was disputed. Some scholars argued it is reducible to knowledge; others claimed it is an additional attribute. Without transmitted evidence, Sanusi chose to exclude it, following the principle of sticking to what is agreed upon.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the author's own words.
Notice the concise style typical of matn works.
2
Identify the two positions on perception
The passage presents two views: that perception is reducible to knowledge, or that it is an additional attribute. Spot how each is described.
3
Understand the argument for suspension
Read why some scholars chose suspension (al-waqf): due to lack of transmitted evidence (samʿ). Consider what this says about kalam methodology.
Suspension means neither affirming nor denying until evidence arrives.
4
Reflect on Sanusi's methodological principle
Sanusi limits his creed to what is agreed upon (al-mujmaʿ ʿalayh). Notice how this principle guides his omission of the eighth attribute.
This is a key point about how kalam texts handle disputed matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخلاف في صفة الإدراك وإنَّما اقتصر في العقيدة على هذه السبع ، ولم يعد معها الصفةَ الثامنة ؛ وهي إدراكه تعالى للطعوم والروائح ونحوهما مِنَ الكيفيَّاتِ التي تستدعي في حقنا بحسَبِ العادةِ اتصالات .. لأجل الخلافِ الذي في هذه الصفة : هل هي في حقه تعالى ترجع إلى العلم ، أو هي زائدة على العلم ، ويكون إدراكه تعالى لتلك الأمور بإدراك زائد على العلمِ مِنْ غيرِ اتصال بها ، ولا تكييف للذات العليَّةِ بما جرَتِ العادةُ أنْ تتكيف به ذواتُنا عند هذا الإدراك مِنَ اللذاتِ والآلام ونحوهما ؟ ويتعلَّقُ هذا الإدراك على هذا القولِ في حقه تعالى بكل موجود ؛ كسمعِهِ جَلَّ وعزَّ وبصرِه . والذي اختارَهُ بعضُ المحققين في هذا الإدراك الوقفُ (۱) ؛ لعدم ورودِ السمع به ، فلأجل ما وقعَ فيهِ مِنْ هذا الخلافِ تركنا عدَّهُ صفاتِ المعاني ، واقتصرنا على المجمع عليه ، وبالله تعالى التوفيق (۱) هو العلامة المقترح في شرح الإرشاد ) ( ص ۳۱۲ ) ، وتبعه عليه تلميذه العلامة ابن التلمساني في ( شرح معالم أصول الدين » ( ص ٣٧٦ ) .
The dispute over the attribute of perception. And the Creed only confined itself to these seven, and did not count with them the eighth attribute, which is His, exalted is He, perception of tastes, odors, and similar qualities which, with respect to us, according to custom, require contact… because of the dispute concerning this attribute: is it, with respect to Him, exalted is He, reducible to knowledge, or is it additional to knowledge, such that His perception of those things is by a perception additional to knowledge without contact with them, and without conditioning the supreme essence by what customarily conditions our essences at this perception, like pleasures, pains, and the like? And this perception, according to this opinion, relates with respect to Him, exalted is He, to every existent, like His hearing and sight, may He be glorified and exalted. And some investigators chose suspension (al-waqf) regarding this perception, due to the lack of transmitted evidence (samʿ) concerning it. And because of this dispute that occurred concerning it, we omitted counting it among the attributes of meanings, and confined ourselves to what is agreed upon, and with Allah, exalted is He, is success. (1) He is the scholar al-Muqri' in his commentary on al-Irshad (p. 312), and his student al-ʿAllāma Ibn al-Tilimsānī followed him in his commentary on Maʿālim Uṣūl al-Dīn (p. 376).
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of a disputed attribute: perception (الإدراك). It explains why the author of the Creed (Sanusi) omitted this attribute from the seven attributes of meanings. The eighth attribute is God's perception of tastes, odors, and similar qualities that in human beings normally require physical contact (like taste buds or olfactory nerves). The dispute is whether in God this perception is reducible to knowledge (i.e., it is not a separate attribute) or whether it is an additional attribute beyond knowledge, through which God perceives without any contact or physical condition, and without subjecting the divine essence to the changes (like pleasure or pain) that occur in humans during such perception. If it is an additional attribute, then it relates to every existent, similar to hearing and sight. Some scholars, like al-Muqri' and his student Ibn al-Tilimsānī, chose suspension (الوقف)—neither affirming nor denying it as a separate attribute—because there is no scriptural evidence (السمع) to support it. Due to this lack of consensus, Sanusi did not include it among the attributes of meanings in his creed, limiting himself to the seven universally agreed-upon ones.

Key Terms
صفة الإدراك
الطعوم
الروائح
الكيفيات
العلم
زائدة
الوقف
السمع
المجمع عليه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the omission of the eighth attribute means it is definitively not an attribute of God.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies it is omitted due to dispute and lack of transmitted evidence; he confines to what is agreed upon, not a denial.

Checkpoint
Why did Sanusi omit the attribute of perception (الإدراك) from the seven attributes of meanings?
A) Because it is definitely not an attribute of God
B) Because there is a dispute about whether it is reducible to knowledge or additional, and no scriptural evidence
C) Because it is the same as hearing and sight
D) Because it only applies to humans

Key Takeaway
Sanusi omitted the attribute of perception from the seven attributes of meanings due to unresolved scholarly dispute and lack of scriptural evidence, adhering to consensus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a theological disagreement among scholars, do you instinctively pick a side, or do you humbly admit uncertainty? How might adopting a posture of 'suspension' (al-waqf) protect you from arrogance or false certainty in matters where revelation is silent?
?
Does the fact that God perceives without physical contact or change challenge your mental image of Him? Are you willing to leave that image incomplete and accept that your understanding is limited, or do you try to fill in the gaps with your own reasoning?
?
The omission of a potentially true attribute from a creedal text was done for the sake of consensus. Is there a teaching or practice in your faith that you hold onto more strongly than the consensus of the scholars? Are you ready to let it go if sound evidence suggests otherwise?

The Disagreement on the Attribute of Perception
Sanusi discusses the dispute over the attribute of perception and why it was omitted.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the disagreement about the divine attribute of perception (al-idrāk) and why Sanusi omitted it from his creedal list.

Core Idea
Perception is disputed: is it knowledge or a distinct attribute? Sanusi omits it due to lack of proof.

Sanusi explains why he excluded the eighth attribute from his creed. The attribute of perception—God's perceiving tastes, smells, and the like—is a subject of scholarly disagreement. Some see it as an aspect of knowledge, others as an additional attribute. Because of this dispute and the absence of clear scriptural evidence, Sanusi leaves it out and sticks to the unanimously agreed attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation once to grasp the overall argument.
2
Identify the two debated views
Find where Sanusi mentions the two possible interpretations of perception: either reducible to knowledge or additional to it.
3
Understand the suspension view
Note that some scholars suspend judgment on this attribute due to lack of textual evidence, and see how Sanusi responds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخلاف في صفة الإدراك وإنَّما اقتصر في العقيدة على هذه السبع ، ولم يعد معها الصفةَ الثامنة ؛ وهي إدراكه تعالى للطعوم والروائح ونحوهما مِنَ الكيفيَّاتِ التي تستدعي في حقنا بحسَبِ العادةِ اتصالات .. لأجل الخلافِ الذي في هذه الصفة : هل هي في حقه تعالى ترجع إلى العلم ، أو هي زائدة على العلم ، ويكون إدراكه تعالى لتلك الأمور بإدراك زائد على العلمِ مِنْ غيرِ اتصال بها ، ولا تكييف للذات العليَّةِ بما جرَتِ العادةُ أنْ تتكيف به ذواتُنا عند هذا الإدراك مِنَ اللذاتِ والآلام ونحوهما ؟ ويتعلَّقُ هذا الإدراك على هذا القولِ في حقه تعالى بكل موجود ؛ كسمعِهِ جَلَّ وعزَّ وبصرِه . والذي اختارَهُ بعضُ المحققين في هذا الإدراك الوقفُ (۱) ؛ لعدم ورودِ السمع به ، فلأجل ما وقعَ فيهِ مِنْ هذا الخلافِ تركنا عدَّهُ صفاتِ المعاني ، واقتصرنا على المجمع عليه ، وبالله تعالى التوفيق
The dispute regarding the attribute of perception. He confined the creed to these seven and did not count the eighth attribute with them, which is His perception, exalted, of tastes, smells, and similar qualities that in our case customarily require contact, because of the disagreement concerning this attribute: is it, with respect to Him, reducible to knowledge, or is it additional to knowledge, and His perception of those things occurs through a perception additional to knowledge, without contact with them, and without attributing a modality to the exalted Essence by what customarily causes our essences to be modified in such perception, such as pleasures, pains, and the like? This perception, on this view, pertains in His case to every existent, like His hearing and seeing, glorious and exalted. Some investigators have chosen suspension (al-waqf) concerning this perception, due to the lack of transmitted evidence about it. Because of this disagreement, we have omitted counting it among the attributes of meanings and confined ourselves to the agreed-upon. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage addresses the disagreement about the divine attribute of perception (al-idrāk). The author, Sanusi, explains why he confined his creedal list to seven attributes and omitted the eighth, which is God's perception of tastes, smells, and similar qualities. In created beings, such perceptions normally require physical contact, but the issue is whether God's perception is reducible to His knowledge or is an additional attribute. If additional, it would mean He perceives without contact and without any change in His essence. Some scholars suspend judgment (al-waqf) on this attribute due to the absence of scriptural evidence. Sanusi states that because of this disagreement, he omitted it from the list of attributes of meanings and stuck to the unanimously agreed attributes. The passage concludes with a call for divine success.

الوقف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sanusi denied the attribute of perception because he omitted it from his creed.
Correction: Sanusi did not deny perception; he omitted it due to lack of scriptural evidence and scholarly disagreement, as a methodological precaution.

Checkpoint
Why did Sanusi omit the eighth attribute of perception from his list in the creed?
A) He denied that God perceives tastes and smells.
B) He considered perception reducible to knowledge without question.
C) There was disagreement about the attribute and no clear scriptural evidence.
D) He forgot to include it.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi omitted the attribute of perception from his creed because of scholarly disagreement and the absence of clear scriptural evidence, not because he denied it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Sanusi omitted an attribute because he lacked clear textual proof. Is there any belief or practice you hold that you have never verified from the Qur'an and Sunnah? Will you commit to examining it today, even if it means suspending your current stance?
?
If you were in Sanusi's position, would you have the courage to leave out a belief that feels intuitive (like God's perception of tastes) simply because the evidence is inconclusive? What does your willingness to hold unverified beliefs say about your relationship with God's revelation?

The Seven Qualitative Attributes
Sanusi defines the seven qualitative attributes and explains their dependency on the attributes of meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nature of the seven qualitative attributes and how they are necessarily concomitant to the attributes of meanings.

Core Idea
The qualitative attributes are necessary descriptions derived from the attributes of meanings.

Sanusi now introduces a second set of seven attributes: the qualitative attributes. He explains that these are not independent but are derived from the attributes of meanings. Understanding this distinction helps us avoid the mistake of thinking that attributes like 'knowing' are separate from the attribute of knowledge itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Let the flow of Sanusi's explanation come through before you analyze.
Notice the markers (1), (2), (3) in the Arabic; they refer to footnotes in the original text.
2
Identify the relationship
Pay attention to how Sanusi describes the qualitative attributes as 'concomitant' (ملازمة) and 'branch' (فرع) of the first seven. Understand that being described as 'knowing' depends on the existence of knowledge.
This is the key difference between attributes-of-meanings and qualitative attributes.
3
List the seven qualitative attributes
At the end of the passage, Sanusi lists the seven: powerful, willing, knowing, living, hearing, seeing, speaking. Write them down and note how each corresponds to an attribute of meanings.
For example, 'knowing' corresponds to knowledge, 'powerful' to power, etc.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصفات المعنوية ثُمَّ سَبْعُ صِفَاتٍ تُسَمَّى : صِفَاتٍ مَعْنَوِيَّةٌ ، وَهِيَ مُلازِمَةٌ لِلسَّبْع الأُولَى إنَّما سُمِّيَتْ هذه الصفاتُ معنويَّةً ؛ لأنَّ الاتصاف بها فرع الاتصافِ بالسبع الأولى ، فإِنَّ اتصاف محلَّ مِنَ المُحَال (۱) بكونه عالماً أو قادراً مثلاً .. لا يصح إلا إذا قام به العلم أو القدرة ، وقس على هذا ، فصارَتِ السبع الأولى - وهي صفاتُ المعاني - عِلَلاً لهذه ؛ أي : ملزومة لها (۲) ، فلهذا نُسِبَتْ هذهِ إلى تلكَ ، فقيل فيها : صفات معنوية ، ولهذا كانَتْ هذه سبعاً مثل الأولى ، فالياء في لفظ ( المعنويَّة ) ياءُ النَّسَبِ (۳) ، نُسِبَتْ إلى ( المعنى ) ، والواو فيها بدلُّ مِنَ الألْفِ التي في ( المعنى ) وَهِيَ كَوْنُهُ تَعَالَى قَادِراً ، وَمُرِيداً ، وَعَالِماً ، وَحَيَّاً ، وَسَمِيعاً ، وَبَصِيراً ، وَمُتَكَلَّماً
The qualitative attributes. Then seven attributes called 'qualitative attributes', and they are concomitant to the first seven. They were called 'qualitative' only because being described by them is a branch of being described by the first seven. For indeed, the description of any locus (from among loci) as being knowing or powerful, for example, is not valid unless knowledge or power subsists in it. And analogize the rest. So the first seven — which are the attributes of meanings — became causes for these, i.e., they are entailed by them. Therefore, these were attributed to those, and it was said: 'qualitative attributes'. For this reason, they became seven like the first. The letter 'ya'' in the word 'al-ma'nawiyya' is the 'ya'' of attribution; they are attributed to 'al-ma'na' (meaning), and the 'waw' in it is a substitute for the 'alif' that is in 'al-ma'na'. And they are: His being, exalted, powerful, willing, knowing, living, hearing, seeing, and speaking.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage defines the seven qualitative attributes (al-ṣifāt al-maʿnawiyya). Sanusi explains that these attributes are called 'qualitative' because they are derived from the seven 'attributes of meanings' (ṣifāt al-maʿānī). The description of God as, for example, 'knowing' only holds if the attribute of knowledge subsists in Him. Thus the attributes of meanings are the causes (ʿilal) and the qualitative attributes are their necessary concomitants (lāzima). The term 'maʿnawiyya' comes from 'maʿna' (meaning) with the relative ya', and the waw replaces the alif in 'maʿna'. Finally, Sanusi lists the seven: being powerful, willing, knowing, living, hearing, seeing, and speaking.

Key Terms
صفات معنوية
ملازمة
صفات المعاني
ياء النسب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The qualitative attributes are separate from the attributes of meanings or identical to them.
Correction: They are distinct but necessary consequences: descriptions that follow necessarily from the existence of the attributes of meanings in the essence.

Checkpoint
Why are the seven attributes called 'qualitative' (معنوية)?
A) They are mental constructions with no real existence.
B) They are derived from and dependent on the attributes of meanings.
C) They are the same as the attributes of meanings.
D) They are negations of imperfections.

Key Takeaway
The seven qualitative attributes are the necessary descriptions of God that follow from the seven attributes of meanings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's attribute of being All-Knowing (ʿālim) is a necessary consequence of His knowledge (ʿilm), then He knows every detail of your life — even your hidden thoughts. Are you comfortable with that? When you sin in secret, do you truly believe He sees you, or do you act as if He doesn't? Before you sleep tonight, sit for two minutes and silently acknowledge that He is al-ʿAlīm, and ask yourself: what would I change if I really lived knowing that?
?
The qualitative attributes are described as 'concomitant' — they are not optional or accidental; they are who God is. If being 'powerful' (qādir) is necessary from His power, then nothing can weaken Him. Think of the one thing in your life that feels impossible to change or overcome. Are you acting as if God's power is limited? Today, write that thing down and say aloud: 'Ya Qādir, You are the One whose power is necessary, not contingent. I rely on You alone.' Then commit to one concrete step of trust.

The Seven Moral Attributes: Definition and Listing
Sanusi defines moral attributes and lists them with their causal concomitants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what the seven moral attributes are, why they are called 'moral', and how they correspond to the seven attributes of meanings.

Core Idea
Moral attributes are not new powers but necessary descriptions that follow from the attributes of meanings.

Sanusi introduces a second set of seven attributes that are derived from the first seven. These are called 'moral attributes' because they describe how the essence is qualified based on the first attributes. Understanding this helps clarify the structure of divine attributes in Sunni theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the English translation. Pay attention to how Sanusi explains the name 'moral attributes' and then lists them.
The passage contains both a conceptual explanation and a list.
2
Understand the naming explanation
Focus on why these attributes are called 'moral' (ma‘nawiyyah). Notice the mention of the yā’ of attribution and the substitute wāw.
3
Trace the correspondence
Each moral attribute is paired with one of the first seven attributes (attributes of meanings). See how 'able' corresponds to 'power', etc.
This shows the direct derivation.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ سَبْعُ صِفَاتٍ تُسَمَّى : صِفَاتٍ مَعْنَوِيَّةٌ ، وَهِيَ مُلازِمَةٌ لِلسَّبْع الأُولَى . إنَّما سُمِّيَتْ هذه الصفاتُ معنويَّةً ؛ لأنَّ الاتصاف بها فرع الاتصافِ بالسبع الأولى ، فإِنَّ اتصاف محلَّ مِنَ المحال بِكونه عالماً أو قادراً مثلاً .. لا يصح إلا إذا قام به العلم أو القدرة ، وقس على هذا ، فصارَتِ السبع الأولى - وهي صفاتُ المعاني - عِلَلاً لهذه ؛ أي : ملزومة لها ، فلهذا نُسِبَتْ هذهِ إلى تلكَ ، فقيل فيها : صفات معنوية ، ولهذا كانَتْ هذه سبعاً مثل الأولى ، فالياء في لفظ ( المعنويَّة ) ياءُ النَّسَبِ ، نُسِبَتْ إلى ( المعنى ) ، والواو فيها بدلُّ مِنَ الألْفِ التي في ( المعنى ) . وَهِيَ كَوْنُهُ تَعَالَى قَادِراً ، وَمُرِيداً ، وَعَالِماً ، وَحَيَّاً ، وَسَمِيعاً ، وَبَصِيراً ، وَمُتَكَلَّماً . لمَّا كانَتْ هذه الصفاتُ المعنويَّةُ لازمة لصفاتِ المعاني .. رتَّبَها على حسب ترتيب تلكَ ؛ فكونُهُ تعالى قادراً لازم للصفة الأولى مِنْ صفات المعاني ؛ وهي القدرةُ القائمة بذاتِهِ تعالى ، وكونه جل وعزَّ مريداً لازم للإرادةِ القائمة بذاتِهِ تبارك وتعالى ، وكونه تعالى عالماً لازم للعلم القائم بذاتِهِ تعالى ، وكونه تعالى حيّاً لازم للحياة القائمة بذاتِهِ تعالى ، وكونه تعالى سميعاً لازم للسمع القائم بذاتِهِ تعالى ، وكونه تعالى بصيراً لازم للبصرِ القائم بذاتِهِ تعالى ، وكونه تعالى متكلّماً لازم للكلام القائم بذاتِهِ تعالى . واعلم : أنَّ عدَّهم لهذهِ السبع في الصفاتِ هو على سبيل الحقيقة إن قلنا بثبوتِ الأحوالِ ؛ وهي صفات ثبوتية ليست بموجـودة ولا معدومة ، تقوم بموجود ، فتكونُ هذه الصفات المعنوية على هذا صفات ثابتة قائمة بذاتِهِ تعالى . وأما إن قلنا بنفي الأحوالِ ، وأنَّهُ لا واسطة بين الوجودِ والعدمِ هو مذهب الشيخ الأشعري .. فالثابتُ مِنَ الصفاتِ التي تقومُ بالذاتِ إِنَّما هو السبع الأولى التي هي صفاتُ المعاني ، وأمَّا هذهِ كما
Then seven attributes called 'moral attributes' (sifāt ma‘nawiyyah), and they are concomitant with the first seven. They were named moral attributes only because being qualified by them is a branch of being qualified by the first seven. For the qualification of any locus (mahall) by being knowing or able, for example, is not valid unless knowledge or power subsists in it. And analogize to this. So the first seven — which are attributes of meanings (sifāt al-ma‘ānī) — became causes for these, i.e., necessary concomitants (malzūmāt) for them. Therefore these were attributed to those, and it was said of them: moral attributes. And for this reason these are seven like the first. So the yā’ in the word (al-ma‘nawiyyah) is the yā’ of attribution; they are attributed to (al-ma‘nā), and the wāw in it is a substitute for the alif in (al-ma‘nā). And they are: His being Almighty – able, willing, knowing, living, hearing, seeing, and speaking. Since these moral attributes are concomitant with the attributes of meanings, he arranged them according to the arrangement of those: His being Almighty able is concomitant with the first of the attributes of meanings — the power subsisting in His essence; His being Mighty and Exalted willing is concomitant with the will subsisting in His essence, Blessed and Exalted; His being Almighty knowing is concomitant with the knowledge subsisting in His essence; His being Almighty living is concomitant with the life subsisting in His essence; His being Almighty hearing is concomitant with the hearing subsisting in His essence; His being Almighty seeing is concomitant with the sight subsisting in His essence; and His being Almighty speaking is concomitant with the speech subsisting in His essence. And know: that their enumeration of these seven among the attributes is in a real sense if we affirm the states (aḥwāl) — which are positive attributes (ṣifāt thubūtiyyah) neither existent nor non-existent, subsisting in an existent. Then these moral attributes are on this view fixed attributes subsisting in His essence. But if we deny the states, and that there is no intermediary between existence and non-existence — which is the school of Shaykh al-Ash‘arī — then only the first seven which are the attributes of meanings are the established attributes subsisting in the essence; and as for these, like
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the seven 'moral attributes' (al-ṣifāt al-ma‘nawiyyah) and examines their ontological reality according to two theological perspectives. The author begins by stating that these seven attributes are concomitant with the first seven attributes (ṣifāt al-ma‘ānī: power, will, knowledge, life, hearing, sight, speech). They are called 'moral' because being described by them depends on being described by the first seven: for example, you cannot say something is 'able' unless power actually subsists in it. Thus the first seven are the 'causes' (or necessary presumptions) of these secondary descriptions. The author then notes that the word 'ma‘nawiyyah' is a relative adjective derived from 'ma‘nā', with a substituted wāw. He then lists the seven: God's being able (qādir), willing (murīd), knowing (‘ālim), living (ḥayy), hearing (samī‘), seeing (baṣīr), and speaking (mutakallim). The author then shows the direct correspondence: each moral attribute is the necessary concomitant of the corresponding attribute of meaning (e.g., being able follows power). Finally, he discusses the reality of these moral attributes. If we affirm the concept of 'states' (aḥwāl) — attributes that are neither existent nor non-existent but subsist in the essence — then these moral attributes are real, fixed attributes. But if we deny states, following Imam al-Ash‘arī's view that there is no intermediary existence, then only the first seven attributes of meanings are real; the moral attributes are not additional entities but mere descriptions. The text ends mid-sentence with 'as for these, like...', suggesting further explanation on the following page.

Correction: In fact, they are dependent descriptions whose existence is logically consequent upon the first seven attributes.

The Ontological Status of Moral Attributes: States or Descriptions?
Sanusi presents two ontological views on moral attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash‘arī debate about whether the moral attributes are real attributes (states) or merely conceptual descriptions.

Core Idea
Moral attributes are real if we affirm states (aḥwāl); otherwise they are only descriptions.

After listing the moral attributes, Sanusi delves into a deeper theological question: do these attributes have an independent reality? The answer depends on whether one affirms the theory of states (aḥwāl) — attributes that are neither existent nor non-existent — or follows Imam al-Ash‘arī in denying any intermediary between existence and non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on ontological status
Read the Arabic and translation for the section beginning with 'Wa‘lam'. This part discusses the reality of the moral attributes.
The passage ends abruptly; we have only the beginning of the Ash‘arī view.
2
Understand the theory of states (aḥwāl)
Grasp what 'states' are: positive attributes that are neither existent nor non-existent but subsist in an existent. If states are affirmed, the moral attributes are real.
3
Contrast with the Ash‘arī position
If states are denied, there is no intermediary; then only the first seven attributes are real, and the moral attributes are not additional entities.
The text cuts off before finishing the Ash‘arī view, but the direction is clear.

Then seven attributes called 'moral attributes' (sifāt ma‘nawiyyah), and they are concomitant with the first seven. They were named moral attributes only because being qualified by them is a branch of being qualified by the first seven. For the qualification of any locus (mahall) by being knowing or able, for example, is not valid unless knowledge or power subsists in it. And analogize to this. So the first seven — which are attributes of meanings (sifāt al-ma‘ānī) — became causes for these, i.e., necessary concomitants (malzūmāt) for them. Therefore these were attributed to those, and it was said of them: moral attributes. And for this reason these are seven like the first. So the yā’ in the word (al-ma‘nawiyyah) is the yā’ of attribution; they are attributed to (al-ma‘nā), and the wāw in it is a substitute for the alif in (al-ma‘nā). And they are: His being Almighty – able, willing, knowing, living, hearing, seeing, and speaking. Commentary
This passage defines and explains the seven 'moral attributes' (al-ṣifāt al-ma‘nawiyyah) and examines their ontological reality according to two theological perspectives. The author begins by stating that these seven attributes are concomitant with the first seven attributes (ṣifāt al-ma‘ānī: power, will, knowledge, life, hearing, sight, speech). They are called 'moral' because being described by them depends on being described by the first seven: for example, you cannot say something is 'able' unless power actually subsists in it. Thus the first seven are the 'causes' (or necessary presumptions) of these secondary descriptions. The author then notes that the word 'ma‘nawiyyah' is a relative adjective derived from 'ma‘nā', with a substituted wāw. He then lists the seven: God's being able (qādir), willing (murīd), knowing (‘ālim), living (ḥayy), hearing (samī‘), seeing (baṣīr), and speaking (mutakallim). The author then shows the direct correspondence: each moral attribute is the necessary concomitant of the corresponding attribute of meaning (e.g., being able follows power). Finally, he discusses the reality of these moral attributes. If we affirm the concept of 'states' (aḥwāl) — attributes that are neither existent nor non-existent but subsist in the essence — then these moral attributes are real, fixed attributes. But if we deny states, following Imam al-Ash‘arī's view that there is no intermediary existence, then only the first seven attributes of meanings are real; the moral attributes are not additional entities but mere descriptions. The text ends mid-sentence with 'as for these, like...', suggesting further explanation on the following page.

The Seven Meaningful Attributes and Their Necessary Concomitance
Sanusi enumerates the seven meaningful attributes and their necessary relationship to the attributes of meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seven meaningful attributes of God and how each is necessarily entailed by the corresponding attribute of meaning.

Core Idea
The seven meaningful attributes are necessary effects of the seven attributes of meanings.

Sanusi begins by establishing a crucial link: the meaningful attributes (like being Powerful, Knowing, etc.) are not separate from the attributes of meanings (Power, Knowledge, etc.) but are their necessary concomitants. In this passage, he lists each one and shows how they follow from the foundational attributes in the divine essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the pattern: each meaningful attribute is described as 'necessary' (lazim) for a specific attribute of meaning.
The word 'lazim' is key – it means a necessary concomitant.
2
Match each meaningful attribute to its source
Identify the seven pairs: (1) being Powerful (Qadir) from Power, (2) being Willing (Murid) from Will, (3) being Knowing ('Alim) from Knowledge, (4) being Living (Hayy) from Life, (5) being Hearing (Sami') from Hearing, (6) being Seeing (Basir) from Sight, (7) being Speaking (Mutakallim) from Speech.
Note the order mirrors the attributes of meanings.
3
Reflect on the concept of 'luzum' (necessity)
Consider why these relationships are necessary. How does this help us understand that God's attributes are not arbitrary but internally consistent?
This necessity is logical, not temporal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لمَّا كانَتْ هذه الصفاتُ المعنويَّةُ لازمة لصفاتِ المعاني .. رتَّبَها على حسب ترتيب تلكَ ؛ فكونُهُ تعالى قادراً لازم للصفة الأولى مِنْ صفات المعاني ؛ وهي القدرةُ القائمة بذاتِهِ تعالى، وكونه جل وعزَّ مريداً لازم للإرادةِ القائمة بذاتِهِ تبارك وتعالى، وكونه تعالى عالماً لازم للعلم القائم بذاتِهِ تعالى، وكونه تعالى حيّاً لازم للحياة القائمة بذاتِهِ تعالى، وكونه تعالى سميعاً لازم للسمع القائم بذاتِهِ تعالى، وكونه تعالى بصيراً لازم للبصرِ القائم بذاتِهِ تعالى، وكونه تعالى متكلّماً لازم للكلام القائم بذاتِهِ تعالى. واعلم : أنَّ عدَّهم لهذهِ السبع في الصفاتِ هو على سبيل الحقيقة إن قلنا بثبوتِ الأحوالِ؛ وهي صفات ثبوتية ليست بموجودة ولا معدومة، تقوم بموجود، فتكونُ هذه الصفات المعنوية على هذا صفات ثابتة قائمة بذاتِهِ تعالى. وأما إن قلنا بنفي الأحوالِ، وأنَّهُ لا واسطة بين الوجودِ والعدمِ هو مذهب الشيخ الأشعري .. فالثابتُ مِنَ الصفاتِ التي تقومُ بالذاتِ إِنَّما هو السبع الأولى التي هي صفاتُ المعاني، وأمَّا هذه عن قيام تلك بالذات، لا أنَّ لهذه ثبوتاً في الخارج فعبارة الذهن.
Since these meaningful attributes are necessary concomitants of the attributes of meanings, they are arranged according to the order of those: So His being, may He be exalted, powerful is necessary for the first attribute of the attributes of meanings, which is the power subsistent in His essence, may He be exalted; and His being, mighty and majestic, willing is necessary for the will subsistent in His essence, blessed and exalted; and His being, may He be exalted, knowing is necessary for the knowledge subsistent in His essence, may He be exalted; and His being, may He be exalted, living is necessary for the life subsistent in His essence, may He be exalted; and His being, may He be exalted, hearing is necessary for the hearing subsistent in His essence, may He be exalted; and His being, may He be exalted, seeing is necessary for the sight subsistent in His essence, may He be exalted; and His being, may He be exalted, speaking is necessary for the speech subsistent in His essence, may He be exalted. And know: that their counting of these seven among the attributes is in the sense of reality if we affirm the states, and they are attributes of affirmation that are neither existent nor non-existent, subsisting in an existent, so these meaningful attributes according to this are established attributes subsistent in His essence. But if we deny the states, and that there is no intermediary between existence and non-existence — and this is the doctrine of Shaykh al-Ash'ari — then the established among the attributes that subsist in the essence is only the first seven, which are the attributes of meanings, and these are on account of the subsistence of those in the essence, not that these have subsistence in the external, so they are mental expressions.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage explains the seven 'meaningful attributes' (الصفات المعنوية) and their ontological status. The author first lists each meaningful attribute as a necessary concomitant of the corresponding attribute of meanings (صفات المعاني): His being Powerful follows from Power, His being Willing from Will, His being Knowing from Knowledge, His being Living from Life, His being Hearing from Hearing, His being Seeing from Sight, and His being Speaking from Speech. The order mirrors the order of the attributes of meanings. The author then clarifies the reality of these meaningful attributes depending on one's position on the 'states' (الأحوال). If one affirms the reality of states — attributes that are neither existent nor non-existent but subsist in an existent — then these meaningful attributes are real, established attributes subsisting in the essence of God. If one denies states, following the doctrine of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, then no intermediary between existence and non-existence is posited. In that case, only the first seven attributes of meanings truly subsist in the essence, and the meaningful attributes are merely mental considerations (اعتبارات ذهنية) that follow from the subsistence of the attributes of meanings, without having independent external existence. Thus the passage presents two positions: one affirms the reality of meaningful attributes as distinct entities, the other treats them as conceptual concomitants.

The Ontological Status of Meaningful Attributes: States vs. Mental Considerations
Sanusi explains the ontological status of meaningful attributes depending on the doctrine of states (ahwal).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two positions on the reality of the meaningful attributes: one affirms them as real states, the other treats them as mental constructs.

Core Idea
Whether meaningful attributes are real or mental depends on the doctrine of states (ahwal).

After listing the meaningful attributes, Sanusi now addresses their ontological status. The key is the concept of 'states' (ahwal) — intermediary entities that are neither existent nor non-existent. He presents two views: that of those who affirm states, making the meaningful attributes real, and that of Imam al-Ash'ari, who denies states and considers them mere mental expressions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Look for the conditional statements: 'if we affirm the states' and 'if we deny the states'. These introduce the two positions.
The first position is often associated with the Mu'tazila or some later Ash'aris, while the second is attributed to Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari.
2
Understand the definition of a 'state' (ḥāl)
A state is described as an attribute that is neither existent nor non-existent, subsisting in an existent. How does this intermediary concept allow meaningful attributes to be real?
The state is a 'third thing' between existence and non-existence.
3
Contrast the consequences
If states are affirmed, the meaningful attributes are real entities. If denied, they are merely mental considerations (i'tibarat dhihniyya) that follow from the real existence of the attributes of meanings.
This difference is subtle but important in Islamic theology.

This passage explains the seven 'meaningful attributes' (الصفات المعنوية) and their ontological status. If one affirms the reality of states — attributes that are neither existent nor non-existent but subsist in an existent — then these meaningful attributes are real, established attributes subsisting in the essence of God. If one denies states, following the doctrine of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, then no intermediary between existence and non-existence is posited. In that case, only the first seven attributes of meanings truly subsist in the essence, and the meaningful attributes are merely mental considerations (اعتبارات ذهنية) that follow from the subsistence of the attributes of meanings, without having independent external existence. Thus the passage presents two positions: one affirms the reality of meaningful attributes as distinct entities, the other treats them as conceptual concomitants.

Key Terms
الأحوال
ثبوت الأحوال
نفي الأحوال
مذهب الشيخ الأشعري
اعتبار ذهني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the Ash'ari position denies the meaningful attributes entirely.
Correction: Ash'ari denies their independent external existence but affirms them as mental considerations that are valid expressions of the attributes of meanings.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consequence of denying states (ahwal)?
A) The meaningful attributes are real.
B) Only the attributes of meanings are real; meaningful attributes are mental expressions.
C) Both sets of attributes are equally real.
D) The meaningful attributes become nonexistent.

Key Takeaway
Depending on the doctrine of states, the seven meaningful attributes are either real entities subsisting in God's essence or mere mental considerations derived from the attributes of meanings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on God's attributes like mercy and justice, do you treat them as independent realities that constrain God, or as mental considerations that describe His perfect actions without limit? How does your answer affect your trust in His absolute freedom and wisdom?
?
The Ash'ari position denies any ontological middle ground between existence and non-existence for divine attributes. Have you ever caught yourself imagining God's attributes as separate entities 'added to' His essence, like human qualities? How can this lesson's distinction protect you from subtle anthropomorphism in your worship and prayer?
?
If the meaningful attributes are merely mental considerations, that means our understanding of God's attributes is always filtered through human concepts. How does this humbling truth challenge your confidence in your own theological opinions and open you to greater reliance on revelation?

The Ash'ari View on Attribute Subsistence
Commentary clarifies the Ash'ari position on attribute subsistence as a verbal dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position that attribute subsistence is a mental consideration, making the disagreement with other schools a verbal dispute.

Core Idea
The Ash'ari affirms attributes but denies their external existence – a verbal dispute, not a real one.

This passage clarifies a subtle but crucial point in Ash'ari theology: how do attributes subsist with the divine essence? The commentary explains that Ash'ari does not deny attributes; rather, he sees their subsistence as a mental consideration, not an extra entity in the external world. The disagreement with others is thus verbal, not substantive. Let us read the source.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Familiarize yourself with the key terms and overall flow.
Notice the phrase 'فالخلاف لفظي' – the verbal nature of the dispute.
2
Identify the core claim
What does al-Ash'ari actually affirm? The passage states attributes are 'amr i'tibari' (mental consideration) and that he does not deny them entirely.
Contrast with the view that attributes are external existents.
3
Analyze the example of power
The example of power (qudra) shows the difference: Ash'ari holds one attribute subsists mentally; others hold two external entities. See how this illustrates the verbal dispute.
4
Reflect on the 'benefit'
The benefit discusses moral attributes (ma'nawiyya) and why they have no relation (ta'allaq). Understand the logic based on the state (haal) doctrine.
This is an additional nuance; not required to grasp the main point.
5
Summarize the takeaway
Conclude that the disagreement is verbal, not doctrinal. The Ash'ari position is not a denial of attributes but a specific ontological claim about their subsistence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عن قيام تلك بالذات، لا أن لهذه ثبوتاً في الخارج، فعبارة الذهن، أي: ليست بزائدة عليها في الخارج، وإنما هي أمر اعتباري، والأشعري لا ينفيها من أصلها، وإلا لكفر من ينفي ثبوتها في الخارج، فالخلاف لفظي؛ فعلى مذهبه: لم يقم بالذات في صورة القدرة مثلاً إلا صفة واحدة: القدرة، وقيامها بالذات أمر اعتباري لها، وعلى مذهب غيره: يقوم بالذات وهي شيئان ثابتان في الخارج: القدرة والقادرية. انتهى شيخنا عطية. فائدة: المعنوية على القول بثبوتها لا تعلق لها؛ اكتفاء بتعلق المعاني، وأيضاً: التعلق حال، والحال لا يثبت للحال.
Concerning the subsistence of those (attributes) with the essence, not that they have existence in the external, it is an expression of the mind. That is, they are not additional to it in the external, but are a mental consideration. And al-Ash'ari does not deny them entirely; otherwise he would deem disbelievers those who deny their existence in the external. So the disagreement is verbal. According to his school: nothing subsists with the essence in the form of power, for example, except one attribute: power, and its subsistence with the essence is a mental consideration for it. And according to the school of others: there subsists with the essence two things fixed in the external: power and being powerful. End of our teacher 'Atiyya. Benefit: The moral (attribute) according to the position of its existence has no relation, as a sufficiency due to the relation of the meanings, and also: relation is a state, and a state is not established for a state.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining the Ash'ari position on the subsistence of divine attributes with the essence. It begins by stating that the attributes are not additional existents in the external world but are mental considerations. The Ash'ari does not deny the attributes themselves; otherwise he would declare takfir on those who deny their external existence. Thus the disagreement with other schools is verbal, not substantive. The passage gives the example of the attribute of power: in the Ash'ari view, only one attribute (power) subsists with the essence, and its subsistence is a mental consideration. In the other view, two things subsist: power and being powerful (the attribute and the attribute's being attributed). The passage then adds a benefit: moral attributes, if considered existent, have no relation (ta'allaq) because the relation of the meanings suffices, and also because relation is a state (haal) and a state cannot be established for a state.

Key Terms
قيام
الاعتبار
الخلاف اللفظي
المعنوية
التعلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Ash'ari rejects divine attributes entirely, denying their reality.
Correction: The passage shows that al-Ash'ari affirms attributes but considers their subsistence a mental consideration, not an extra external entity. He would even declare takfir on those who totally deny them.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what is the nature of the disagreement between al-Ash'ari and others on attribute subsistence?
A) A real doctrinal difference
B) A verbal dispute
C) A disagreement about the number of attributes
D) A disagreement about God's essence

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari position on attribute subsistence is that it is a mental consideration, not an external existence, making the disagreement with opponents a verbal one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider Allah's attribute of power (qudra), do you treat it as a mental concept or a tangible reality that should shape your trust and actions? What would change if you truly believed it is as real as the world you see?
?
The passage calls the disagreement 'verbal.' Think of a time you argued about religion. Could the conflict have been merely verbal? Are you willing to investigate your own assumptions to find common ground, or do you cling to being right?
?
Al-Ash'ari's position protects against denying attributes entirely. Ask yourself: In your daily life, do you ever reduce Allah's attributes to empty words, losing their weight? What one practice can you start today to make His power and mercy more present to you?

Impossibility of Allah Resembling Created Things
Sanusi enumerates the modes of resemblance that are impossible for Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific ways in which Allah cannot resemble created beings and why this is essential to tawhid.

Core Idea
Allah transcends all forms of resemblance to creation.

Sanusi begins by establishing that Allah is absolutely unique and cannot resemble any created thing. He then enumerates the specific ways in which resemblance would be impossible, covering aspects such as being a body, accident, direction, restriction by time/place, and more.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own listing of modes come through.
2
Identify each mode of resemblance
The passage lists several modes: being a body, accident, in a direction, having a direction, restricted by place/time, qualified by originated attributes, described with smallness/bigness, described with purposes. Find each one.
3
Reflect on the negation of each mode
For each mode, ask yourself: why is it impossible for Allah? This reinforces tawhid.
Use the commentary to understand the implications.
4
Summarize the key principle
After studying, write a one-sentence summary of why Allah cannot resemble created things.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
استحالة المماثلة للحوادثِ ] وَالْمُمَاثَلَةُ لِلْحَوَادِثِ ؛ بأَنْ يَكُونَ جِرْماً ؛ أَيْ : تَأْخُذُ ذَاتُهُ الْعَلِيَّةُ قَدْراً مِنَ الْفَرَاغِ ، أَوْ يَكُونَ عَرَضاً يَقُومُ بِالْحِرْمِ ، أَوْ يَكُونَ فِي جِهَةٍ لِلْجِرْمِ ، أَوْ لَهُ هُوَ جِهَةٌ ، أَوْ يَتَقَيَّدَ بِمَكَانِ أَوْ زَمَانٍ ، أَوْ تَتَّصِفَ ذَاتُهُ الْعَلِيَّةُ بِالْحَوَادِثِ ، أَوْ يَتَّصِفَ بالصِّغَرِ أَوِ الْكِبَرِ ، أَوْ يَتَّصِفَ بِالْأَغْرَاضِ فِي الْأَفْعَالِ وَالْأَحْكَامِ . حقيقة المثلينِ : هما الأمران المتساويان في جميع صفاتِ النفس ؛ وهي التي لا تتقرَّرُ حقيقةُ الذاتِ بدونها ، فالمتساويان في بعض صفاتِ النفس أو في العرضيَّاتِ ؛ وهي الصفات الخارجة عن حقيقة الذات . . ليسا بمثلين . فزيدٌ مثلاً إِنَّما يماثلُهُ مَنْ ساواه في جميع صفاته النفسية ؛ وهي كونه حيواناً ذا نفس ناطقةٍ ؛ أي : مفكّرة بالقوَّةِ ، أما ما ساواه في بعضها ؛ كالفرس الذي ساواه في مجرَّدِ الحيوانية فقط .. فليسَ مِثْلاً له ، وكذا ما ساواه في الصفاتِ العرضيَّاتِ ؛ كالبياض الذي ساواه في الحدوثِ وصحة الرؤية ونحو ذلك .. فليس أيضاً مثلاً لهُ
Impossibility of resembling created things. And resembling created things is that He be a body, i.e., His lofty essence occupies a measure of space, or that He be an accident subsisting in a body, or that He be in a direction relative to a body, or that He have a direction, or that He be restricted by place or time, or that His lofty essence be qualified by originated things, or that He be described with smallness or bigness, or that He be described with purposes in actions and judgments. The reality of the two similars: they are two things equal in all essential attributes (ṣifāt al-nafs); these are the attributes without which the reality of the essence is not constituted. Thus, two things equal in some essential attributes or in accidental attributes (al-ʿaraḍiyyāt) – which are attributes external to the reality of the essence – are not two similars. For example, Zaid resembles only someone who equals him in all his essential attributes; these are that he is an animal possessing a rational soul, i.e., potentially thinking. As for what equals him in some of them, like a horse which equals him in mere animality only, it is not similar to him. Likewise, what equals him in accidental attributes, like whiteness which equals him in temporality and the possibility of being seen and the like, it also is not similar to him.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage defines the impossibility of Allah resembling created things and clarifies the true meaning of similars. It begins by listing the ways in which resemblance would occur: if Allah were a body, an accident, in a direction, restricted by place/time, qualified by originated attributes, or described with smallness/bigness or purposes. All these are negated of Allah. Then it defines the reality of two similars (mithlayn): they are two things that are equal in all essential attributes (ṣifāt al-nafs), i.e., those without which the essence is not constituted. Equality in only some essential attributes or in accidental attributes does not constitute similars. The author gives examples: Zaid and a horse share animality but not rational soul, so not similars. Whiteness shares temporality with Zaid but not essence, so not similars.

حوادث
جرم
عرض
جهة
صغر
كبر
أغراض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Allah can be described with some attributes of creation, like being in a direction or having a body.
Correction: Sanusi lists these modes precisely to show they are all impossible for Allah, affirming His absolute uniqueness.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, which of the following is NOT a mode of resemblance that is impossible for Allah?
A) Being a body
B) Being restricted by place or time
C) Being all-knowing
D) Being described with smallness or bigness

Key Takeaway
Allah is absolutely unique and cannot resemble any created thing in any of the enumerated ways.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of Allah, do you ever unconsciously imagine Him as having a form or location, like being 'up there'? How does this passage challenge that tendency, and what step can you take today to correct your mental image?
?
In your daily life, have you ever described Allah with a human quality (like 'getting angry' in a temporal sense) without realizing it implies change? Will you commit to using only the names and attributes revealed in Quran and Sunnah, avoiding anthropomorphic interpretations?
?
The passage negates that Allah has purposes in actions and judgments. Do you sometimes assume Allah's commands are for His own benefit? How can you shift your mindset to see that all divine commands are for our good, not His need?

Definition of True Similars (Mithlayn)
Sanusi defines true similars and illustrates with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of two things being similar (mithlayn) and how this applies to creatures but not to Allah.

Core Idea
True similarity requires equality in all essential attributes, not just some.

After negating resemblance to created things, Sanusi clarifies what true similars are. He defines them as two things equal in all essential attributes (ṣifāt al-nafs) and illustrates with examples of Zaid, a horse, and whiteness.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of mithlayn
Read the passage that defines true similars. Note the key term 'essential attributes' (ṣifāt al-nafs).
2
Understand the distinction between essential and accidental attributes
Sanusi distinguishes between essential attributes (without which the essence is not constituted) and accidental attributes (external to the essence). This is crucial.
3
Examine the examples
The examples of Zaid and a horse (share animality but not rational soul) and Zaid and whiteness (share temporality but not essence) clarify the definition.
4
Apply the concept
Think of other examples: two humans are similars because they share all essential attributes; a human and a tree are not.

God's Self-Subsistence: Independence from Locus and Particularizer
Sanusi defines and proves the necessity of God's self-subsistence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God must be self-subsistent, meaning He cannot be an accident needing a substrate nor a possible being needing a particularizer.

Core Idea
God's self-subsistence means absolute independence from both a locus and a particularizer.

Sanusi now turns to a necessary attribute of God: self-subsistence (qiyam bi nafsihi). He has already defined it earlier, but here he reinforces its necessity by eliminating two alternatives: that God could be an accident subsisting in a substrate, or that He could be a possible being requiring a particularizer. This passage grounds the divine attribute of independence (ghina mutlaq).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let Sanusi's concise style settle before interpreting.
Notice the structure: impossibility statement, recall of definition, two-part explanation, conclusion.
2
Identify the two impossibilities
The passage says God cannot be an attribute subsisting in a locus, nor can He need a particularizer. Underline these two points in the translation.
These correspond to the two parts of the definition of self-subsistence: independence from a locus and from a particularizer.
3
Understand why each is impossible
Work through each part: why can't God be a 'meaning' (accident)? Because accidents need a substrate. Why can't He be possible? Because possible beings need a particularizer. The passage gives the reason for each.
The key is that God is 'wajib al-qidam wa al-baqa' (necessarily pre-eternal and everlasting).
4
Grasp the conclusion
The passage concludes that only God has absolute independence. Reflect on how this attribute distinguishes the Creator from all creatures.
This is a foundational point in Islamic theology about tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَكَذَا يَسْتَحِيلُ عَلَيْهِ تَعَالَى أَلا يَكُونَ قَائِماً بِنَفْسِهِ ؛ بأَنْ يَكُونَ صِفَةٌ يَقُومُ بِمَحَلٌ ، أَوْ يَحْتَاجَ إِلَىٰ مُخَصِّصٍ عنِ قد عرفت فيما سبق معنى قيامِهِ تعالى بنفسه ، وأنَّهُ عبارةٌ استغنائه تعالى عنِ المحل والمخصص ؛ أي : ليس تعالى معنى مِنَ المعاني ؛ أي : الأشياء التي ليسَتْ بذوات ، فيحتاج إلى محل ؛ أي : ذات يقومُ بها ، وليسَ أيضاً جلَّ وعزَّ بجائز العدمِ فيحتاج إلى المخصّص ؛ أي : الفاعل الذي يخصِّص كل جائز ببعض ما جازَ عليه ، بل هو جلَّ وعزَّ واجب القدم والبقاء ، لا تقبلُ ذاتُهُ العليَّةُ ولا صفاته المرفّعةُ العدمَ أصلاً ، فهو المنفرد بالغنى المطلق وحده تبارك وتعالى
And likewise it is impossible for Him, Exalted, that He not be self-subsistent; such that He be an attribute subsisting in a locus, or that He need a particularizer. You have already known previously the meaning of His self-subsistence, Exalted, and that it is an expression of His independence, Exalted, from a locus and a particularizer; that is, He is not a meaning among meanings; that is, among things that are not self-subsistent entities, so they need a locus; that is, a substrate in which they subsist. And also He, Majestic and Mighty, is not possible of non-existence, so He would need a particularizer; that is, the agent who specifies each possible thing with some of what is possible for it. Rather, He, Majestic and Mighty, is necessarily pre-eternal and everlasting; His supreme essence and His exalted attributes do not accept non-existence at all. So He alone is unique in absolute independence, Blessed and Exalted.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof of the impossibility of God not being self-subsistent (qa'im bi nafsihi). It begins by stating that it is impossible for God to not be self-subsistent, meaning He cannot be an attribute subsisting in a substrate (mahall) or need a particularizer (mukhassis). The author then recalls the previously defined meaning of God's self-subsistence: it is His independence from both a locus and a particularizer. The explanation proceeds in two parts. First, God is not a 'meaning' (ma'na), i.e., an accident that subsists in a substance (dhat). Second, God is not a possible being (ja'iz al-'adam) that would require a particularizer to specify its existence. Instead, God is necessarily existent (wajib al-qidam wa al-baqa'), and neither His essence nor His attributes can accept non-existence. The conclusion emphasizes that only God possesses absolute independence (ghina mutlaq).

Misconception: Some think that God's self-subsistence only means not needing a locus.
Correction: The passage clarifies it also means not needing a particularizer; God is not a possible being.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does God not need a particularizer (مُخَصِّص)?
A) Because He is not a possible being
B) Because He is an attribute
C) Because He has a locus
D) Because He is a meaning

Key Takeaway
God's self-subsistence means He is independent from both a locus and a particularizer, establishing His absolute uniqueness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
