?
The passage says God is not an attribute needing a locus—He is not confined to any place. Do you live as if God is present only in religious settings, or do you acknowledge His omnipresence in every moment of your day? What would change if you truly believed He is with you right now, without any limitation?
?
If God does not need a particularizer to bring Him into existence, then nothing in the universe can be truly random or accidental. How does this truth challenge any secret doubt you have about the purpose of your own life? Write down one area where you feel your existence lacks direction and commit to asking God for guidance in it.

Allah's Necessary Self-Subsistence
Sanusi proves that Allah must be self-subsistent and defines its meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what it means for Allah to be self-subsistent (qā'im bi-nafsih) and why it is rationally necessary.

Core Idea
Allah's self-subsistence means independence from both a locus and a specifier.

In this passage, Sanusi reinforces the foundational attribute of self-subsistence. He argues that denying this attribute would reduce Allah to a contingent accident or a possible being. By understanding what Allah is not, we affirm His absolute independence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a condensed logical style.
2
Identify the two things Allah is independent from
The passage states that if Allah were not self-subsistent, He would either be an attribute needing a locus or a possible being needing a specifier. Identify these two conditions.
These are the key components of the definition.
3
Understand the key terms
Sanusi explains 'meaning' (ma'nā) as something that is not a self-subsistent essence, and 'possible of non-existence' as requiring a specifier. Grasp these definitions.
These terms are central to the argument.
4
Follow the logical conclusion
After showing what Allah is not, Sanusi affirms that Allah is necessary of existence and absolutely self-sufficient. See how the negative leads to positive affirmation.
This is a via negativa argument.
5
Reflect on the takeaway
Consider why this attribute is fundamental in Islamic theology. It establishes that Allah depends on nothing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَكَذَا يَسْتَحِيلُ عَلَيْهِ تَعَالَى أَلا يَكُونَ قَائِماً بِنَفْسِهِ ؛ بأَنْ يَكُونَ صِفَةٌ يَقُومُ بِمَحَلٌ ، أَوْ يَحْتَاجَ إِلَىٰ مُخَصِّصٍ . قد عرفت فيما سبق معنى قيامِهِ تعالى بنفسه ، وأنَّهُ عبارةٌ استغنائه تعالى عنِ المحل والمخصص ؛ أي : ليس تعالى معنى مِنَ المعاني ؛ أي : الأشياء التي ليسَتْ بذوات ، فيحتاج إلى محل ؛ أي : ذات يقومُ بها ، وليسَ أيضاً جلَّ وعزَّ بجائز العدمِ فيحتاج إلى المخصّص ؛ أي : الفاعل الذي يخصِّص كل جائز ببعض ما جازَ عليه ، بل هو جلَّ وعزَّ واجب القدم والبقاء ، لا تقبلُ ذاتُهُ العليَّةُ ولا صفاته المرفّعةُ العدمَ أصلاً ، فهو المنفرد بالغنى المطلق وحده تبارك وتعالى
And likewise it is impossible for Him, exalted, not to be self-subsistent, such that He would be an attribute subsisting in a locus, or would need a specifier. You have already known previously the meaning of His being self-subsistent, and that it is an expression of His independence, exalted, from a locus and a specifier; i.e., He is not a meaning among meanings—i.e., things that are not essences—so as to need a locus, i.e., an essence in which to subsist; and also He is not, may He be glorified and exalted, possible of non-existence, so as to need a specifier—i.e., the agent who specifies every possible thing with some of what is possible for it. Rather He, may He be glorified and exalted, is necessary of pre-eternity and perpetuity; His sublime essence and His exalted attributes do not admit non-existence at all. So He alone is uniquely possessed of absolute self-sufficiency, blessed and exalted.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the necessity of Allah being self-subsistent (qā'im bi-nafsih). The author states that it is impossible for Allah not to be self-subsistent, because if He were not, He would either be an attribute needing a locus (an essence to inhere in) or a contingent being needing a specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ) to bring it into existence. He then recalls the earlier definition: being self-subsistent means independence from both a locus and a specifier. He explains that a 'meaning' (ma'nā) is something that is not a self-subsistent essence (dhāt) and therefore requires a locus. Similarly, a 'possible being' (jā'iz al-'adam) requires a specifier to determine its existence. Allah, however, is necessary of existence (wājib al-qidam wa al-baqā') and His essence and attributes do not accept non-existence. Therefore, He is uniquely and absolutely self-sufficient. The argument follows a via negativa: it shows what Allah must not be, and then affirms the positive attribute.

Key Terms
قيام بنفسه
مخصِّص
معنى
ذات
جائز العدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Self-subsistence means only independence from a creator.
Correction: It also means independence from a locus (an essence to inhere in). An accident (attribute) needs a substance to exist in; Allah is not an accident.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what are the two things that Allah is independent from in being self-subsistent?
A) A locus and a specifier
B) A creator and a sustainer
C) Time and space
D) Knowledge and power

Key Takeaway
Allah's self-subsistence means He depends on nothing—not a locus nor a specifier—and is necessarily eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah is absolutely independent and does not need anything, how does that challenge your daily tendency to rely on created things as if they have independent power? What is one concrete situation today where you can replace that false reliance with trust in Allah alone?
?
The passage says Allah is not a 'meaning' that needs a locus. Have you ever pictured Allah as if He resides somewhere or needs something to sustain Him? What would it take to purify your mind from such limiting images and truly believe in His transcendence?
?
Consider that while you depend on food, air, and people, Allah depends on nothing—even His attributes are not separate from His essence. Does this truth cause you to feel overwhelmed by your own neediness? How can you use that feeling to increase your humility and gratitude, rather than despair?

The Three Impossibilities Against Divine Unity
Sanusi lists three impossible consequences of not being one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that Allah must be one by examining the three absurd alternatives to unity.

Core Idea
Allah cannot not be one; composition, likeness, or a partner are impossible.

Sanusi begins by stating that Allah cannot be other than one. This may seem like a simple faith statement, but it is grounded in rational necessity: if Allah were not one, He would have to be composite, have a like, or have a partner in action. Each possibility is logically impossible for the necessary being.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic sentence once, then the English translation. Notice the three impossible scenarios listed.
The passage is a single sentence but contains three distinct alternatives.
2
Identify the three impossibilities
List the three ways Allah would not be one: composition (tarkīb), having a likeness (mumāthil), and having a partner effective agent (mu'aththir).
3
Reflect on why each is impossible
Consider why each alternative contradicts the nature of Allah as necessary, independent, and exclusive creator. The commentary will help you understand the reasoning.
This reflection connects to the core idea of necessary unity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَكَذَا يَسْتَحِيلُ عَلَيْهِ تَعَالَى أَلا يَكُونَ وَاحِداً ، بأَنْ يَكُونَ مُرَكَّباً فِي ذَاتِهِ ، أَوْ يَكُونَ لَهُ مُمَائِلٌ فِي ذَاتِهِ أَوْ صِفَاتِهِ ، أَوْ يَكُونَ مَعَهُ فِي الْوُجُودِ مُؤَكِّرٌ فِي فِعْلٍ مِنَ الْأَفْعَالِ . قد عرفت أن أوجه الوحدانية ثلاثة : وحدانيَّةُ الذاتِ ، ووحدانية الصفات ، ووحدانية الأفعال ، وكلُّها واجبة لمولانا جل وعزَّ وحده . فوحدانية الذاتِ : تنفي التركيب في ذاتِهِ تعالى ، ووجودَ ذاتِ أخرى تماثلُ الذات العليَّةَ ، وبالجملة : فوحدانية الذاتِ تنفي التعدُّد في حقيقتها ، متصلاً كان أو منفصلاً . ووحدانية الصفاتِ : تنفي التعدُّدَ في حقيقة كل واحدة منها متصلاً كان أيضاً أو منفصلاً ؛ فعلمُ مولانا جل وعزَّ ليسَ لهُ ثانٍ يماثله ، لا متصلاً ؛ أي : قائماً بالذاتِ العليَّة ، ولا منفصلاً ؛ أي : قائماً بذات أخرى ، بل هو تعالى يعلمُ المعلومات التي لا نهاية لها بعلم واحدٍ لا عددَ له ولا ثاني له أصلاً ، وقس على هذا سائر صفاتِ مولانا جل وعز . ووحدانية الأفعالِ : تنفي أنْ يكونَ ثَمَّ اختراع لكل ما سواه جلَّ وعزَّ
And likewise it is impossible for Him, exalted, not to be one, such that He would be composite in His essence, or have a likeness in His essence or His attributes, or that there be with Him in existence an effective agent in any of the actions. You have already known that the aspects of unity are three: unity of essence, unity of attributes, and unity of actions, and all of them are necessary for our Lord, may He be glorified and exalted, alone. So unity of essence negates composition in His essence, and the existence of another essence similar to the sublime essence. In short, unity of essence negates multiplicity in its reality, whether connected or separate. And unity of attributes negates multiplicity in the reality of each one of them, whether connected or separate; so the knowledge of our Lord, may He be glorified and exalted, has no second similar to it, neither connected—i.e., subsisting in the sublime essence—nor separate—i.e., subsisting in another essence. Rather, He knows infinite objects of knowledge with a single knowledge that has no number and no second at all. And apply this to the rest of the attributes of our Lord, may He be glorified and exalted. And unity of actions negates that there be any invention for everything other than Him, may He be glorified and exalted.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the necessity of Allah being one (wāḥid). The author first states that it is impossible for Allah not to be one, meaning that He cannot be composite in essence, cannot have a like (mumāthil) in essence or attributes, and cannot have a partner effective agent (mu'aththir) in any action. Then he recalls that the aspects of unity (wujūh al-waḥdāniyya) are three: unity of essence (waḥdāniyyat al-dhāt), unity of attributes (waḥdāniyyat al-ṣifāt), and unity of actions (waḥdāniyyat al-af'āl). All are necessary for Allah alone. He explains each: Unity of essence negates composition (tarkīb) within Allah's essence and the existence of another essence similar to it—it negates multiplicity in the reality (ḥaqīqa) of the essence, whether the multiplicity is 'connected' (muttasil, i.e., internal composition) or 'separate' (munfaṣil, i.e., a separate similar entity). Unity of attributes negates multiplicity in the reality of each attribute; for example, Allah's knowledge has no second similar to it, neither a second knowledge subsisting in His own essence (connected) nor a second knowledge subsisting in another essence (separate). He knows infinite objects of knowledge with a single knowledge that has no number or second. The same applies to all other attributes. Unity of actions negates that any other agent independently invents (ikhtirā') anything besides Allah. The proof structure is: impossibility of not being one is supported by listing the absurd alternatives (composition, likeness, partner), then the positive doctrine of threefold unity is explained. The threefold division is standard in Ash'ari kalam.

Key Terms
ممائل
تركيب
مؤثر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'not being one' only means having multiple parts, not considering the other forms of multiplicity.
Correction: The passage clarifies that not being one can take three forms: composition, having a likeness, or having a partner in actions. All are impossible for Allah.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT listed as an impossibility if Allah were not one?
A) Allah would be composite in His essence
B) Allah would have a likeness in His essence or attributes
C) Allah would have a partner in some actions
D) Allah would have a son

Key Takeaway
Allah must be one; any form of multiplicity in essence, attributes, or agency is rationally impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself relying on a created cause as if it were an independent 'effective agent' with Allah, such as your wealth, health, or social status? Will you turn your heart to see Allah alone as the true source of all effects?
?
Have you imagined Allah in your mind as having a form or being similar to something you know, even in a fleeting thought? How will you repent from that and strive to worship Him as He truly is, without likeness?
?
When you feel that your own efforts are the primary cause of your success, are you not, in that moment, believing in a 'partner' in the act? How will you change today to attribute all outcomes solely to Allah?

The Three Aspects of Divine Unity
Sanusi enumerates and explains the three aspects of divine unity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three aspects of divine unity—essence, attributes, and actions—and explain what each negates.

Core Idea
Divine unity is threefold: essence, attributes, and actions are each singular and incomparable.

Having established that Allah must be one, Sanusi now unpacks what this oneness means. He divides unity into three categories: unity of essence, unity of attributes, and unity of actions. Each addresses a different potential form of multiplicity, and each is necessary for Allah alone.

Guided Path
1
Read the explanation of unity of essence
Read the Arabic and translation for unity of essence (waḥdāniyyat al-dhāt). Note that it negates composition and any separate similar essence.
Key terms: 'connected' (muttasil) means internal composition; 'separate' (munfaṣil) means a separate entity.
2
Read the explanation of unity of attributes
Read about unity of attributes. Pay attention to the example of knowledge: it has no second similar, whether within Allah or outside.
Apply this analogy to all other attributes.
3
Read the explanation of unity of actions
Read the brief statement on unity of actions. It negates any independent creator besides Allah.
4
Compare the three aspects
Consider how each aspect targets a different domain: essence (what Allah is), attributes (what Allah has), actions (what Allah does).
All three are necessary for complete tawḥīd.

The Three Aspects of Tawhid
Sanusi enumerates the three aspects of tawhid and explains each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three aspects of tawhid (oneness of Allah) and how each negates specific false conceptions about Allah's essence, attributes, and actions.

Core Idea
Tawhid is not just belief in one God; it requires negating composition, peer, and partner in every respect.

In this passage, Sanusi explains the three necessary aspects of Allah's oneness: Oneness of Essence, Oneness of Attributes, and Oneness of Actions. He begins by stating the impossibility of Allah not being one, then defines each aspect and shows what it negates. This lesson will clarify the depth of tawhid beyond mere monotheism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to get an overview of the three aspects.
Notice the structure: impossibility statement, enumeration, then individual explanations.
2
Identify the three aspects
Find the three named aspects: وحدانية الذات, وحدانية الصفات, وحدانية الأفعال. Understand what each negates.
3
Study the example of knowledge
Sanusi uses Allah's knowledge as an example for Oneness of Attributes. See how it negates any second similar knowledge.
Apply the same reasoning to all attributes.
4
Reflect on Oneness of Actions
Understand that Allah alone creates, and other beings' actions are metaphorical. This preserves His exclusive agency.

وَكَذَا يَسْتَحِيلُ عَلَيْهِ تَعَالَى أَلا يَكُونَ وَاحِداً ، بأَنْ يَكُونَ مُرَكَّباً فِي ذَاتِهِ ، أَوْ يَكُونَ لَهُ مُمَائِلٌ فِي ذَاتِهِ أَوْ صِفَاتِهِ ، أَوْ يَكُونَ مَعَهُ فِي الْوُجُودِ مُؤَكِّرٌ فِي فِعْلٍ مِنَ الْأَفْعَالِ . قد عرفت أن أوجه الوحدانية ثلاثة : وحدانيَّةُ الذاتِ ، ووحدانية الصفات ، ووحدانية الأفعال ، وكلُّها واجبة لمولانا جل وعزَّ وحده . فوحدانية الذاتِ : تنفي التركيب في ذاتِهِ تعالى ، ووجودَ ذاتِ أخرى تماثلُ الذات العليَّةَ ، وبالجملة : فوحدانية الذاتِ تنفي التعدُّد في حقيقتها ، متصلاً كان أو منفصلاً ووحدانية الصفاتِ : تنفي التعدُّدَ في حقيقة كل واحدة منها متصلاً كان أيضاً أو منفصلاً ؛ فعلمُ مولانا جل وعزَّ ليسَ لهُ ثانٍ يماثله ، لا متصلاً ؛ أي : قائماً بالذاتِ العليَّة ، ولا منفصلاً ؛ أي : قائماً بذات أخرى ، بل هو تعالى يعلمُ المعلومات التي لا نهاية لها بعلم واحدٍ لا عددَ له ولا ثاني له أصلاً ، وقس على هذا سائر صفاتِ مولانا جل وعز . ووحدانية الأفعالِ : تنفي أنْ يكونَ ثَمَّ اختراع لكل ما سواه جلَّ وعزَّ في فعلٍ مِنَ الأفعال ، بل جميع الكائنات الحادثة قد عمَّها العجزُ الضروري الدائم عن إيجاد أثر ما ، ومولانا جل وعزَّ هو المنفرد باختراعها وحدَهُ بلا واسطة ، وما يُنسَبُ منها إلى غيرِهِ جَلَّ وعزَّ على وجه يظهرُ منه التأثيرُ . . فهو مؤوّل ، وبالله تعالى التوفيق .
And likewise it is impossible for Him, exalted, not to be one, such that He be composed in His essence, or that He have a peer in His essence or His attributes, or that there be with Him in existence any effective agent in any action. You have known that the aspects of oneness are three: Oneness of Essence, Oneness of Attributes, and Oneness of Actions, and all of them are necessary for our Lord, majestic and exalted, alone. So Oneness of Essence negates composition in His essence, exalted, and the existence of another essence similar to the Supreme Essence. In summary: Oneness of Essence negates multiplicity in its reality, whether connected or separate. And Oneness of Attributes negates multiplicity in the reality of each one of them, also whether connected or separate. So the knowledge of our Lord, majestic and exalted, has no second similar to it, neither connected, meaning subsisting in the Supreme Essence, nor separate, meaning subsisting in another essence. Rather, He, exalted, knows the infinite knowable things with a single knowledge that has no number and no second to it at all. And apply this analogically to the rest of the attributes of our Lord, majestic and exalted. And Oneness of Actions negates that there be any invention for everything other than Him, majestic and exalted, in any action. Rather, all contingent beings have been encompassed by necessary and permanent inability to produce any effect whatsoever, and our Lord, majestic and exalted, is unique in inventing them alone without any intermediary. And what is attributed to other than Him, majestic and exalted, in a manner that appears to show effect... it is metaphorically interpreted. And with Allah, exalted, is success.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of the three aspects of tawhid (oneness) that are necessary attributes of Allah. The author begins by restating the impossibility that Allah not be one, specifying three ways that false oneness could be understood: composition in essence, having a peer in essence or attributes, or having a partner in actions. Then he announces that the aspects of oneness are three: Oneness of Essence, Oneness of Attributes, and Oneness of Actions, and all are obligatory for Allah alone. Next, he explains each in order. First, Oneness of Essence: it negates composition in Allah's essence and the existence of any other essence similar to His; it negates multiplicity whether connected (i.e., internal parts) or separate (i.e., another independent essence). Second, Oneness of Attributes: it negates multiplicity in the reality of each attribute, also whether connected or separate; meaning there is no second identical attribute, whether it subsists in Allah or in another being. As an example, Allah's knowledge has no second—neither another knowledge subsisting in Allah nor a separate knowledge in another being; His knowledge is single and infinite. All His attributes are analogous. Third, Oneness of Actions: it negates that any other being has any share in inventing (creating) any action; rather, all contingent beings are inherently and permanently incapable of producing any effect; Allah alone creates everything without intermediary. Actions attributed to others are metaphorically interpreted (as causal statements), not implying real independent effect. The passage ends with a prayer for divine assistance.

وحدانية الصفات
وحدانية الأفعال
تركيب
تماثل
تعدد
اختراع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Tawhid only means there is one God and no other gods.
Correction: Tawhid also requires negating composition in essence, peer in attributes, and partner in actions.

Checkpoint
Which aspect of tawhid negates composition in Allah's essence?
A) Oneness of Essence (وحدانية الذات)
B) Oneness of Attributes (وحدانية الصفات)
C) Oneness of Actions (وحدانية الأفعال)
D) All three

Key Takeaway
Tawhid encompasses three aspects: oneness of essence, attributes, and actions, each negating crucial false notions about Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you succeed or fail, do you truly feel in your heart that Allah alone is the creator of that outcome, or do you secretly believe your own effort is the real cause? What would change if you lived with full conviction of Oneness of Actions?
?
You confess that Allah's knowledge is one and infinite. Yet when you pray for something, do you act as if your request informs Him of something new? How can your dua reflect trust in His all-encompassing, singular knowledge?
?
Oneness of Essence requires that Allah has no peer—no one like Him in any way. In your moments of deepest fear or need, do you turn to anything or anyone as if they could stand in His place? What does your heart’s reliance reveal about your tawhid?

The Impossibility of Incapacity for God
Sanusi proves that incapacity for any possible thing is impossible for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why incapacity (al-ʿajz) for any possible thing is impossible for God, based on the necessary universality of His power.

Core Idea
Incapacity for any possible thing would negate God's universal power, which is impossible.

This passage from Sanusi's creed tackles a key divine attribute: God's power (qudra) is absolute and universal. Using a rational proof, Sanusi shows that any form of incapacity—an inability to do something possible—is logically impossible for God. Let's trace his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Let the author's own words and structure sink in before analyzing.
Notice that the passage begins by restating the thesis: incapacity for any possible thing is impossible.
2
Identify the two key premises
The proof builds on two premises: (1) God's power is one and universally related to all possible things, (2) If power were specific, it would need a specificator, making it contingent. Understand each premise.
3
Trace the logical consequence
Incapacity for some possible thing would negate the necessary universality of power. Furthermore, incapacity and power are opposites that cannot coexist, so even power itself would be negated.
This shows the rational impossibility of incapacity.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Consider the common misunderstanding that God's inability to do certain things (e.g., injustice) is a form of deficiency. Understand why it does not apply: incapacity is inability, not voluntary refraining.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
استحالة العجز عن ممكن ما
وَكَذَا يَسْتَحِيلُ عَلَيْهِ تَعَالَى الْعَجْزُ عَنْ مُمْكِن مَا .
قد عرفتَ أنَّ قدرة الله تعالى واحدة ، عامة التعلُّق بجميعِ الممكناتِ ؛ إذْ لو اختصَّتْ ببعضها دونَ بعض لافتقرت إلى مخصص ، فتكون حادثةً ، وهو محال ، فلو اتّصف تعالى بالعجز عن ممكن ما لانتفى العموم الواجب للقدرة ، بل ويلزمُ عليهِ نفي القدرةِ أصلاً ؛ لاستحالة اجتماع الضدين .
The impossibility of incapacity for any possible thing. And likewise, it is impossible for Him, exalted, to be incapable of any possible thing. You have already known that the power of God, exalted, is one, universally related to all possible things; for if it were specific to some to the exclusion of others, it would need a specificator, and thus would be contingent, which is impossible. So if He, exalted, were described with incapacity for some possible thing, then the universality necessary for power would be negated; rather, the negation of power altogether would follow from it, due to the impossibility of the coexistence of the two opposites.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage is a proof that incapacity (al-ʿajz) for any possible thing is impossible for God. It begins with a heading stating the thesis: the impossibility of incapacity for any possible thing. Then it states the thesis again: it is impossible for God to be incapable of any possible thing. The proof builds on the established attribute of God's power (al-qudra): His power is one and universally related to all possible things. The universality is necessary; if power were limited to some possibilities and not others, it would require a specificator (mukhaṣṣiṣ), which would make it contingent (ḥāditha) and thus impossible for God. Therefore, if God were attributed with incapacity for some possible thing, then the necessary universality of His power would be negated. Moreover, incapacity and power are opposites (ḍiddān) that cannot coexist, so attributing incapacity would even entail the negation of power altogether. The proof thus shows the rational impossibility of incapacity in God based on the nature of divine power as universal. Key technical terms: عجز (incapacity), قدرة (power), ممكن (possible), عموم (universality), محال (impossible), ضدّان (two opposites).

ممكن
عموم
محال
ضدان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's inability to do certain things (like injustice) is a form of incapacity or deficiency.
Correction: Incapacity (ʿajz) refers to an actual inability to perform an act, not voluntary refraining. God's inability to do injustice is a necessary consequence of His perfection, not a lack.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why is it impossible for God to be incapable of any possible thing?
A) Because incapacity is a deficiency and God is perfect.
B) Because God's power is one and universally related to all possible things.
C) Because God's power is limited to only some possible things.
D) Because incapacity would make God similar to creatures.

Key Takeaway
Incapacity for any possible thing is impossible for God because it would negate the necessary universality of His power and the coexistence of opposites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that God is incapable of incapacity, then why do you sometimes act as if He is too busy or too distant to help you? What specific area of your life do you hold back from trusting Him fully, and what would it look like to entrust Him with that today?
?
When you face a situation that seems impossible to you, do you ever subconsciously doubt that God's power covers that particular possibility? Reflect on a time you mentally limited God. What step can you take right now to replace that doubt with conviction in His universal power?
?
This proof shows that God's incapacity for any possible thing is impossible. How does this truth confront your tendency to rely on your own strength or to despair? What practical action can you take today to act on the certainty that His power extends to every possibility?

The Impossibility of Incapability in God
Sanusi proves the impossibility of incapability in God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God cannot be incapable of any possible thing, based on the necessary universality of His power.

Core Idea
Divine power is universal; incapability would completely negate it.

Sanusi builds on the prior understanding of God's power as one and universally related to all possible things. He now proves that attributing incapability to God would destroy the very nature of divine power, as incapability and power are opposites that cannot coexist.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the concise proof structure: Sanusi first states the ruling, then gives the reasoning.
The passage builds on earlier discussions about the universality of divine power.
2
Identify the key premise
Find the premise that God's power is 'one and universally related to all possible things.' Understand why this is necessary: if power were specific, it would need a specificr and thus be originated.
This premise is central to the proof.
3
Follow the proof by contradiction
Trace the argument: if God were incapable of some possible thing, then power would not be universal. Sanusi then argues that this would even negate power entirely, because incapability and power are opposing attributes.
The proof shows that incapability is not just a limitation but a direct contradiction to divine power.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand that the impossibility of incapability follows necessarily from the nature of divine power. This reinforces the attribute of power as perfect and all-encompassing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
استحالة العجز عن ممكن ما
وَكَذَا يَسْتَحِيلُ عَلَيْهِ تَعَالَى الْعَجْزُ عَنْ مُمْكِن مَا . قد عرفتَ أنَّ قدرة الله تعالى واحدة ، عامة التعلُّق بجميعِ الممكناتِ ؛ إذْ لو اختصَّتْ ببعضها دونَ بعض لافتقرت إلى مخصص ، فتكون حادثةً ، وهو محال ، فلو اتّصف تعالى بالعجز عن ممكن ما لانتفى العموم الواجب للقدرة ، بل ويلزمُ عليهِ نفي القدرةِ أصلاً ؛ لاستحالة اجتماع الضدين .
The impossibility of incapability regarding any possible thing. And likewise it is impossible for Him, exalted, to be incapable of any possible thing. You have known that the power of God, exalted, is one, universally related to all possible things; because if it were specific to some without others, it would need a specificr, and thus be originated, which is impossible. So if the Exalted were described with incapability for any possible thing, the necessary universality of power would be negated, rather it would necessitate the negation of power entirely, due to the impossibility of the two opposites coexisting.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage is a proof by contradiction. Sanusi first states the ruling: it is impossible for God to be incapable of any possible thing. He then recalls a prior principle: God's power is one and universally related to all possible things. The proof: if power were specific to some possibles, it would need a specificr, making it contingent and originated—impossible for God. Therefore power must be universal. If God were incapable of some possible, that would negate the required universality, and even negate power entirely because incapability and power are opposites that cannot coexist.

Misconception: Incapability is a mere limitation that does not negate power.
Correction: Sanusi shows that incapability negates the universality essential to divine power, and because power and incapability are opposites, incapability would negate power entirely.

Key Takeaway

Nothing Occurs Without God's Will
Sanusi proves the necessity of God's will for all occurrences and refutes alternative models.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why nothing in the universe can occur without God's will, and how this refutes philosophical views of necessary causation.

Core Idea
Without God's will, nothing exists.

Sanusi now moves to a crucial attribute: divine will. He argues that God's will is universal and must accompany every event. He then demolishes alternative explanations—forgetfulness, heedlessness, causation, or nature—and warns that rejecting this leads to outright disbelief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the translation. Notice how Sanusi builds his argument step by step.
The heading 'The Impossibility of the Absence of Will' sets the topic.
2
Understand the definition of will
Sanusi defines will as 'intention to specify the possible'. Grasp this definition, as it is the foundation for the proof.
Will is not just desire; it is a purposeful specification.
3
Identify the four refuted alternatives
Find the four things Sanusi says are negated by the universality of God's will: opposite will, forgetfulness, heedlessness, causation, and nature.
Note that 'forgetfulness' and 'heedlessness' are negated because they contradict intention.
4
Trace the refutation of causation
Sanusi explains that if God were a 'cause' in the philosophical sense, the world would be pre-eternal, which contradicts the need for will.
This is a key argument against the philosophers.
5
Conclude with the danger of denial
Sanusi states that the philosophers who deny God's will end up in kufr. Reflect on why this is so serious.
The lesson shows the high stakes of this theological point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
استحالة عدم الإرادة
وَإِيجَادُ شَيْءٍ مِنَ الْعَالَم مَعَ كَرَاهَتِهِ لِوُجُودِهِ ؛ أَيْ : عَدَم إرَادَتِهِ لَهُ تَعَالَى ، أَوْ مَعَ الدُّهُولِ ، أَوِ الْغَفْلَةِ ، أَوَ بِالتَّعْلِيلِ ، أَوْ بِالطَّبْعِ
ما يجوز عليه
قد علمتَ أنَّ حقيقة الإرادة هي القصد إلى تخصيص الجائز ببعض ، وقد تقرَّرَ أَنَّ إرادته تعالى عامَّةُ التعلقِ بجميع الممكناتِ ، فيلزمُ أنْ يستحيل وقوع شيءٍ منها بغير إرادة منه تعالى ذلك الشيء ، وذلك ينفي إرادته تعالى لضد ذلكَ الواقع ، وإلا لاجتمعَ الضدَّانِ ، وينفي أيضاً اتصافَهُ تعالى بالذهول والغفلة ؛ لأنَّهُما منافيان للقصدِ الذي هو معنى الإرادة ، وينفي أيضاً أن تكونَ الذاتُ العليَّةُ علة لوجودِ شيءٍ مِنَ الممكنات ، أو مؤثرةً فيه بالطبع ؛ لأنَّه يلزمُ عليهِ قدمُ ذلكَ الممكن ؛ لوجوب اقتران العلة بمعلولها والطبيعة بمطبوعها ، وذلك ينافي إرادة ذلك الممكن القديم ؛ لأنَّ القصد إلى إيجاد الموجودِ محال ؛ إذْ هو مِنْ بابِ تحصيل الحاصلِ . ولهذا لما اعتقدَتِ الملحدةُ مِنَ الفلاسفة أَهْلَكَهمُ اللهُ تعالى : أَنَّ استناد العالم إليهِ تعالى إنَّما هو على طريق استناد المعلول إلى العلة . . قالوا بقدم العالم ، ونفَوا لعنهم الله تعالى جميع الصفاتِ الواجبة لمولانا جل وعزَّ ؛ مِنَ القدرةِ والإرادة وغيرهما ، وذلك كفر صراح.
The Impossibility of the Absence of Will
And to bring into existence something from the world while having aversion to its existence, meaning: His not willing it, may He be exalted, or with forgetfulness, or heedlessness, or by causation, or by nature.
What is permissible for Him
You have known that the reality of will is the intention to specify the possible with some aspect, and it has been established that His will, may He be exalted, is universal in relation to all possibles. So it necessarily follows that it is impossible for anything among them to occur without His will, may He be exalted, for that thing. And that negates His will, may He be exalted, for the opposite of that actual thing, otherwise the two opposites would coexist. And it also negates describing Him, may He be exalted, with forgetfulness and heedlessness, because they are opposed to intention, which is the meaning of will. And it also negates that the exalted Essence be a cause for the existence of any of the possibles, or an agent by nature; because that would necessitate the pre-eternity of that possible, due to the necessity of the conjunction of the cause with its effect and of nature with its natural product. And that contradicts the will of that pre-eternal possible, because the intention to bring into existence what already exists is impossible, since it is a case of obtaining the already obtained. And for this reason, when the heretics among the philosophers - may God destroy them - believed that the dependence of the world upon Him, may He be exalted, is only in the manner of the dependence of the effect upon the cause, they asserted the pre-eternity of the world and denied, may God curse them, all the necessary attributes of our Master, may He be glorified and exalted, such as power, will, and others. And that is outright disbelief.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage is a proof-problem combination. It first defines the impossibility of anything occurring without God's will, then refutes alternative models (causation and nature) that would imply pre-eternity of the world, and concludes that such beliefs are kufr. The passage can be broken down as follows:

1. The heading 'The Impossibility of the Absence of Will' sets the topic.
2. The first sentence explains that bringing something into existence while having aversion to it (i.e., not willing it) is impossible. Likewise, producing something through forgetfulness, heedlessness, causation, or nature is impossible.
3. The author then reminds the reader that the reality of will is intention to specify a possible in a particular way. Since God's will encompasses all possibles, nothing can occur without His willing it. This implies that He cannot will the opposite of what occurs, because that would entail two opposites (will and non-will) coexisting.
4. It also negates that God could be characterized by forgetfulness or heedlessness, as these are contrary to the intention that constitutes will.
5. It further negates that God could be a cause in the philosophical sense (i.e., necessary emanation) or an agent by nature (unconscious compulsion), because if the world were caused necessarily, it would be pre-eternal (since cause and effect are simultaneous). This would contradict the will of a pre-eternal thing, because willing what already exists is impossible (it would be 'obtaining the obtained').
6. The author then applies this to the philosophers: they claimed the world emanates from God as an effect from a cause, leading them to assert the world's pre-eternity. They thereby denied God's attributes like power and will, which is outright disbelief (kufr).

Key Terms
إرادة
قصد
جائز
علة
طبع
ذهول
غفلة
تحصيل الحاصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's will may not be involved in some events, especially those that appear random or evil.
Correction: The passage establishes that God's will is universal: nothing occurs without it, regardless of human perception.

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi argue that the world could not be pre-eternal if it were caused by God in the manner of a philosophical cause?
A) Because a cause requires a temporal delay before its effect appears.
B) Because a cause necessitates the simultaneous existence of its effect, implying the effect is eternal if the cause is eternal.
C) Because God's will is irrelevant to causation.
D) Because the world is contingent and must have a beginning.

Key Takeaway
God's will is universal and necessary for every occurrence; any model that denies this leads to disbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficult situation, do you truly believe that it occurs only by God's will, or is there a part of you that thinks it happened by chance or by your own planning? How does this passage challenge that assumption, and what is one step you will take today to internalize that nothing is outside His will?
?
Reflect on a time you attributed an outcome to your own effort or to natural causes, forgetting the role of God's will. What would it mean for you to consistently affirm God's will in every event, even the ones you dislike? Write down one specific event from this week and re-frame it as occurring solely by God's will.
?
The passage warns that denying God's universal will leads to disbelief. Examine your own heart: in what subtle ways do you sometimes act as if things happen without God's will? What is one concrete change you will make today to align your belief with this truth, and how will you remind yourself of it regularly?

Proof That God Acts by Pure Choice
Sanusi proves that God cannot act by causal determination or nature, so He acts by choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi argues that God cannot act by necessary causation (ta'lil or tab') and must act by pure choice (ikhtiyar).

Core Idea
If God's action were necessary, the world would be eternal, but it is not.

Sanusi presents a logical proof that God's agency is by pure choice, not by causal determination or nature. The argument hinges on the impossibility of an eternal act from God given the temporal nature of the universe. Let's walk through this tightly reasoned proof step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the flow of the argument.
Notice the technical terms: ta'lil, tab', ikhtiyar.
2
Identify the two alternatives
Sanusi considers two ways God could act: by causal determination (ta'lil) or by nature (tab'). Both are forms of necessary causation.
3
See why each leads to an eternal act
For ta'lil, it's obvious: effect is eternal with cause. For tab', he argues that no obstacle can prevent the act, else act never occurs; and condition delay leads to infinite regress.
This shows that necessary causation implies a co-eternal act.
4
Connect to temporality of creation
Sanusi states it is proven that everything other than God is temporally originated (hadith). So God's act cannot be eternal, thus cannot be by ta'lil or tab'.
5
Conclude and reflect
Therefore, God acts by pure choice. This refutes the philosophers and naturalists. Ask yourself how this proof strengthens the Islamic understanding of divine freedom.
The conclusion is a major doctrinal point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما البارى جلَّ وعزَّ فلو كانَ فعلهُ بالتعليل أو بالطبع لزم قدم الفعل فيهما معاً ، واقترانُ الفعل حينئذٍ بوجودِهِ تعالى ؛ أما على التعليل فظاهر ، وأما على طريق الطبع فلا يصحُ أنْ يكونَ ثَمَّ مانع ؛ وإلا لزمَ ألا يوجد الفعلُ أبداً ؛ لأنَّ ذلكَ المانع لا يكون إلا قديماً ، والقديمُ لا ينعدم أبداً ، ولا يصح تأخُرُ الشرطِ ؛ لما يلزمُ عليهِ مِنَ التسلسل. فلهذا قلنا فيما سبق : إِنَّهُ يلزمُ على طريق التعليلِ أوِ الطبع في حقّهِ تعالى قدم المعلولِ أو المطبوع. وقد قام البرهان على وجوب الحدوثِ لكلِّ ما سواه تعالى ، فتعيَّنَ أنَّهُ سبحانَهُ فاعل بمحض الاختيار، وبطل مذهب الفلاسفة والطبائعيينَ أذلَّ الله تعالى جميعهم ، وأخلى منهم الأرض.
As for the Creator, Exalted and Glorified, if His action were by causal determination (al-taʿlīl) or by nature (al-ṭabʿ), then the eternity of the act would necessarily follow in both cases, and the conjunction of the act would then be with His existence, Exalted. As for causal determination, it is obvious. As for the way of nature, it is not correct that there could be an obstacle; otherwise it would necessarily follow that the act would never exist, because that obstacle could only be eternal, and the eternal never ceases to exist, and it is not valid for the condition to be delayed due to what would follow of infinite regress (al-tasalsul). Therefore we said previously: That by the way of causal determination or nature in His right, Exalted, the eternity of the effect or the nature-impressed follows. And the proof has been established for the necessity of temporal origination (al-ḥudūth) for everything other than Him, Exalted. So it is established that He, Glorified, is an agent by pure choice (fāʿil bi-muḥḍ al-ikhtiyār). And the doctrine of the philosophers and the naturalists is refuted—may Allah debase them all and empty the earth of them.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage is a proof that God is an agent by choice, not by causal determination (taʿlīl) or nature (ṭabʿ). The author first states the hypothesis: if God's action were by taʿlīl or ṭabʿ, then the act would be eternal. He explains: for taʿlīl, it is obvious because the effect is coeternal with the cause. For ṭabʿ, he argues that there cannot be an obstacle (māniʿ) because any obstacle would be eternal (since it is not created by God) and would never cease, so the act would never occur. Similarly, the condition (sharṭ) cannot be delayed because that would lead to infinite regress (tasalsul). He then concludes that the act would be eternal. But since it is proven that everything other than God is temporally originated (ḥādith), God cannot be an agent by taʿlīl or ṭabʿ. Therefore, He must be an agent by pure choice (ikhtiyār). This refutes the philosophers (falāsifa) and naturalists (ṭabāʿiyyūn).

Key Terms
التعليل
الطبع
الاختيار
التسلسل
المانع
الشرط
الحدوث
الفاعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's actions can be explained by secondary causes or natural necessity.
Correction: Sanusi shows that any necessary causation in God leads to the act being eternal, contradicting the temporal origination of the world. Therefore, God acts only by pure choice, not by necessity.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what would necessarily follow if God's action were by causal determination or nature?
A) The act would be temporally originated.
B) The act would be eternal.
C) The act would be contingent.
D) The act would be impossible.

Key Takeaway
God's agency is by pure choice, not by necessary causation, because necessary causation would make the world eternal, contradicting its temporal origination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's actions are purely by choice, why do you often act as if your own choices are forced by your circumstances, desires, or habits? What would it mean for you to truly believe that your Lord is completely free, and how should that shape your reliance on Him alone?
?
The world is temporally originated—it will not last forever. Does your daily life reflect this truth, or do you live as if this world is eternal? What specific attachment must you cut today to align your heart with the reality that only God is eternal?
?
Sanusi refutes those who explain God's actions by nature or causal necessity. Are there areas in your faith where you secretly think God 'must' do something (like forgive you without repentance, or grant you something without asking)? How can you replace that sense of entitlement with the awe of a God who acts by pure, unconstrained choice?

The Two Meanings of Karāha (Abhorrence)
Sanusi distinguishes legal karāha from karāha as 'not willing'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will distinguish between legal karāha and karāha as 'not willing' and understand why Sanusi restricts the term in the creed.

Core Idea
Divine karāha in creed means 'not willing' – not a mere prohibition.

Sanusi clarifies a subtle but critical distinction. The term 'karāha' (abhorrence) can be misunderstood. In this passage, he explains why he interprets Allah's karāha as 'not willing' rather than as a legal prohibition that can coexist with creation. This distinction is key to preserving divine omnipotence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Notice how Sanusi contrasts two meanings of karāha.
Pay attention to the phrase 'to guard thereby against' – it signals his purpose.
2
Identify the two meanings
Find where Sanusi defines legal karāha and where he defines karāha as 'not willing'. Note how they differ regarding coexistence with bringing into existence.
Legal karāha is a non-binding demand to refrain; karāha as not willing is absolute absence of will.
3
Examine the examples
Sanusi gives the example of Allah leading people astray while prohibiting them. This illustrates legal karāha coexisting with the act. Contrast it with the impossibility of an act occurring if Allah does not will it.
4
Reflect on the subtlety
Sanusi calls this an 'amazing subtlety'. Ask yourself why this distinction matters for the creed. How does it protect Allah's omnipotence?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإِنَّما فسَّرنا الكراهة بعدم الإرادة ; لنحترز بذلكَ مِنَ الكراهة التي هي مِنْ أقسام الحكم الشرعي ; وهي طلب الكفّ عن الفعل طلباً غير جازم يصح أن تجتمع مع الإيجاد فيوجد الله تعالى الفعل مع كراهته له ; أي نهيه عنه ; كما أضل الله تعالى كثيراً من الخلق مع نهيه لهم عن ذلك الضلال . أما الكراهة بمعنى عدم إرادة الله تعالى للفعل فيستحيل اجتماعها مع الإيجاد ; إذ يستحيل أن يقع في ملك مولانا جل وعز ما لا يريد وقوعه , فتنبه لهذه النكتة العجيبة في هذا التقييد الذي قيدنا به الكراهة في أصل العقيدة , وبالله تعالى التوفيق
We have only interpreted karāha (abhorrence) as 'not willing' in order to guard thereby against the karāha that is one of the divisions of legal ruling (ḥukm sharʿī), which is the demand to refrain from an action – a demand that is not decisive – and it can coexist with [the act of] bringing into existence; so Allah brings the act into existence while having karāha of it, i.e., His prohibition of it. Just as Allah led many of the creation astray while concurrently forbidding them that misguidance. As for karāha in the sense of Allah not willing the act, it is impossible for it to coexist with bringing into existence, because it is impossible that there should occur in the dominion of our Master, glorified and exalted, what He does not will to occur. So pay attention to this amazing subtlety in this restriction with which we have restricted karāha in the original creed. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage clarifies a potential ambiguity in the term 'karāha' (abhorrence/dislike) as used in the creed. Sanusi states that the karāha predicated of Allah in the creed is interpreted as 'not willing' (ʿadam al-irāda). This interpretation is chosen to avoid confusion with another meaning of karāha, namely the legal ruling of 'disliked' (makrūh) in Islamic law. Legal karāha is a non-binding demand to refrain; it can coexist with the act being brought into existence. For example, Allah may decree that someone goes astray while simultaneously prohibiting that person from going astray – the prohibition does not prevent the decree. However, karāha in the sense of 'not willing' cannot coexist with bringing into existence, because whatever Allah does not will cannot occur in His dominion. Sanusi calls this distinction an 'amazing subtlety' and urges careful attention to the restriction placed on the term in the original creed.

Key Terms
الكراهة
عدم الإرادة
الحكم الشرعي
الإيجاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That any divine karāha implies opposition to the act's occurrence, so if Allah 'abhors' an act, He cannot bring it into existence.
Correction: Sanusi distinguishes legal karāha (a prohibition that can coexist with creation) from karāha as 'not willing' (which forbids coexistence). Only the latter meaning is used in the creed.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the meaning of karāha in the original creed?
A) Prohibition
B) Not willing
C) Dislike that coexists with creation
D) Demand to refrain

Key Takeaway
Sanusi interprets divine karāha as 'not willing' to avoid implying that Allah creates what He actively dislikes in the sense of legal prohibition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear that Allah 'dislikes' something, do you instinctively think it will never happen in your life – or that it might happen as a test? How does this lesson change your understanding of why Allah decrees things He forbids?
?
Is there an area in your life where you assume that because Allah prohibited something, He will never let it be decreed for you – perhaps making you heedless of His ultimate will? How can you hold both His prohibition and His decree in your heart without contradiction?
?
If you truly internalize that nothing occurs except what Allah wills, how would that reshape your response to trials that seem to 'contradict' His commands? What would you need to surrender to feel at peace with that truth?

Interpretation of Karāhah as Absence of Will
Sanusi defines karāhah in the creed and distinguishes it from the legal term.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between karāhah as a legal ruling and karāhah as absence of divine will, and why the latter is used in creed.

Core Idea
Karāhah in Allah is not disapproval but the absence of will.

This passage is a crucial clarification about a term that can be misunderstood. When we say Allah dislikes evil, we must not confuse it with the legal term that implies a demand to refrain that can coexist with the act. Sanusi explains that in creed, karāhah means Allah does not will the act.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the distinction Sanusi is making between two meanings of karāhah.
Pay attention to the warning at the beginning.
2
Identify the two meanings
Notice the legal karāhah (a demand to refrain) and the creedal karāhah (absence of will). Look for how each is defined.
3
Examine the example
The example of God leading people astray despite His prohibition shows that legal karāhah can coexist with the act occurring.
This is key to understanding the distinction.
4
Understand the impossibility
Contrast that with creedal karāhah: absence of will cannot coexist with bringing about the act, because it is impossible for something Allah does not will to occur in His dominion.
5
Appreciate the subtlety
Sanusi calls this a wonderful subtlety. Reflect on why this distinction protects against misattributing evil to Allah.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا تغتر بظواهر العباراتِ فتهلِكَ معَ الهالكينَ وإِنَّما فسَّرنا الكراهة بعدم الإرادة ؛ لنحترز بذلكَ مِنَ الكراهة التي هي مِنْ أقسام الحكم الشرعي ؛ وهي طلب الكفّ عن الفعل طلباً غيرَ يصح أن تجتمعَ معَ الإيجادِ ، فيوجدَ الله تعالى الفعلَ معَ كراهتِهِ لهُ؛ أي: نهيهِ عنهُ؛ كما أضلَّ اللهُ تعالى كثيراً مِنَ الخلقِ مَعَ نهيه لهم عن ذلك الضلال • أما الكراهة بمعنى عدم إرادة الله تعالى للفعل فيستحيل اجتماعها معَ الإيجادِ؛ إذْ يستحيلُ أنْ يقع في ملكِ مولانا جل وعزَّ ما لا يريدُ وقوعَهُ، فتنبَّه لهذهِ النكتة العجيبة في هذا التقييد الذي قيَّدنا بهِ الكراهة في أصل العقيدة، وبالله تعالى التوفيق
Do not be deceived by the outward meanings of expressions lest you perish among the perishing. We have only interpreted 'karāhah' as absence of will in order to avoid thereby the karāhah that is one of the categories of legal ruling, which is the demand to refrain from an act in a non-firm demand that can coexist with bringing about [the act], such that God Most High brings about the act along with His karāhah of it, i.e., His prohibition of it, just as God Most High led many of creation astray despite His prohibition to them of that misguidance. As for karāhah in the sense of God Most High not willing the act, it is impossible for it to coexist with bringing it about, because it is impossible that something He does not will to occur should occur in the dominion of our Master, majestic and mighty is He. So pay attention to this wonderful subtlety in this restriction with which we have restricted karāhah in the original creed. And with God Most High is success.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is a definition and distinction. The author explains that the term 'karāhah' (aversion) when attributed to God in the creed does not mean the legal category of 'disliked' (makrūh), which is a demand to refrain that can coexist with the act occurring (like God prohibiting something yet it happens). Rather, it means that God does not will the act. The author warns against being deceived by outward expressions. He then gives an example: God led many astray despite His prohibition, meaning His legal prohibition does not prevent His willing the act. In the creed, we say God does not will evil; we interpret karāhah as 'adam al-irādah (absence of will). The passage clarifies this theological nuance.

Key Terms
كراهة
عدم الإرادة
الحكم الشرعي
الإيجاد
النهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God's 'dislike' of sin means He does not will it, but the legal 'dislike' can coexist with His will.
Correction: In creed, karāhah means absence of will, which cannot coexist with the act; legal karāhah is prohibition which can coexist.

Checkpoint
What is the key difference between the two meanings of karāhah discussed?
A) One is legal and the other is moral.
B) One can coexist with the act occurring, the other cannot.
C) One applies to humans only, the other to God.
D) One is a command, the other is a prohibition.

Key Takeaway
In creed, karāhah means Allah does not will the act, not that He disapproves in a way that could coexist with the act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a situation where God's command (e.g., prohibition) seems to conflict with His decree (e.g., the event happening), does it shake your trust in His wisdom? How does this lesson's distinction between legal and creedal karāhah help you submit to His will without confusion?
?
Are there any 'outward expressions' of Islamic terms that you have taken at face value without seeking deeper theological understanding? What might be the consequence of such neglect, and how will you guard against it starting today?
?
This lesson warns against perishing with the perishing due to deception by expressions. Examine your heart: is there a specific belief or doubt you hold about Allah's actions that stems from misunderstanding His attributes? What step can you take now to align your creed with the truth taught here?

Why Divine Speech Cannot Be Letters and Sounds
Sanusi refutes the claim that divine speech is composed of letters and sounds, showing it entails two vices: non-existence and dumbness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi argues that describing God's speech as letters and sounds would imply temporality and deficiency, contradicting divine perfection.

Core Idea
Letters and sounds entail temporality and dumbness — impossible for the Eternal All-Knowing.

Sanusi continues his exposition of divine speech by refuting a common but flawed understanding. Some might think that because human speech consists of letters and sounds, God's speech must be similar. However, Sanusi shows that such a view leads to absurd consequences — it would make God temporal and deficient. In this passage, he identifies two specific vices that arise from this claim, and explains why they are incompatible with divinity.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of dumbness
Sanusi defines 'dumbness' (albakam) as the absence of speech due to a defect, and then equates it with silence and with speech consisting of letters and sounds.
Notice that he expands the definition to include speech with letters and sounds.
2
Identify the first vice: non-existence
Letters and sounds are preceded and followed by non-existence, which implies temporality in the one described. Ask yourself why temporality is a deficiency.
3
Identify the second vice: dumbness itself
Because letters cannot occur simultaneously, a speaker using them cannot indicate multiple pieces of knowledge at once. This is a form of dumbness.
Sanusi calls this 'stammer' (hubsa).
4
Apply the argument to God
If God's speech were letters and sounds, He would suffer from both temporality and stammer. This is impossible for the All-Powerful.
5
Reflect on the analogy
Sanusi ends with a striking analogy: it is like thinking the braying of donkeys is perfection for them.
This underlines the absurdity of the claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمراد بالبكم : عدم الكلام أصلا بوجود آفة تمنع من وجوده، وفي معناه السكوت، وفي معناه كونه بالحرف والصوت؛ إذ الكلام الذي يكون بالحروف والأصوات ولو بلغ غاية البلاغة والفصاحة، وكان كمالا بالنسبة إلى الحوادث الناقصة.. فهو بالنسبة إلى مقام الألوهية الأعلى نقيصة عظيمة؛ إذ فيه رذيلتان: إحداهما: رذيلة العدم الذي يجب للحروف والأصوات سابقا ولاحقا، ويستلزم حدوث من اتصف به، وأي نقيصة أعظم من نقيصة الحدوث الملازمة ربقة الافتقار على الدوام؟! الثانية: رذيلة البكم الذي هو لازم للحروف والأصوات؛ لأنه لما استحال اجتماع حرفين في آن واحد، فضلا عن الكلمتين، فضلا عن الكلامين.. تبكم المتكلم بالحرف والصوت واحتبس عن أن يدل على معلومات له في آن واحد بصفة الكلام المركب من الحروف والأصوات، فلو كان كلام مولانا جل وعز بالحرف والصوت لزم زيادة رذيلة على زيادة الحدوث؛ وهي اتصافه تعالى عن ذلك بالحبسة التي هي أصل البكم عن الدلالة على معلوماته التي لا نهاية لها بصفة الكلام، بل يلزم الحبسة عن الدلالة به في آن واحد على معلومين له فأكثر فقد ظهر لك بهذا: أن الكلام الذي يكون بالحروف والأصوات وما في معناه من كلامنا النفسي ملازم لمعنى البكم، فيستحيل اتصاف مولانا جل وعز بمثلهما، وأن الواصف لمولانا جل وعز بذلك، مستندا إلى أن مثل ذلك حقنا كمال ينفي عنا رذيلة البكم.. قد وصفه تعالى بنقيصة عظيمة تعالى عنها علوا كبيرا. ونظيره في ذلك: نظير من عرف أن نهيق الحمير وأصواتها كمال
And what is meant by 'dumbness' (albakam) is: the absence of speech originally due to the existence of a defect that prevents its existence. And in its meaning is silence, and in its meaning is his being with the letter and sound. For speech that is composed of letters and sounds, even if it reaches the utmost eloquence and fluency, and is perfection relative to deficient temporal beings, it is, relative to the supreme station of divinity, a great deficiency. For in it are two vices: one of them is the vice of non-existence (al-'adam) which is necessarily prior and posterior for letters and sounds, and it entails the temporality (huduth) of the one described by it. And what deficiency is greater than the deficiency of temporality which is perpetually bound to the rope of neediness?! The second is the vice of dumbness (albakam) which is necessary for letters and sounds. For when it is impossible for two letters to combine in one moment, let alone two words, let alone two speeches, the speaker with the letter and sound becomes dumb and prevented from indicating his pieces of knowledge in one moment by the attribute of speech composed of letters and sounds. So if the speech of our Master (mawla), Glorious and Mighty, were with the letter and sound, an increase of vice upon the increase of temporality would be necessary: namely, His description—Exalted is He above that—with 'stammer' (alhubsa), which is the origin of dumbness from indicating His pieces of knowledge which have no end by the attribute of speech. Rather, stammer from indicating by it in one moment two or more things known to Him would be necessary. So it has appeared to you by this: that speech which consists of letters and sounds, and what is in its meaning of our inner speech, is inseparable from the meaning of dumbness. Thus it is impossible for our Master, Glorious and Mighty, to be described by either of them. And that he who describes our Master with that, relying on the argument that such a thing grants us perfection and removes from us the vice of dumbness, has indeed described Him, Exalted is He, with a great deficiency—far exalted is He above that, a great exaltation. And the likeness of that in this matter is the likeness of one who knows that the braying of donkeys and their sounds are perfection.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that God's speech consists of letters and sounds. The author begins by defining 'dumbness' (albakam) as the absence of speech due to a defect, and then states that the term also applies to silence and to speech that is composed of letters and sounds. He argues that speech composed of letters and sounds, however eloquent, is perfect only relative to deficient temporal beings; relative to the divine station, it is a great deficiency because it contains two vices. The first vice is that of non-existence (al-'adam): letters and sounds are preceded and followed by non-existence, which entails that the one described by them is temporal (hadith). This is the greatest deficiency because temporality implies perpetual neediness. The second vice is dumbness (albakam) itself, which is necessarily attached to letters and sounds. The author explains that because it is impossible for two letters to occur simultaneously, let alone two words or two speeches, the speaker who uses letters and sounds is 'dumb' in the sense of being unable to indicate multiple pieces of knowledge at once. If God's speech were of this type, He would suffer from 'stammer' (alhubsa), which is the root of dumbness, because He could not indicate His infinite knowledge at once. Even indicating two things at once would be impossible. The author concludes that speech composed of letters and sounds, along with inner speech that shares the same characteristics, is inseparable from dumbness, so it is impossible for God to be described with either. Those who describe God with such speech, claiming it is a perfection for us, have actually attributed a great deficiency to God. The author ends with an analogy: it is like someone who thinks the braying of donkeys is perfection for them.

Key Terms
البكم (albakam)
الحبسة (alhubsa)
رذيلة (badilah)
العدم (al-'adam)
الحدوث (al-huduth)
الكلام النفسي (inner speech)
الحروف والأصوات (letters and sounds)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that because humans speak with letters and sounds, God's speech must also be composed of letters and sounds for it to be real speech.
Correction: Sanusi shows that such a view actually implies temporality and defect in God, and that divine speech is an eternal attribute (inner speech) not composed of letters and sounds.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what are the two vices that arise from ascribing letters and sounds to God's speech?
A) Non-existence and temporality
B) Non-existence and dumbness
C) Temporality and stammer
D) Silence and deficiency

Key Takeaway
Describing God's speech as letters and sounds would imply temporality and deficiency, contradicting His perfection.

?

Letters and Sounds Entail Muteness for God
Sanusi's commentator proves that letters-and-sounds speech entails muteness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why attributing speech composed of letters and sounds to God necessarily implies muteness and is therefore impossible.

Core Idea
Letters and sounds limit God's ability to indicate multiple meanings at once, which is a form of muteness.

The commentator builds on the discussion of temporality by identifying another impossibility: if God's speech consisted of letters and sounds, He would be unable to express infinite objects of knowledge simultaneously. This passage explains why such speech entails a kind of muteness, making it inconceivable for Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Begin by reading the Arabic text from 'زيادة رذيلة' to 'على معلومين له فأكثر'. Let the argument unfold in the author's own words.
Focus on the phrase 'بل يلزم الحبسة عن الدلالة به في آن واحد على معلومين له فأكثر'.
2
Identify the key terms
Note the terms 'الحبسة' (hubsa) and 'البكم' (bkam). Hubsa is the inability to speak, the root of muteness. Understand how they are linked.
3
Trace the entailment
Follow the reasoning: if God's speech uses letters and sounds, each utterance can only indicate one meaning at a time. Since God knows infinite objects, He would be unable to express them simultaneously—this is a form of muteness.
The phrase 'في آن واحد' (in one instant) is key.
4
Connect to impossibility
Conclude that such speech is impossible for God because it implies a deficiency. This is the central point of the lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
زيادة رذيلة على زيادة الحدوثِ ؛ وهي اتصافه تعالى عن ذلكَ بالحُبْسة التي هي أصلُ البَكَم عن الدلالة على معلوماته التي لا نهاية لها بصفة الكلامِ) ، بل يلزمُ الحُبْسةً عنِ ، بل يلزمُ الحُبْسةُ عن الدلالةِ بهِ في آنٍ واحدٍ على معلومين لهُ فأكثرَ
فقد ظهرَ لكَ بهذا : أنَّ الكلام الذي يكون بالحروف والأصوات وما في معناهُ مِنْ كلامنا النفسي ملازم لمعنى البكم ، فيستحيلُ اتصافُ مولانا جل وعزَّ بمثلهما ، وأنَّ الواصف لمولانا جل وعزّ بذلكَ ، مستنداً إلى أنَّ مثلَ ذلكَ حقنا كمال ينفي عنا رذيلة البكم . . قد وصفَهُ تعالى بنقيصة عظيمة تعالى عنها علوّاً كبيراً .
An additional vice upon the vice of temporality: that He, exalted above that, is described with hubsa (inability to speak) which is the root of muteness from indicating His infinite objects of knowledge by the attribute of speech; rather, muteness necessarily follows from indicating by it in one instant two or more objects of knowledge for Him. So it has become clear to you by this: that speech which consists of letters and sounds and what is like that from our internal speech is concomitant with the meaning of muteness, so it is impossible for our Lord, glorified and exalted, to be described with either of them; and that the one who describes our Lord, glorified and exalted, with that, relying on the fact that such a thing is a perfection for us that negates from us the vice of muteness, has indeed described Him with a great deficiency—exalted is He above that by a high exaltation.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage is a proof that attributing speech consisting of letters and sounds to God entails an impossibility. The commentator begins by identifying an additional vice beyond the vice of temporality: the attribute of hubsa (inability to speak), which is the root of bkam (muteness). If God were described with such speech, He would be unable to indicate multiple objects of knowledge simultaneously, which is a form of muteness. The commentator then states the conclusion: speech with letters and sounds necessarily implies muteness, therefore it is impossible for God to be described with it. Moreover, those who claim that such speech is a perfection because it negates muteness in us are actually ascribing a great deficiency to God, because God is infinitely beyond such limitations.

Key Terms
الحبسة (hubsa)
البكم (bkam)
الدلالة (indication)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that speech with letters and sounds is a neutral attribute that could apply to God.
Correction: The passage shows that such speech actually implies muteness (bkam), which is a deficiency, so it cannot apply to God.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does speech consisting of letters and sounds necessarily entail for God?
A) Temporal origination.
B) Muteness (bkam).
C) Perfection of eloquence.
D) Multiplicity of meanings.

Key Takeaway
Speech with letters and sounds is inherently limited and would imply muteness for God, so it cannot be attributed to Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Qur'an as a sequence of Arabic letters and sounds, do you consciously recognize that this is a created expression of Allah's eternal, non-sequential speech? How does this truth reframe your reverence for the words you speak? Today, before reciting, pause for one minute to silently affirm Allah's transcendence over the limitations of letters and sounds.
?
You speak only one thought at a time; have you ever subconsciously projected this limitation onto Allah's speech? If so, you have unknowingly attributed a form of muteness to Him. Will you actively repent of such anthropomorphic imaginings and train your heart to conceive of Allah's attribute of speech as perfect and unbounded? Commit to a weekly reflection on divine attributes free from human comparison.
?
The commentator argues that letters-and-sounds speech entails muteness for God because He cannot indicate multiple meanings at once. If you previously believed the Qur'an's Arabic text is the uncreated speech itself, you were implicitly attributing that muteness to Allah. Are you willing to adopt the correct belief that the Qur'an is created and that Allah's eternal speech is beyond letters and sounds? Make a firm intention to study the Ash'ari position on the createdness of the Qur'an this week.

Human Perfection Does Not Justify Attributing Letters and Sounds to God
Sanusi's commentator refutes the claim that human speech perfection justifies attributing letters and sounds to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the argument that human speech with letters is a perfection does not apply to God, and that attributing such speech to God actually ascribes great deficiency.

Core Idea
What is perfection for us can be deficiency for God because God is infinite and beyond limitation.

Having established that letters-and-sounds speech entails muteness, the commentator now refutes a potential counterargument. Some might claim that since such speech is a perfection for humans (it negates muteness), it should be a perfection for God. The commentator shows that this reasoning backfires, as it implies God needs to overcome a deficiency.

Guided Path
1
Read the conclusion
Read from 'فقد ظهر لك' to the end. The commentator first restates the conclusion that such speech is impossible for God.
Notice the phrase 'ملازم لمعنى البكم' (concomitant with the meaning of muteness).
2
Identify the counterargument
The commentator mentions those who argue that letters-and-sounds speech is a perfection for us because it negates muteness. Understand this reasoning.
3
See the refutation
The commentator points out that applying this to God describes Him with a great deficiency, because it suggests He needs to acquire a perfection that overcomes a lack.
The phrase 'قد وصفه تعالى بنقيصة عظيمة' is key.

The Absurdity of Comparing Divine Speech to Animal Sounds
Sanusi's commentator uses an analogy to expose the absurdity of describing God's speech with animal-like sounds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why comparing God's speech to animal sounds is a category error and a grave denigration.

Core Idea
Comparing God's speech to donkey braying is like comparing a king to barnyard animals — pure insult.

The commentator uses a striking analogy to drive home the absurdity of describing God's speech with letters and sounds. He imagines someone who claims a king's speech is like animal noises, and shows how that is a gross insult. This lesson unpacks that analogy and its theological lesson.

Guided Path
1
Read the analogy and its setup
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Notice how the commentator sets up the analogy: someone acknowledges animal sounds as perfections for animals, then applies the same reasoning to a king's speech.
2
Identify the error in reasoning
The error is a category mistake: what is a perfection for one kind of being (animals) is not necessarily a perfection for a higher kind (human king). The commentator points out that describing a king with animal sounds is a grave insult.
This shows the principle that attributes must be appropriate to the nature of the being.
3
Apply the lesson to divine speech
The analogy then applies to God: if human speech is infinitely above animal sounds, then God's speech is infinitely beyond human speech. Therefore, describing God's speech with letters and sounds is an even greater category error.
This reinforces divine transcendence (tanzih).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونظيره في ذلكَ : نظيرُ مَنْ عرفَ أنَّ نهيقَ الحميرِ وأصواتها كمالٌ في حقها ، وكذا نُباحُ الكلابِ كمالٌ في حقها ، فسُئِلَ عن صفةِ كلامِ ملكٍ مِنَ الملوكِ لم يسمع قطَّ كلامَهُ ، فقال : هو مثلُ نهيقِ الحميرِ ونُباحِ الكلابِ ؛ معتقداً أنَّ ذلكَ الصوتَ منهما لمَّا كانَ كمالاً يمنعُ مِن اتصافهما برذيلةِ البَكَم .. لزمَ أَنَّ اتصافَ الملكِ بمثل ذلكَ كمالٌ في حقه يَنْفِي عنه رذيلةَ البَكَمِ ! ومِنَ المعلومِ ضرورة : أنَّ الواصفَ للملكِ بمثل هذا قد استنقصه غايةَ الاستنقاص ، ووصفه بأقبحِ أنواعِ البكمِ بالنسبةِ إلى نوعِهِ الإنساني ، وإن لم يكن بكماً بالنسبةِ إلى نهيقِ الحميرِ ونباحِ الكلابِ
ولا شكّ أنَّ كلامنا وإِنْ بلغَ الغاية في البلاغة والفصاحة والحُسْنِ بالنسبة إلى كلام الله تعالى .. أدنى بما لا حصر لهُ مِنْ نهيقِ الحميرِ ونباحِ الكلابِ بالنسبة إلى أفصح كلام وأعذبه؛ إذ الحوادث كلها
And an analogy for that is: the analogy of one who knows that the braying of donkeys and their sounds are perfection for them, and likewise the barking of dogs is perfection for them. He was asked about the attribute of the speech of a king among kings who had never heard his speech, so he said: 'It is like the braying of donkeys and the barking of dogs,' believing that since that sound from them was a perfection preventing them from being described with the vice of muteness, then necessarily the king's being described with something like that is a perfection in his right that negates from him the vice of muteness! And it is known necessarily that the one who describes the king with such has indeed denigrated him to the utmost denigration and described him with the ugliest types of muteness relative to his human species, even if he is not mute relative to the braying of donkeys and the barking of dogs. And there is no doubt that our speech, even if it reaches the utmost in eloquence, fluency, and beauty relative to the speech of God Almighty—it is incomparably lower than the braying of donkeys and the barking of dogs relative to the most eloquent and sweetest speech; for all contingent things...
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage presents an analogy to further demonstrate the absurdity of describing God with letters and sounds. The commentator imagines someone who acknowledges that braying and barking are perfections for animals because they prevent muteness in their species. This person is then asked about the speech of a king whose speech has never been heard, and he replies that it is like donkey braying and dog barking. He reasons that if such sounds are perfections for animals, then they must be perfections for the king too. But the commentator points out that anyone who describes a king this way has grossly insulted him, because the king's human nature requires far higher speech. The passage then concludes that even the most eloquent human speech is incomparably inferior to God's speech, more so than animal sounds are to human speech.

Key Terms
نهيق الحمير
نباح الكلاب
الاستنقاص
البكم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Animal sounds can be a valid analogy for divine speech because they are perfections in their own context.
Correction: The passage shows this is a category error; what is perfect for an animal is degrading for a higher being, especially God.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the analogy of the king and the animal sounds?
A) Animal sounds are perfections that should be attributed to kings.

C) God's speech is like animal sounds but more beautiful.
